We were sitting in my room, smoking, and talking about\r\nhow bad we were\xe2\x80\x94bad from a medical point of view I mean, of course.\r\n\r\nWe were all feeling seedy, and we were getting quite nervous about it.\r\nHarris said he felt such extraordinary fits of giddiness come over him\r\nat times, that he hardly knew what he was doing; and then George said\r\nthat _he_ had fits of giddiness too, and hardly knew what _he_ was\r\ndoing. With me, it was my liver that was out of order. I knew it was my\r\nliver that was out of order, because I had just been reading a patent\r\nliver-pill circular, in which were detailed the various symptoms by\r\nwhich a man could tell when his liver was out of order. I had them all.\r\n\r\nIt is a most extraordinary thing, but I never read a patent medicine\r\nadvertisement without being impelled to the conclusion that I am\r\nsuffering from the particular disease therein dealt with in its most\r\nvirulent form. The diagnosis seems in every case to correspond exactly\r\nwith all the sensations that I have ever felt.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Man reading book] I remember going to the British Museum one\r\nday to read up the treatment for some slight ailment of which I had\r\na touch\xe2\x80\x94hay fever, I fancy it was. I got down the book, and read all\r\nI came to read; and then, in an unthinking moment, I idly turned the\r\nleaves, and began to indolently study diseases, generally. I forget\r\nwhich was the first distemper I plunged into\xe2\x80\x94some fearful, devastating\r\nscourge, I know\xe2\x80\x94and, before I had glanced half down the list of\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cpremonitory symptoms,\xe2\x80\x9d it was borne in upon me that I had fairly got\r\nit.\r\n\r\nI sat for awhile, frozen with horror; and then, in the listlessness of\r\ndespair, I again turned over the pages. I came to typhoid fever\xe2\x80\x94read\r\nthe symptoms\xe2\x80\x94discovered that I had typhoid fever, must have had it\r\nfor months without knowing it\xe2\x80\x94wondered what else I had got; turned up\r\nSt. Vitus\xe2\x80\x99s Dance\xe2\x80\x94found, as I expected, that I had that too,\xe2\x80\x94began to\r\nget interested in my case, and determined to sift it to the bottom,\r\nand so started alphabetically\xe2\x80\x94read up ague, and learnt that I was\r\nsickening for it, and that the acute stage would commence in about\r\nanother fortnight. Bright\xe2\x80\x99s disease, I was relieved to find, I had only\r\nin a modified form, and, so far as that was concerned, I might live\r\nfor years. Cholera I had, with severe complications; and diphtheria I\r\nseemed to have been born with. I plodded conscientiously through the\r\ntwenty-six letters, and the only malady I could conclude I had not got\r\nwas housemaid\xe2\x80\x99s knee.\r\n\r\nI felt rather hurt about this at first; it seemed somehow to be a\r\nsort of slight. Why hadn\xe2\x80\x99t I got housemaid\xe2\x80\x99s knee? Why this invidious\r\nreservation? After a while, however, less grasping feelings prevailed.\r\nI reflected that I had every other known malady in the pharmacology,\r\nand I grew less selfish, and determined to do without housemaid\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nknee. Gout, in its most malignant stage, it would appear, had seized\r\nme without my being aware of it; and zymosis I had evidently been\r\nsuffering with from boyhood. There were no more diseases after zymosis,\r\nso I concluded there was nothing else the matter with me.\r\n\r\nI sat and pondered. I thought what an interesting case I must be from\r\na medical point of view, what an acquisition I should be to a class!\r\nStudents would have no need to \xe2\x80\x9cwalk the hospitals,\xe2\x80\x9d if they had me. I\r\nwas a hospital in myself. All they need do would be to walk round me,\r\nand, after that, take their diploma.\r\n\r\nThen I wondered how long I had to live. I tried to examine myself. I\r\nfelt my pulse. I could not at first feel any pulse at all. Then, all\r\nof a sudden, it seemed to start off. I pulled out my watch and timed\r\nit. I made it a hundred and forty-seven to the minute. I tried to feel\r\nmy heart. I could not feel my heart. It had stopped beating. I have\r\nsince been induced to come to the opinion that it must have been there\r\nall the time, and must have been beating, but I cannot account for it.\r\nI patted myself all over my front, from what I call my waist up to my\r\nhead, and I went a bit round each side, and a little way up the back.\r\nBut I could not feel or hear anything. I tried to look at my tongue. I\r\nstuck it out as far as ever it would go, and I shut one eye, and tried\r\nto examine it with the other. I could only see the tip, and the only\r\nthing that I could gain from that was to feel more certain than before\r\nthat I had scarlet fever.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Man with walking stick] I had walked into that reading-room a\r\nhappy, healthy man. I crawled out a decrepit wreck.\r\n\r\nI went to my medical man. He is an old chum of mine, and feels my\r\npulse, and looks at my tongue, and talks about the weather, all for\r\nnothing, when I fancy I\xe2\x80\x99m ill; so I thought I would do him a good turn\r\nby going to him now. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a doctor wants,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cis practice.\r\nHe shall have me. He will get more practice out of me than out of\r\nseventeen hundred of your ordinary, commonplace patients, with only one\r\nor two diseases each.\xe2\x80\x9d So I went straight up and saw him, and he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will not take up your time, dear boy, with telling you what is the\r\nmatter with me. Life is brief, and you might pass away before I had\r\nfinished. But I will tell you what is _not_ the matter with me. I have\r\nnot got housemaid\xe2\x80\x99s knee. Why I have not got housemaid\xe2\x80\x99s knee, I cannot\r\ntell you; but the fact remains that I have not got it. Everything else,\r\nhowever, I _have_ got.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd I told him how I came to discover it all.\r\n\r\nThen he opened me and looked down me, and clutched hold of my wrist,\r\nand then he hit me over the chest when I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t expecting it\xe2\x80\x94a cowardly\r\nthing to do, I call it\xe2\x80\x94and immediately afterwards butted me with the\r\nside of his head. After that, he sat down and wrote out a prescription,\r\nand folded it up and gave it me, and I put it in my pocket and went out.\r\n\r\nI did not open it. I took it to the nearest chemist\xe2\x80\x99s, and handed it\r\nin. The man read it, and then handed it back.\r\n\r\nHe said he didn\xe2\x80\x99t keep it.\r\n\r\nI said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a chemist?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a chemist. If I was a co-operative stores and family hotel\r\ncombined, I might be able to oblige you. Being only a chemist hampers\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI read the prescription. It ran:\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9c1 lb. beefsteak, with\r\n    1 pt. bitter beer\r\n\r\n                                                          every 6 hours.\r\n\r\n    1 ten-mile walk every morning.\r\n\r\n    1 bed at 11 sharp every night.\r\n\r\n    And don\xe2\x80\x99t stuff up your head with things you don\xe2\x80\x99t understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nI followed the directions, with the happy result\xe2\x80\x94speaking for\r\nmyself\xe2\x80\x94that my life was preserved, and is still going on.\r\n\r\nIn the present instance, going back to the liver-pill circular, I had\r\nthe symptoms, beyond all mistake, the chief among them being \xe2\x80\x9ca general\r\ndisinclination to work of any kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat I suffer in that way no tongue can tell. From my earliest infancy\r\nI have been a martyr to it. As a boy, the disease hardly ever left\r\nme for a day. They did not know, then, that it was my liver. Medical\r\nscience was in a far less advanced state than now, and they used to put\r\nit down to laziness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you skulking little devil, you,\xe2\x80\x9d they would say, \xe2\x80\x9cget up and do\r\nsomething for your living, can\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94not knowing, of course, that I\r\nwas ill.\r\n\r\nAnd they didn\xe2\x80\x99t give me pills; they gave me clumps on the side of the\r\nhead. And, strange as it may appear, those clumps on the head often\r\ncured me\xe2\x80\x94for the time being. I have known one clump on the head have\r\nmore effect upon my liver, and make me feel more anxious to go straight\r\naway then and there, and do what was wanted to be done, without further\r\nloss of time, than a whole box of pills does now.\r\n\r\nYou know, it often is so\xe2\x80\x94those simple, old-fashioned remedies are\r\nsometimes more efficacious than all the dispensary stuff.\r\n\r\nWe sat there for half-an-hour, describing to each other our maladies. I\r\nexplained to George and William Harris how I felt when I got up in the\r\nmorning, and William Harris told us how he felt when he went to bed;\r\nand George stood on the hearth-rug, and gave us a clever and powerful\r\npiece of acting, illustrative of how he felt in the night.\r\n\r\nGeorge _fancies_ he is ill; but there\xe2\x80\x99s never anything really the\r\nmatter with him, you know.\r\n\r\nAt this point, Mrs. Poppets knocked at the door to know if we were\r\nready for supper. We smiled sadly at one another, and said we supposed\r\nwe had better try to swallow a bit. Harris said a little something in\r\none\xe2\x80\x99s stomach often kept the disease in check; and Mrs. Poppets brought\r\nthe tray in, and we drew up to the table, and toyed with a little steak\r\nand onions, and some rhubarb tart.\r\n\r\nI must have been very weak at the time; because I know, after the first\r\nhalf-hour or so, I seemed to take no interest whatever in my food\xe2\x80\x94an\r\nunusual thing for me\xe2\x80\x94and I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want any cheese.\r\n\r\nThis duty done, we refilled our glasses, lit our pipes, and resumed\r\nthe discussion upon our state of health. What it was that was actually\r\nthe matter with us, we none of us could be sure of; but the unanimous\r\nopinion was that it\xe2\x80\x94whatever it was\xe2\x80\x94had been brought on by overwork.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat we want is rest,\xe2\x80\x9d said Harris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRest and a complete change,\xe2\x80\x9d said George. \xe2\x80\x9cThe overstrain upon our\r\nbrains has produced a general depression throughout the system. Change\r\nof scene, and absence of the necessity for thought, will restore the\r\nmental equilibrium.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGeorge has a cousin, who is usually described in the charge-sheet\r\nas a medical student, so that he naturally has a somewhat\r\nfamily-physicianary way of putting things.\r\n\r\nI agreed with George, and suggested that we should seek out some\r\nretired and old-world spot, far from the madding crowd, and dream\r\naway a sunny week among its drowsy lanes\xe2\x80\x94some half-forgotten nook,\r\nhidden away by the fairies, out of reach of the noisy world\xe2\x80\x94some\r\nquaint-perched eyrie on the cliffs of Time, from whence the surging\r\nwaves of the nineteenth century would sound far-off and faint.\r\n\r\nHarris said he thought it would be humpy. He said he knew the sort of\r\nplace I meant; where everybody went to bed at eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock, and you\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t get a _Referee_ for love or money, and had to walk ten miles\r\nto get your baccy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Harris, \xe2\x80\x9cif you want rest and change, you can\xe2\x80\x99t beat a sea\r\ntrip.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI objected to the sea trip strongly. A sea trip does you good when you\r\nare going to have a couple of months of it, but, for a week, it is\r\nwicked.\r\n\r\nYou start on Monday with the idea implanted in your bosom that you\r\nare going to enjoy yourself. You wave an airy adieu to the boys on\r\nshore, light your biggest pipe, and swagger about the deck as if you\r\nwere Captain Cook, Sir Francis Drake, and Christopher Columbus all\r\nrolled into one. On Tuesday, you wish you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t come. On Wednesday,\r\nThursday, and Friday, you wish you were dead. On Saturday, you are able\r\nto swallow a little beef tea, and to sit up on deck, and answer with\r\na wan, sweet smile when kind-hearted people ask you how you feel now.\r\nOn Sunday, you begin to walk about again, and take solid food. And on\r\nMonday morning, as, with your bag and umbrella in your hand, you stand\r\nby the gunwale, waiting to step ashore, you begin to thoroughly like it.\r\n\r\nI remember my brother-in-law going for a short sea trip once, for the\r\nbenefit of his health. He took a return berth from London to Liverpool;\r\nand when he got to Liverpool, the only thing he was anxious about was\r\nto sell that return ticket.\r\n\r\nIt was offered round the town at a tremendous reduction, so I am told;\r\nand was eventually sold for eighteenpence to a bilious-looking youth\r\nwho had just been advised by his medical men to go to the sea-side, and\r\ntake exercise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSea-side!\xe2\x80\x9d said my brother-in-law, pressing the ticket affectionately\r\ninto his hand; \xe2\x80\x9cwhy, you\xe2\x80\x99ll have enough to last you a lifetime; and as\r\nfor exercise! why, you\xe2\x80\x99ll get more exercise, sitting down on that ship,\r\nthan you would turning somersaults on dry land.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe himself\xe2\x80\x94my brother-in-law\xe2\x80\x94came back by train. He said the\r\nNorth-Western Railway was healthy enough for him.\r\n\r\nAnother fellow I knew went for a week\xe2\x80\x99s voyage round the coast, and,\r\nbefore they started, the steward came to him to ask whether he would\r\npay for each meal as he had it, or arrange beforehand for the whole\r\nseries.\r\n\r\nThe steward recommended the latter course, as it would come so much\r\ncheaper. He said they would do him for the whole week at two pounds\r\nfive. He said for breakfast there would be fish, followed by a grill.\r\nLunch was at one, and consisted of four courses. Dinner at six\xe2\x80\x94soup,\r\nfish, entree, joint, poultry, salad, sweets, cheese, and dessert. And a\r\nlight meat supper at ten.\r\n\r\nMy friend thought he would close on the two-pound-five job (he is a\r\nhearty eater), and did so.\r\n\r\nLunch came just as they were off Sheerness. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t feel so hungry\r\nas he thought he should, and so contented himself with a bit of boiled\r\nbeef, and some strawberries and cream. He pondered a good deal during\r\nthe afternoon, and at one time it seemed to him that he had been eating\r\nnothing but boiled beef for weeks, and at other times it seemed that he\r\nmust have been living on strawberries and cream for years.\r\n\r\nNeither the beef nor the strawberries and cream seemed happy,\r\neither\xe2\x80\x94seemed discontented like.\r\n\r\nAt six, they came and told him dinner was ready. The announcement\r\naroused no enthusiasm within him, but he felt that there was some of\r\nthat two-pound-five to be worked off, and he held on to ropes and\r\nthings and went down. A pleasant odour of onions and hot ham, mingled\r\nwith fried fish and greens, greeted him at the bottom of the ladder;\r\nand then the steward came up with an oily smile, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can I get you, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Picture: Man feeling ill] \xe2\x80\x9cGet me out of this,\xe2\x80\x9d was the feeble reply.\r\n\r\nAnd they ran him up quick, and propped him up, over to leeward, and\r\nleft him.\r\n\r\nFor the next four days he lived a simple and blameless life on thin\r\ncaptain\xe2\x80\x99s biscuits (I mean that the biscuits were thin, not the\r\ncaptain) and soda-water; but, towards Saturday, he got uppish, and went\r\nin for weak tea and dry toast, and on Monday he was gorging himself on\r\nchicken broth. He left the ship on Tuesday, and as it steamed away from\r\nthe landing-stage he gazed after it regretfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere she goes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthere she goes, with two pounds\xe2\x80\x99 worth of\r\nfood on board that belongs to me, and that I haven\xe2\x80\x99t had.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe said that if they had given him another day he thought he could have\r\nput it straight.\r\n\r\nSo I set my face against the sea trip. Not, as I explained, upon my\r\nown account. I was never queer. But I was afraid for George. George\r\nsaid he should be all right, and would rather like it, but he would\r\nadvise Harris and me not to think of it, as he felt sure we should\r\nboth be ill. Harris said that, to himself, it was always a mystery how\r\npeople managed to get sick at sea\xe2\x80\x94said he thought people must do it on\r\npurpose, from affectation\xe2\x80\x94said he had often wished to be, but had never\r\nbeen able.\r\n\r\nThen he told us anecdotes of how he had gone across the Channel when it\r\nwas so rough that the passengers had to be tied into their berths, and\r\nhe and the captain were the only two living souls on board who were not\r\nill. Sometimes it was he and the second mate who were not ill; but it\r\nwas generally he and one other man. If not he and another man, then it\r\nwas he by himself.\r\n\r\nIt is a curious fact, but nobody ever is sea-sick\xe2\x80\x94on land. At sea, you\r\ncome across plenty of people very bad indeed, whole boat-loads of them;\r\nbut I never met a man yet, on land, who had ever known at all what it\r\nwas to be sea-sick. Where the thousands upon thousands of bad sailors\r\nthat swarm in every ship hide themselves when they are on land is a\r\nmystery.\r\n\r\nIf most men were like a fellow I saw on the Yarmouth boat one day, I\r\ncould account for the seeming enigma easily enough. It was just off\r\nSouthend Pier, I recollect, and he was leaning out through one of the\r\nport-holes in a very dangerous position. I went up to him to try and\r\nsave him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHi! come further in,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, shaking him by the shoulder. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll be\r\noverboard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh my! I wish I was,\xe2\x80\x9d was the only answer I could get; and there I had\r\nto leave him.\r\n\r\nThree weeks afterwards, I met him in the coffee-room of a Bath hotel,\r\ntalking about his voyages, and explaining, with enthusiasm, how he\r\nloved the sea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood sailor!\xe2\x80\x9d he replied in answer to a mild young man\xe2\x80\x99s envious\r\nquery; \xe2\x80\x9cwell, I did feel a little queer _once_, I confess. It was off\r\nCape Horn. The vessel was wrecked the next morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWeren\xe2\x80\x99t you a little shaky by Southend Pier one day, and wanted to be\r\nthrown overboard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSouthend Pier!\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, with a puzzled expression.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; going down to Yarmouth, last Friday three weeks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, ah\xe2\x80\x94yes,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, brightening up; \xe2\x80\x9cI remember now. I did have\r\na headache that afternoon. It was the pickles, you know. They were the\r\nmost disgraceful pickles I ever tasted in a respectable boat. Did _you_\r\nhave any?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor myself, I have discovered an excellent preventive against\r\nsea-sickness, in balancing myself. You stand in the centre of the deck,\r\nand, as the ship heaves and pitches, you move your body about, so as\r\nto keep it always straight. When the front of the ship rises, you lean\r\nforward, till the deck almost touches your nose; and when its back end\r\ngets up, you lean backwards. This is all very well for an hour or two;\r\nbut you can\xe2\x80\x99t balance yourself for a week.\r\n\r\nGeorge said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s go up the river.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe said we should have fresh air, exercise and quiet; the constant\r\nchange of scene would occupy our minds (including what there was of\r\nHarris\xe2\x80\x99s); and the hard work would give us a good appetite, and make us\r\nsleep well.\r\n\r\nHarris said he didn\xe2\x80\x99t think George ought to do anything that would\r\nhave a tendency to make him sleepier than he always was, as it might\r\nbe dangerous. He said he didn\xe2\x80\x99t very well understand how George was\r\ngoing to sleep any more than he did now, seeing that there were only\r\ntwenty-four hours in each day, summer and winter alike; but thought\r\nthat if he _did_ sleep any more, he might just as well be dead, and so\r\nsave his board and lodging.\r\n\r\nHarris said, however, that the river would suit him to a \xe2\x80\x9cT.\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what a \xe2\x80\x9cT\xe2\x80\x9d is (except a sixpenny one, which includes\r\nbread-and-butter and cake _ad lib._, and is cheap at the price, if you\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t had any dinner). It seems to suit everybody, however, which is\r\ngreatly to its credit.\r\n\r\nIt suited me to a \xe2\x80\x9cT\xe2\x80\x9d too, and Harris and I both said it was a good\r\nidea of George\xe2\x80\x99s; and we said it in a tone that seemed to somehow imply\r\nthat we were surprised that George should have come out so sensible.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Montmorency] The only one who was not struck with the\r\nsuggestion was Montmorency. He never did care for the river, did\r\nMontmorency.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all very well for you fellows,\xe2\x80\x9d he says; \xe2\x80\x9cyou like it, but _I_\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t. There\xe2\x80\x99s nothing for me to do. Scenery is not in my line, and I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t smoke. If I see a rat, you won\xe2\x80\x99t stop; and if I go to sleep, you\r\nget fooling about with the boat, and slop me overboard. If you ask me,\r\nI call the whole thing bally foolishness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe were three to one, however, and the motion was carried.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II.\r\n\r\n\r\nPlans discussed.\xe2\x80\x94Pleasures of \xe2\x80\x9ccamping-out,\xe2\x80\x9d on fine nights.\xe2\x80\x94Ditto, wet\r\nnights.\xe2\x80\x94Compromise decided on.\xe2\x80\x94Montmorency, first impressions of.\xe2\x80\x94Fears\r\nlest he is too good for this world, fears subsequently dismissed as\r\ngroundless.\xe2\x80\x94Meeting adjourns.\r\n\r\nWe pulled out the maps, and discussed plans.\r\n\r\nWe arranged to start on the following Saturday from Kingston. Harris\r\nand I would go down in the morning, and take the boat up to Chertsey,\r\nand George, who would not be able to get away from the City till the\r\nafternoon (George goes to sleep at a bank from ten to four each day,\r\nexcept Saturdays, when they wake him up and put him outside at two),\r\nwould meet us there.\r\n\r\nShould we \xe2\x80\x9ccamp out\xe2\x80\x9d or sleep at inns?\r\n\r\nGeorge and I were for camping out. We said it would be so wild and\r\nfree, so patriarchal like.\r\n\r\nSlowly the golden memory of the dead sun fades from the hearts of the\r\ncold, sad clouds. Silent, like sorrowing children, the birds have\r\nceased their song, and only the moorhen\xe2\x80\x99s plaintive cry and the harsh\r\ncroak of the corncrake stirs the awed hush around the couch of waters,\r\nwhere the dying day breathes out her last.\r\n\r\nFrom the dim woods on either bank, Night\xe2\x80\x99s ghostly army, the grey\r\nshadows, creep out with noiseless tread to chase away the lingering\r\nrear-guard of the light, and pass, with noiseless, unseen feet, above\r\nthe waving river-grass, and through the sighing rushes; and Night,\r\nupon her sombre throne, folds her black wings above the darkening\r\nworld, and, from her phantom palace, lit by the pale stars, reigns in\r\nstillness.\r\n\r\n                          [Picture: River scene]\r\n\r\nThen we run our little boat into some quiet nook, and the tent is\r\npitched, and the frugal supper cooked and eaten. Then the big pipes\r\nare filled and lighted, and the pleasant chat goes round in musical\r\nundertone; while, in the pauses of our talk, the river, playing round\r\nthe boat, prattles strange old tales and secrets, sings low the old\r\nchild\xe2\x80\x99s song that it has sung so many thousand years\xe2\x80\x94will sing so many\r\nthousand years to come, before its voice grows harsh and old\xe2\x80\x94a song\r\nthat we, who have learnt to love its changing face, who have so often\r\nnestled on its yielding bosom, think, somehow, we understand, though we\r\ncould not tell you in mere words the story that we listen to.\r\n\r\nAnd we sit there, by its margin, while the moon, who loves it too,\r\nstoops down to kiss it with a sister\xe2\x80\x99s kiss, and throws her silver arms\r\naround it clingingly; and we watch it as it flows, ever singing, ever\r\nwhispering, out to meet its king, the sea\xe2\x80\x94till our voices die away in\r\nsilence, and the pipes go out\xe2\x80\x94till we, common-place, everyday young men\r\nenough, feel strangely full of thoughts, half sad, half sweet, and do\r\nnot care or want to speak\xe2\x80\x94till we laugh, and, rising, knock the ashes\r\nfrom our burnt-out pipes, and say \xe2\x80\x9cGood-night,\xe2\x80\x9d and, lulled by the\r\nlapping water and the rustling trees, we fall asleep beneath the great,\r\nstill stars, and dream that the world is young again\xe2\x80\x94young and sweet\r\nas she used to be ere the centuries of fret and care had furrowed her\r\nfair face, ere her children\xe2\x80\x99s sins and follies had made old her loving\r\nheart\xe2\x80\x94sweet as she was in those bygone days when, a new-made mother,\r\nshe nursed us, her children, upon her own deep breast\xe2\x80\x94ere the wiles\r\nof painted civilization had lured us away from her fond arms, and the\r\npoisoned sneers of artificiality had made us ashamed of the simple life\r\nwe led with her, and the simple, stately home where mankind was born so\r\nmany thousands years ago.\r\n\r\nHarris said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow about when it rained?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYou can never rouse Harris. There is no poetry about Harris\xe2\x80\x94no wild\r\nyearning for the unattainable. Harris never \xe2\x80\x9cweeps, he knows not why.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nIf Harris\xe2\x80\x99s eyes fill with tears, you can bet it is because Harris has\r\nbeen eating raw onions, or has put too much Worcester over his chop.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Mermaid] If you were to stand at night by the sea-shore with\r\nHarris, and say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHark! do you not hear? Is it but the mermaids singing deep below the\r\nwaving waters; or sad spirits, chanting dirges for white corpses, held\r\nby seaweed?\xe2\x80\x9d Harris would take you by the arm, and say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know what it is, old man; you\xe2\x80\x99ve got a chill. Now, you come along\r\nwith me. I know a place round the corner here, where you can get a drop\r\nof the finest Scotch whisky you ever tasted\xe2\x80\x94put you right in less than\r\nno time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHarris always does know a place round the corner where you can get\r\nsomething brilliant in the drinking line. I believe that if you met\r\nHarris up in Paradise (supposing such a thing likely), he would\r\nimmediately greet you with:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo glad you\xe2\x80\x99ve come, old fellow; I\xe2\x80\x99ve found a nice place round the\r\ncorner here, where you can get some really first-class nectar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the present instance, however, as regarded the camping out, his\r\npractical view of the matter came as a very timely hint. Camping out in\r\nrainy weather is not pleasant.\r\n\r\nIt is evening. You are wet through, and there is a good two inches of\r\nwater in the boat, and all the things are damp. You find a place on the\r\nbanks that is not quite so puddly as other places you have seen, and\r\nyou land and lug out the tent, and two of you proceed to fix it.\r\n\r\nIt is soaked and heavy, and it flops about, and tumbles down on you,\r\nand clings round your head and makes you mad. The rain is pouring\r\nsteadily down all the time. It is difficult enough to fix a tent in dry\r\nweather: in wet, the task becomes herculean. Instead of helping you, it\r\nseems to you that the other man is simply playing the fool. Just as you\r\nget your side beautifully fixed, he gives it a hoist from his end, and\r\nspoils it all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere! what are you up to?\xe2\x80\x9d you call out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are _you_ up to?\xe2\x80\x9d he retorts; \xe2\x80\x9cleggo, can\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t pull it; you\xe2\x80\x99ve got it all wrong, you stupid ass!\xe2\x80\x9d you shout.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d he yells back; \xe2\x80\x9clet go your side!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tell you you\xe2\x80\x99ve got it all wrong!\xe2\x80\x9d you roar, wishing that you could\r\nget at him; and you give your ropes a lug that pulls all his pegs out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, the bally idiot!\xe2\x80\x9d you hear him mutter to himself; and then comes\r\na savage haul, and away goes your side. You lay down the mallet and\r\nstart to go round and tell him what you think about the whole business,\r\nand, at the same time, he starts round in the same direction to come\r\nand explain his views to you. And you follow each other round and\r\nround, swearing at one another, until the tent tumbles down in a heap,\r\nand leaves you looking at each other across its ruins, when you both\r\nindignantly exclaim, in the same breath:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere you are! what did I tell you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMeanwhile the third man, who has been baling out the boat, and who has\r\nspilled the water down his sleeve, and has been cursing away to himself\r\nsteadily for the last ten minutes, wants to know what the thundering\r\nblazes you\xe2\x80\x99re playing at, and why the blarmed tent isn\xe2\x80\x99t up yet.\r\n\r\nAt last, somehow or other, it does get up, and you land the things. It\r\nis hopeless attempting to make a wood fire, so you light the methylated\r\nspirit stove, and crowd round that.\r\n\r\nRainwater is the chief article of diet at supper. The bread is\r\ntwo-thirds rainwater, the beefsteak-pie is exceedingly rich in it, and\r\nthe jam, and the butter, and the salt, and the coffee have all combined\r\nwith it to make soup.\r\n\r\nAfter supper, you find your tobacco is damp, and you cannot smoke.\r\nLuckily you have a bottle of the stuff that cheers and inebriates, if\r\ntaken in proper quantity, and this restores to you sufficient interest\r\nin life to induce you to go to bed.\r\n\r\nThere you dream that an elephant has suddenly sat down on your chest,\r\nand that the volcano has exploded and thrown you down to the bottom\r\nof the sea\xe2\x80\x94the elephant still sleeping peacefully on your bosom. You\r\nwake up and grasp the idea that something terrible really has happened.\r\nYour first impression is that the end of the world has come; and then\r\nyou think that this cannot be, and that it is thieves and murderers,\r\nor else fire, and this opinion you express in the usual method. No\r\nhelp comes, however, and all you know is that thousands of people are\r\nkicking you, and you are being smothered.\r\n\r\nSomebody else seems in trouble, too. You can hear his faint cries\r\ncoming from underneath your bed. Determining, at all events, to sell\r\nyour life dearly, you struggle frantically, hitting out right and\r\nleft with arms and legs, and yelling lustily the while, and at last\r\nsomething gives way, and you find your head in the fresh air. Two feet\r\noff, you dimly observe a half-dressed ruffian, waiting to kill you,\r\nand you are preparing for a life-and-death struggle with him, when it\r\nbegins to dawn upon you that it\xe2\x80\x99s Jim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s you, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d he says, recognising you at the same moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d you answer, rubbing your eyes; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat\xe2\x80\x99s happened?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBally tent\xe2\x80\x99s blown down, I think,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99s Bill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen you both raise up your voices and shout for \xe2\x80\x9cBill!\xe2\x80\x9d and the ground\r\nbeneath you heaves and rocks, and the muffled voice that you heard\r\nbefore replies from out the ruin:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet off my head, can\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Bill struggles out, a muddy, trampled wreck, and in an\r\nunnecessarily aggressive mood\xe2\x80\x94he being under the evident belief that\r\nthe whole thing has been done on purpose.\r\n\r\nIn the morning you are all three speechless, owing to having caught\r\nsevere colds in the night; you also feel very quarrelsome, and you\r\nswear at each other in hoarse whispers during the whole of breakfast\r\ntime.\r\n\r\nWe therefore decided that we would sleep out on fine nights; and hotel\r\nit, and inn it, and pub. it, like respectable folks, when it was wet,\r\nor when we felt inclined for a change.\r\n\r\nMontmorency hailed this compromise with much approval. He does not\r\nrevel in romantic solitude. Give him something noisy; and if a trifle\r\nlow, so much the jollier. To look at Montmorency you would imagine\r\nthat he was an angel sent upon the earth, for some reason withheld\r\nfrom mankind, in the shape of a small fox-terrier. There is a sort of\r\nOh-what-a-wicked-world-this-is-and-how-I-wish-I-could-do-something-to-\r\nmake-it-better-and-nobler expression about Montmorency that has\r\nbeen known to bring the tears into the eyes of pious old ladies and\r\ngentlemen.\r\n\r\nWhen first he came to live at my expense, I never thought I should be\r\nable to get him to stop long. I used to sit down and look at him, as he\r\nsat on the rug and looked up at me, and think: \xe2\x80\x9cOh, that dog will never\r\nlive. He will be snatched up to the bright skies in a chariot, that is\r\nwhat will happen to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut, when I had paid for about a dozen chickens that he had killed;\r\nand had dragged him, growling and kicking, by the scruff of his neck,\r\nout of a hundred and fourteen street fights; and had had a dead cat\r\nbrought round for my inspection by an irate female, who called me\r\na murderer; and had been summoned by the man next door but one for\r\nhaving a ferocious dog at large, that had kept him pinned up in his own\r\ntool-shed, afraid to venture his nose outside the door for over two\r\nhours on a cold night; and had learned that the gardener, unknown to\r\nmyself, had won thirty shillings by backing him to kill rats against\r\ntime, then I began to think that maybe they\xe2\x80\x99d let him remain on earth\r\nfor a bit longer, after all.\r\n\r\nTo hang about a stable, and collect a gang of the most disreputable\r\ndogs to be found in the town, and lead them out to march round the\r\nslums to fight other disreputable dogs, is Montmorency\xe2\x80\x99s idea of\r\n\xe2\x80\x9clife;\xe2\x80\x9d and so, as I before observed, he gave to the suggestion of\r\ninns, and pubs., and hotels his most emphatic approbation.\r\n\r\nHaving thus settled the sleeping arrangements to the satisfaction of\r\nall four of us, the only thing left to discuss was what we should take\r\nwith us; and this we had begun to argue, when Harris said he\xe2\x80\x99d had\r\nenough oratory for one night, and proposed that we should go out and\r\nhave a smile, saying that he had found a place, round by the square,\r\nwhere you could really get a drop of Irish worth drinking.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Whisky glass] George said he felt thirsty (I never knew\r\nGeorge when he didn\xe2\x80\x99t); and, as I had a presentiment that a little\r\nwhisky, warm, with a slice of lemon, would do my complaint good, the\r\ndebate was, by common assent, adjourned to the following night; and the\r\nassembly put on its hats and went out.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III.\r\n\r\n\r\nArrangements settled.\xe2\x80\x94Harris\xe2\x80\x99s method of doing work.\xe2\x80\x94How the elderly,\r\nfamily-man puts up a picture.\xe2\x80\x94George makes a sensible, remark.\xe2\x80\x94Delights\r\nof early morning bathing.\xe2\x80\x94Provisions for getting upset.\r\n\r\nSo, on the following evening, we again assembled, to discuss and\r\narrange our plans. Harris said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, the first thing to settle is what to take with us. Now, you get\r\na bit of paper and write down, J., and you get the grocery catalogue,\r\nGeorge, and somebody give me a bit of pencil, and then I\xe2\x80\x99ll make out a\r\nlist.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s Harris all over\xe2\x80\x94so ready to take the burden of everything\r\nhimself, and put it on the backs of other people.\r\n\r\nHe always reminds me of my poor Uncle Podger. You never saw such a\r\ncommotion up and down a house, in all your life, as when my Uncle\r\nPodger undertook to do a job. A picture would have come home from the\r\nframe-maker\xe2\x80\x99s, and be standing in the dining-room, waiting to be put\r\nup; and Aunt Podger would ask what was to be done with it, and Uncle\r\nPodger would say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you leave that to _me_. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you, any of you, worry yourselves\r\nabout that. _I\xe2\x80\x99ll_ do all that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then he would take off his coat, and begin. He would send the girl\r\nout for sixpen\xe2\x80\x99orth of nails, and then one of the boys after her to\r\ntell her what size to get; and, from that, he would gradually work\r\ndown, and start the whole house.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Candle] \xe2\x80\x9cNow you go and get me my hammer, Will,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nwould shout; \xe2\x80\x9cand you bring me the rule, Tom; and I shall want the\r\nstep-ladder, and I had better have a kitchen-chair, too; and, Jim! you\r\nrun round to Mr. Goggles, and tell him, \xe2\x80\x98Pa\xe2\x80\x99s kind regards, and hopes\r\nhis leg\xe2\x80\x99s better; and will he lend him his spirit-level?\xe2\x80\x99 And don\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\ngo, Maria, because I shall want somebody to hold me the light; and when\r\nthe girl comes back, she must go out again for a bit of picture-cord;\r\nand Tom!\xe2\x80\x94where\xe2\x80\x99s Tom?\xe2\x80\x94Tom, you come here; I shall want you to hand me\r\nup the picture.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then he would lift up the picture, and drop it, and it would come\r\nout of the frame, and he would try to save the glass, and cut himself;\r\nand then he would spring round the room, looking for his handkerchief.\r\nHe could not find his handkerchief, because it was in the pocket of the\r\ncoat he had taken off, and he did not know where he had put the coat,\r\nand all the house had to leave off looking for his tools, and start\r\nlooking for his coat; while he would dance round and hinder them.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Nails etc.] \xe2\x80\x9cDoesn\xe2\x80\x99t anybody in the whole house know where my\r\ncoat is? I never came across such a set in all my life\xe2\x80\x94upon my word I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t. Six of you!\xe2\x80\x94and you can\xe2\x80\x99t find a coat that I put down not five\r\nminutes ago! Well, of all the\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he\xe2\x80\x99d get up, and find that he had been sitting on it, and would\r\ncall out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you can give it up! I\xe2\x80\x99ve found it myself now. Might just as well\r\nask the cat to find anything as expect you people to find it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd, when half an hour had been spent in tying up his finger, and a\r\nnew glass had been got, and the tools, and the ladder, and the chair,\r\nand the candle had been brought, he would have another go, the whole\r\nfamily, including the girl and the charwoman, standing round in a\r\nsemi-circle, ready to help. Two people would have to hold the chair,\r\nand a third would help him up on it, and hold him there, and a fourth\r\nwould hand him a nail, and a fifth would pass him up the hammer, and he\r\nwould take hold of the nail, and drop it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere!\xe2\x80\x9d he would say, in an injured tone, \xe2\x80\x9cnow the nail\xe2\x80\x99s gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd we would all have to go down on our knees and grovel for it, while\r\nhe would stand on the chair, and grunt, and want to know if he was to\r\nbe kept there all the evening.\r\n\r\nThe nail would be found at last, but by that time he would have lost\r\nthe hammer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99s the hammer? What did I do with the hammer? Great heavens!\r\nSeven of you, gaping round there, and you don\xe2\x80\x99t know what I did with\r\nthe hammer!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe would find the hammer for him, and then he would have lost sight of\r\nthe mark he had made on the wall, where the nail was to go in, and each\r\nof us had to get up on the chair, beside him, and see if we could find\r\nit; and we would each discover it in a different place, and he would\r\ncall us all fools, one after another, and tell us to get down. And\r\nhe would take the rule, and re-measure, and find that he wanted half\r\nthirty-one and three-eighths inches from the corner, and would try to\r\ndo it in his head, and go mad.\r\n\r\nAnd we would all try to do it in our heads, and all arrive at different\r\nresults, and sneer at one another. And in the general row, the original\r\nnumber would be forgotten, and Uncle Podger would have to measure it\r\nagain.\r\n\r\nHe would use a bit of string this time, and at the critical moment,\r\nwhen the old fool was leaning over the chair at an angle of forty-five,\r\nand trying to reach a point three inches beyond what was possible for\r\nhim to reach, the string would slip, and down he would slide on to the\r\npiano, a really fine musical effect being produced by the suddenness\r\nwith which his head and body struck all the notes at the same time.\r\n\r\nAnd Aunt Maria would say that she would not allow the children to stand\r\nround and hear such language.\r\n\r\nAt last, Uncle Podger would get the spot fixed again, and put the point\r\nof the nail on it with his left hand, and take the hammer in his right\r\nhand. And, with the first blow, he would smash his thumb, and drop the\r\nhammer, with a yell, on somebody\xe2\x80\x99s toes.\r\n\r\nAunt Maria would mildly observe that, next time Uncle Podger was going\r\nto hammer a nail into the wall, she hoped he\xe2\x80\x99d let her know in time, so\r\nthat she could make arrangements to go and spend a week with her mother\r\nwhile it was being done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! you women, you make such a fuss over everything,\xe2\x80\x9d Uncle Podger\r\nwould reply, picking himself up. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I _like_ doing a little job of\r\nthis sort.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n                [Picture: Uncle Podger admiring his work]\r\n\r\nAnd then he would have another try, and, at the second blow, the nail\r\nwould go clean through the plaster, and half the hammer after it,\r\nand Uncle Podger be precipitated against the wall with force nearly\r\nsufficient to flatten his nose.\r\n\r\nThen we had to find the rule and the string again, and a new hole was\r\nmade; and, about midnight, the picture would be up\xe2\x80\x94very crooked and\r\ninsecure, the wall for yards round looking as if it had been smoothed\r\ndown with a rake, and everybody dead beat and wretched\xe2\x80\x94except Uncle\r\nPodger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere you are,\xe2\x80\x9d he would say, stepping heavily off the chair on to\r\nthe charwoman\xe2\x80\x99s corns, and surveying the mess he had made with evident\r\npride. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, some people would have had a man in to do a little thing\r\nlike that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHarris will be just that sort of man when he grows up, I know, and I\r\ntold him so. I said I could not permit him to take so much labour upon\r\nhimself. I said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; _you_ get the paper, and the pencil, and the catalogue, and George\r\nwrite down, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll do the work.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe first list we made out had to be discarded. It was clear that the\r\nupper reaches of the Thames would not allow of the navigation of a boat\r\nsufficiently large to take the things we had set down as indispensable;\r\nso we tore the list up, and looked at one another!\r\n\r\nGeorge said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know we are on a wrong track altogether. We must not think of\r\nthe things we could do with, but only of the things that we can\xe2\x80\x99t do\r\nwithout.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGeorge comes out really quite sensible at times. You\xe2\x80\x99d be surprised. I\r\ncall that downright wisdom, not merely as regards the present case, but\r\nwith reference to our trip up the river of life, generally. How many\r\npeople, on that voyage, load up the boat till it is ever in danger of\r\nswamping with a store of foolish things which they think essential to\r\nthe pleasure and comfort of the trip, but which are really only useless\r\nlumber.\r\n\r\nHow they pile the poor little craft mast-high with fine clothes and big\r\nhouses; with useless servants, and a host of swell friends that do not\r\ncare twopence for them, and that they do not care three ha\xe2\x80\x99pence for;\r\nwith expensive entertainments that nobody enjoys, with formalities and\r\nfashions, with pretence and ostentation, and with\xe2\x80\x94oh, heaviest, maddest\r\nlumber of all!\xe2\x80\x94the dread of what will my neighbour think, with luxuries\r\nthat only cloy, with pleasures that bore, with empty show that, like\r\nthe criminal\xe2\x80\x99s iron crown of yore, makes to bleed and swoon the aching\r\nhead that wears it!\r\n\r\nIt is lumber, man\xe2\x80\x94all lumber! Throw it overboard. It makes the boat so\r\nheavy to pull, you nearly faint at the oars. It makes it so cumbersome\r\nand dangerous to manage, you never know a moment\xe2\x80\x99s freedom from anxiety\r\nand care, never gain a moment\xe2\x80\x99s rest for dreamy laziness\xe2\x80\x94no time to\r\nwatch the windy shadows skimming lightly o\xe2\x80\x99er the shallows, or the\r\nglittering sunbeams flitting in and out among the ripples, or the great\r\ntrees by the margin looking down at their own image, or the woods all\r\ngreen and golden, or the lilies white and yellow, or the sombre-waving\r\nrushes, or the sedges, or the orchis, or the blue forget-me-nots.\r\n\r\nThrow the lumber over, man! Let your boat of life be light, packed\r\nwith only what you need\xe2\x80\x94a homely home and simple pleasures, one or two\r\nfriends, worth the name, someone to love and someone to love you, a\r\ncat, a dog, and a pipe or two, enough to eat and enough to wear, and a\r\nlittle more than enough to drink; for thirst is a dangerous thing.\r\n\r\nYou will find the boat easier to pull then, and it will not be so\r\nliable to upset, and it will not matter so much if it does upset; good,\r\nplain merchandise will stand water. You will have time to think as well\r\nas to work. Time to drink in life\xe2\x80\x99s sunshine\xe2\x80\x94time to listen to the\r\n\xc3\x86olian music that the wind of God draws from the human heart-strings\r\naround us\xe2\x80\x94time to\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nI beg your pardon, really. I quite forgot.\r\n\r\nWell, we left the list to George, and he began it.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Tent] \xe2\x80\x9cWe won\xe2\x80\x99t take a tent,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested George; \xe2\x80\x9cwe will have\r\na boat with a cover. It is ever so much simpler, and more comfortable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt seemed a good thought, and we adopted it. I do not know whether you\r\nhave ever seen the thing I mean. You fix iron hoops up over the boat,\r\nand stretch a huge canvas over them, and fasten it down all round,\r\nfrom stem to stern, and it converts the boat into a sort of little\r\nhouse, and it is beautifully cosy, though a trifle stuffy; but there,\r\neverything has its drawbacks, as the man said when his mother-in-law\r\ndied, and they came down upon him for the funeral expenses.\r\n\r\nGeorge said that in that case we must take a rug each, a lamp, some\r\nsoap, a brush and comb (between us), a toothbrush (each), a basin,\r\nsome tooth-powder, some shaving tackle (sounds like a French exercise,\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t it?), and a couple of big-towels for bathing. I notice that\r\npeople always make gigantic arrangements for bathing when they are\r\ngoing anywhere near the water, but that they don\xe2\x80\x99t bathe much when they\r\nare there.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Sea-side scene] It is the same when you go to the sea-side. I\r\nalways determine\xe2\x80\x94when thinking over the matter in London\xe2\x80\x94that I\xe2\x80\x99ll get\r\nup early every morning, and go and have a dip before breakfast, and I\r\nreligiously pack up a pair of drawers and a bath towel. I always get\r\nred bathing drawers. I rather fancy myself in red drawers. They suit\r\nmy complexion so. But when I get to the sea I don\xe2\x80\x99t feel somehow that\r\nI want that early morning bathe nearly so much as I did when I was in\r\ntown.\r\n\r\nOn the contrary, I feel more that I want to stop in bed till the last\r\nmoment, and then come down and have my breakfast. Once or twice virtue\r\nhas triumphed, and I have got out at six and half-dressed myself, and\r\nhave taken my drawers and towel, and stumbled dismally off. But I\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t enjoyed it. They seem to keep a specially cutting east wind,\r\nwaiting for me, when I go to bathe in the early morning; and they pick\r\nout all the three-cornered stones, and put them on the top, and they\r\nsharpen up the rocks and cover the points over with a bit of sand so\r\nthat I can\xe2\x80\x99t see them, and they take the sea and put it two miles out,\r\nso that I have to huddle myself up in my arms and hop, shivering,\r\nthrough six inches of water. And when I do get to the sea, it is rough\r\nand quite insulting.\r\n\r\nOne huge wave catches me up and chucks me in a sitting posture, as hard\r\nas ever it can, down on to a rock which has been put there for me. And,\r\nbefore I\xe2\x80\x99ve said \xe2\x80\x9cOh! Ugh!\xe2\x80\x9d and found out what has gone, the wave comes\r\nback and carries me out to mid-ocean. I begin to strike out frantically\r\nfor the shore, and wonder if I shall ever see home and friends again,\r\nand wish I\xe2\x80\x99d been kinder to my little sister when a boy (when I was a\r\nboy, I mean). Just when I have given up all hope, a wave retires and\r\nleaves me sprawling like a star-fish on the sand, and I get up and look\r\nback and find that I\xe2\x80\x99ve been swimming for my life in two feet of water.\r\nI hop back and dress, and crawl home, where I have to pretend I liked\r\nit.\r\n\r\nIn the present instance, we all talked as if we were going to have a\r\nlong swim every morning.\r\n\r\nGeorge said it was so pleasant to wake up in the boat in the fresh\r\nmorning, and plunge into the limpid river. Harris said there was\r\nnothing like a swim before breakfast to give you an appetite. He said\r\nit always gave him an appetite. George said that if it was going to\r\nmake Harris eat more than Harris ordinarily ate, then he should protest\r\nagainst Harris having a bath at all.\r\n\r\nHe said there would be quite enough hard work in towing sufficient food\r\nfor Harris up against stream, as it was.\r\n\r\nI urged upon George, however, how much pleasanter it would be to have\r\nHarris clean and fresh about the boat, even if we did have to take a\r\nfew more hundredweight of provisions; and he got to see it in my light,\r\nand withdrew his opposition to Harris\xe2\x80\x99s bath.\r\n\r\nAgreed, finally, that we should take _three_ bath towels, so as not to\r\nkeep each other waiting.\r\n\r\nFor clothes, George said two suits of flannel would be sufficient, as\r\nwe could wash them ourselves, in the river, when they got dirty. We\r\nasked him if he had ever tried washing flannels in the river, and he\r\nreplied: \xe2\x80\x9cNo, not exactly himself like; but he knew some fellows who\r\nhad, and it was easy enough;\xe2\x80\x9d and Harris and I were weak enough to\r\nfancy he knew what he was talking about, and that three respectable\r\nyoung men, without position or influence, and with no experience in\r\nwashing, could really clean their own shirts and trousers in the river\r\nThames with a bit of soap.\r\n\r\nWe were to learn in the days to come, when it was too late, that George\r\nwas a miserable impostor, who could evidently have known nothing\r\nwhatever about the matter. If you had seen these clothes after\xe2\x80\x94but, as\r\nthe shilling shockers say, we anticipate.\r\n\r\nGeorge impressed upon us to take a change of under-things and plenty\r\nof socks, in case we got upset and wanted a change; also plenty of\r\nhandkerchiefs, as they would do to wipe things, and a pair of leather\r\nboots as well as our boating shoes, as we should want them if we got\r\nupset.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe food question.\xe2\x80\x94Objections to paraffine oil as an\r\natmosphere.\xe2\x80\x94Advantages of cheese as a travelling companion.\xe2\x80\x94A married\r\nwoman deserts her home.\xe2\x80\x94Further provision for getting upset.\xe2\x80\x94I\r\npack.\xe2\x80\x94Cussedness of tooth-brushes.\xe2\x80\x94George and Harris pack.\xe2\x80\x94Awful\r\nbehaviour of Montmorency.\xe2\x80\x94We retire to rest.\r\n\r\nThen we discussed the food question. George said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBegin with breakfast.\xe2\x80\x9d (George is so practical.) \xe2\x80\x9cNow for breakfast\r\nwe shall want a frying-pan\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94(Harris said it was indigestible; but we\r\nmerely urged him not to be an ass, and George went on)\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9ca tea-pot and a\r\nkettle, and a methylated spirit stove.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo oil,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, with a significant look; and Harris and I agreed.\r\n\r\nWe had taken up an oil-stove once, but \xe2\x80\x9cnever again.\xe2\x80\x9d It had been like\r\nliving in an oil-shop that week. It oozed. I never saw such a thing as\r\nparaffine oil is to ooze. We kept it in the nose of the boat, and, from\r\nthere, it oozed down to the rudder, impregnating the whole boat and\r\neverything in it on its way, and it oozed over the river, and saturated\r\nthe scenery and spoilt the atmosphere. Sometimes a westerly oily wind\r\nblew, and at other times an easterly oily wind, and sometimes it blew\r\na northerly oily wind, and maybe a southerly oily wind; but whether it\r\ncame from the Arctic snows, or was raised in the waste of the desert\r\nsands, it came alike to us laden with the fragrance of paraffine oil.\r\n\r\nAnd that oil oozed up and ruined the sunset; and as for the moonbeams,\r\nthey positively reeked of paraffine.\r\n\r\nWe tried to get away from it at Marlow. We left the boat by the bridge,\r\nand took a walk through the town to escape it, but it followed us. The\r\nwhole town was full of oil. We passed through the church-yard, and it\r\nseemed as if the people had been buried in oil. The High Street stunk\r\nof oil; we wondered how people could live in it. And we walked miles\r\nupon miles out Birmingham way; but it was no use, the country was\r\nsteeped in oil.\r\n\r\nAt the end of that trip we met together at midnight in a lonely field,\r\nunder a blasted oak, and took an awful oath (we had been swearing for a\r\nwhole week about the thing in an ordinary, middle-class way, but this\r\nwas a swell affair)\xe2\x80\x94an awful oath never to take paraffine oil with us\r\nin a boat again-except, of course, in case of sickness.\r\n\r\nTherefore, in the present instance, we confined ourselves to methylated\r\nspirit. Even that is bad enough. You get methylated pie and methylated\r\ncake. But methylated spirit is more wholesome when taken into the\r\nsystem in large quantities than paraffine oil.\r\n\r\nFor other breakfast things, George suggested eggs and bacon, which were\r\neasy to cook, cold meat, tea, bread and butter, and jam. For lunch, he\r\nsaid, we could have biscuits, cold meat, bread and butter, and jam\xe2\x80\x94but\r\n_no cheese_. Cheese, like oil, makes too much of itself. It wants the\r\nwhole boat to itself. It goes through the hamper, and gives a cheesy\r\nflavour to everything else there. You can\xe2\x80\x99t tell whether you are eating\r\napple-pie or German sausage, or strawberries and cream. It all seems\r\ncheese. There is too much odour about cheese.\r\n\r\nI remember a friend of mine, buying a couple of cheeses at Liverpool.\r\nSplendid cheeses they were, ripe and mellow, and with a two hundred\r\nhorse-power scent about them that might have been warranted to carry\r\nthree miles, and knock a man over at two hundred yards. I was in\r\nLiverpool at the time, and my friend said that if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mind he\r\nwould get me to take them back with me to London, as he should not be\r\ncoming up for a day or two himself, and he did not think the cheeses\r\nought to be kept much longer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, with pleasure, dear boy,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied, \xe2\x80\x9cwith pleasure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI called for the cheeses, and took them away in a cab. It was a\r\nramshackle affair, dragged along by a knock-kneed, broken-winded\r\nsomnambulist, which his owner, in a moment of enthusiasm, during\r\nconversation, referred to as a horse. I put the cheeses on the top,\r\nand we started off at a shamble that would have done credit to the\r\nswiftest steam-roller ever built, and all went merry as a funeral\r\nbell, until we turned the corner. There, the wind carried a whiff from\r\nthe cheeses full on to our steed. It woke him up, and, with a snort\r\nof terror, he dashed off at three miles an hour. The wind still blew\r\nin his direction, and before we reached the end of the street he was\r\nlaying himself out at the rate of nearly four miles an hour, leaving\r\nthe cripples and stout old ladies simply nowhere.\r\n\r\nIt took two porters as well as the driver to hold him in at the\r\nstation; and I do not think they would have done it, even then, had not\r\none of the men had the presence of mind to put a handkerchief over his\r\nnose, and to light a bit of brown paper.\r\n\r\nI took my ticket, and marched proudly up the platform, with my cheeses,\r\nthe people falling back respectfully on either side. The train was\r\ncrowded, and I had to get into a carriage where there were already\r\nseven other people. One crusty old gentleman objected, but I got in,\r\nnotwithstanding; and, putting my cheeses upon the rack, squeezed down\r\nwith a pleasant smile, and said it was a warm day.\r\n\r\nA few moments passed, and then the old gentleman began to fidget.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery close in here,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite oppressive,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man next him.\r\n\r\nAnd then they both began sniffing, and, at the third sniff, they\r\ncaught it right on the chest, and rose up without another word and\r\nwent out. And then a stout lady got up, and said it was disgraceful\r\nthat a respectable married woman should be harried about in this way,\r\nand gathered up a bag and eight parcels and went. The remaining four\r\npassengers sat on for a while, until a solemn-looking man in the\r\ncorner, who, from his dress and general appearance, seemed to belong\r\nto the undertaker class, said it put him in mind of dead baby; and the\r\nother three passengers tried to get out of the door at the same time,\r\nand hurt themselves.\r\n\r\n                       [Picture: Railway carriage]\r\n\r\nI smiled at the black gentleman, and said I thought we were going to\r\nhave the carriage to ourselves; and he laughed pleasantly, and said\r\nthat some people made such a fuss over a little thing. But even he\r\ngrew strangely depressed after we had started, and so, when we reached\r\nCrewe, I asked him to come and have a drink. He accepted, and we forced\r\nour way into the buffet, where we yelled, and stamped, and waved our\r\numbrellas for a quarter of an hour; and then a young lady came, and\r\nasked us if we wanted anything.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s yours?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, turning to my friend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll have half-a-crown\xe2\x80\x99s worth of brandy, neat, if you please, miss,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe responded.\r\n\r\nAnd he went off quietly after he had drunk it and got into another\r\ncarriage, which I thought mean.\r\n\r\nFrom Crewe I had the compartment to myself, though the train was\r\ncrowded. As we drew up at the different stations, the people, seeing\r\nmy empty carriage, would rush for it. \xe2\x80\x9cHere y\xe2\x80\x99 are, Maria; come along,\r\nplenty of room.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cAll right, Tom; we\xe2\x80\x99ll get in here,\xe2\x80\x9d they would shout.\r\nAnd they would run along, carrying heavy bags, and fight round the\r\ndoor to get in first. And one would open the door and mount the steps,\r\nand stagger back into the arms of the man behind him; and they would\r\nall come and have a sniff, and then droop off and squeeze into other\r\ncarriages, or pay the difference and go first.\r\n\r\nFrom Euston, I took the cheeses down to my friend\xe2\x80\x99s house. When his\r\nwife came into the room she smelt round for an instant. Then she said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it? Tell me the worst.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s cheeses. Tom bought them in Liverpool, and asked me to bring them\r\nup with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd I added that I hoped she understood that it had nothing to do with\r\nme; and she said that she was sure of that, but that she would speak to\r\nTom about it when he came back.\r\n\r\nMy friend was detained in Liverpool longer than he expected; and, three\r\ndays later, as he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t returned home, his wife called on me. She said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did Tom say about those cheeses?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI replied that he had directed they were to be kept in a moist place,\r\nand that nobody was to touch them.\r\n\r\nShe said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody\xe2\x80\x99s likely to touch them. Had he smelt them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI thought he had, and added that he seemed greatly attached to them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think he would be upset,\xe2\x80\x9d she queried, \xe2\x80\x9cif I gave a man a\r\nsovereign to take them away and bury them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI answered that I thought he would never smile again.\r\n\r\nAn idea struck her. She said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mind keeping them for him? Let me send them round to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadam,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied, \xe2\x80\x9cfor myself I like the smell of cheese, and the\r\njourney the other day with them from Liverpool I shall ever look back\r\nupon as a happy ending to a pleasant holiday. But, in this world, we\r\nmust consider others. The lady under whose roof I have the honour of\r\nresiding is a widow, and, for all I know, possibly an orphan too. She\r\nhas a strong, I may say an eloquent, objection to being what she terms\r\n\xe2\x80\x98put upon.\xe2\x80\x99 The presence of your husband\xe2\x80\x99s cheeses in her house she\r\nwould, I instinctively feel, regard as a \xe2\x80\x98put upon\xe2\x80\x99; and it shall never\r\nbe said that I put upon the widow and the orphan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said my friend\xe2\x80\x99s wife, rising, \xe2\x80\x9call I have to say\r\nis, that I shall take the children and go to an hotel until those\r\ncheeses are eaten. I decline to live any longer in the same house with\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe kept her word, leaving the place in charge of the charwoman, who,\r\nwhen asked if she could stand the smell, replied, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat smell?\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nwho, when taken close to the cheeses and told to sniff hard, said she\r\ncould detect a faint odour of melons. It was argued from this that\r\nlittle injury could result to the woman from the atmosphere, and she\r\nwas left.\r\n\r\nThe hotel bill came to fifteen guineas; and my friend, after reckoning\r\neverything up, found that the cheeses had cost him eight-and-sixpence\r\na pound. He said he dearly loved a bit of cheese, but it was beyond\r\nhis means; so he determined to get rid of them. He threw them into the\r\ncanal; but had to fish them out again, as the bargemen complained.\r\nThey said it made them feel quite faint. And, after that, he took them\r\none dark night and left them in the parish mortuary. But the coroner\r\ndiscovered them, and made a fearful fuss.\r\n\r\nHe said it was a plot to deprive him of his living by waking up the\r\ncorpses.\r\n\r\nMy friend got rid of them, at last, by taking them down to a sea-side\r\ntown, and burying them on the beach. It gained the place quite a\r\nreputation. Visitors said they had never noticed before how strong the\r\nair was, and weak-chested and consumptive people used to throng there\r\nfor years afterwards.\r\n\r\nFond as I am of cheese, therefore, I hold that George was right in\r\ndeclining to take any.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shan\xe2\x80\x99t want any tea,\xe2\x80\x9d said George (Harris\xe2\x80\x99s face fell at this);\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut we\xe2\x80\x99ll have a good round, square, slap-up meal at seven\xe2\x80\x94dinner,\r\ntea, and supper combined.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHarris grew more cheerful. George suggested meat and fruit pies,\r\ncold meat, tomatoes, fruit, and green stuff. For drink, we took some\r\nwonderful sticky concoction of Harris\xe2\x80\x99s, which you mixed with water and\r\ncalled lemonade, plenty of tea, and a bottle of whisky, in case, as\r\nGeorge said, we got upset.\r\n\r\nIt seemed to me that George harped too much on the getting-upset idea.\r\nIt seemed to me the wrong spirit to go about the trip in.\r\n\r\nBut I\xe2\x80\x99m glad we took the whisky.\r\n\r\nWe didn\xe2\x80\x99t take beer or wine. They are a mistake up the river. They make\r\nyou feel sleepy and heavy. A glass in the evening when you are doing a\r\nmouch round the town and looking at the girls is all right enough; but\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t drink when the sun is blazing down on your head, and you\xe2\x80\x99ve got\r\nhard work to do.\r\n\r\nWe made a list of the things to be taken, and a pretty lengthy one it\r\nwas, before we parted that evening. The next day, which was Friday, we\r\ngot them all together, and met in the evening to pack. We got a big\r\nGladstone for the clothes, and a couple of hampers for the victuals and\r\nthe cooking utensils. We moved the table up against the window, piled\r\neverything in a heap in the middle of the floor, and sat round and\r\nlooked at it.\r\n\r\nI said I\xe2\x80\x99d pack.\r\n\r\nI rather pride myself on my packing. Packing is one of those many\r\nthings that I feel I know more about than any other person living. (It\r\nsurprises me myself, sometimes, how many of these subjects there are.)\r\nI impressed the fact upon George and Harris, and told them that they\r\nhad better leave the whole matter entirely to me. They fell into the\r\nsuggestion with a readiness that had something uncanny about it. George\r\nput on a pipe and spread himself over the easy-chair, and Harris cocked\r\nhis legs on the table and lit a cigar.\r\n\r\nThis was hardly what I intended. What I had meant, of course, was, that\r\nI should boss the job, and that Harris and George should potter about\r\nunder my directions, I pushing them aside every now and then with, \xe2\x80\x9cOh,\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94!\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cHere, let me do it.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cThere you are, simple enough!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94really\r\nteaching them, as you might say. Their taking it in the way they did\r\nirritated me. There is nothing does irritate me more than seeing other\r\npeople sitting about doing nothing when I\xe2\x80\x99m working.\r\n\r\nI lived with a man once who used to make me mad that way. He would loll\r\non the sofa and watch me doing things by the hour together, following\r\nme round the room with his eyes, wherever I went. He said it did him\r\nreal good to look on at me, messing about. He said it made him feel\r\nthat life was not an idle dream to be gaped and yawned through, but a\r\nnoble task, full of duty and stern work. He said he often wondered now\r\nhow he could have gone on before he met me, never having anybody to\r\nlook at while they worked.\r\n\r\nNow, I\xe2\x80\x99m not like that. I can\xe2\x80\x99t sit still and see another man slaving\r\nand working. I want to get up and superintend, and walk round with\r\nmy hands in my pockets, and tell him what to do. It is my energetic\r\nnature. I can\xe2\x80\x99t help it.\r\n\r\nHowever, I did not say anything, but started the packing. It seemed a\r\nlonger job than I had thought it was going to be; but I got the bag\r\nfinished at last, and I sat on it and strapped it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAin\xe2\x80\x99t you going to put the boots in?\xe2\x80\x9d said Harris.\r\n\r\nAnd I looked round, and found I had forgotten them. That\xe2\x80\x99s just like\r\nHarris. He couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have said a word until I\xe2\x80\x99d got the bag shut and\r\nstrapped, of course. And George laughed\xe2\x80\x94one of those irritating,\r\nsenseless, chuckle-headed, crack-jawed laughs of his. They do make me\r\nso wild.\r\n\r\nI opened the bag and packed the boots in; and then, just as I was\r\ngoing to close it, a horrible idea occurred to me. Had I packed my\r\ntooth-brush? I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how it is, but I never do know whether I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\npacked my tooth-brush.\r\n\r\nMy tooth-brush is a thing that haunts me when I\xe2\x80\x99m travelling, and\r\nmakes my life a misery. I dream that I haven\xe2\x80\x99t packed it, and wake up\r\nin a cold perspiration, and get out of bed and hunt for it. And, in\r\nthe morning, I pack it before I have used it, and have to unpack again\r\nto get it, and it is always the last thing I turn out of the bag; and\r\nthen I repack and forget it, and have to rush upstairs for it at the\r\nlast moment and carry it to the railway station, wrapped up in my\r\npocket-handkerchief.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Boot] Of course I had to turn every mortal thing out now,\r\nand, of course, I could not find it. I rummaged the things up into much\r\nthe same state that they must have been before the world was created,\r\nand when chaos reigned. Of course, I found George\xe2\x80\x99s and Harris\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neighteen times over, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t find my own. I put the things back\r\none by one, and held everything up and shook it. Then I found it inside\r\na boot. I repacked once more.\r\n\r\nWhen I had finished, George asked if the soap was in. I said I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncare a hang whether the soap was in or whether it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t; and I\r\nslammed the bag to and strapped it, and found that I had packed my\r\ntobacco-pouch in it, and had to re-open it. It got shut up finally at\r\n10.05 p.m., and then there remained the hampers to do. Harris said that\r\nwe should be wanting to start in less than twelve hours\xe2\x80\x99 time, and\r\nthought that he and George had better do the rest; and I agreed and sat\r\ndown, and they had a go.\r\n\r\nThey began in a light-hearted spirit, evidently intending to show me\r\nhow to do it. I made no comment; I only waited. When George is hanged,\r\nHarris will be the worst packer in this world; and I looked at the\r\npiles of plates and cups, and kettles, and bottles and jars, and pies,\r\nand stoves, and cakes, and tomatoes, &c., and felt that the thing would\r\nsoon become exciting.\r\n\r\nIt did. They started with breaking a cup. That was the first thing they\r\ndid. They did that just to show you what they _could_ do, and to get\r\nyou interested.\r\n\r\nThen Harris packed the strawberry jam on top of a tomato and squashed\r\nit, and they had to pick out the tomato with a teaspoon.\r\n\r\nAnd then it was George\xe2\x80\x99s turn, and he trod on the butter. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t say\r\nanything, but I came over and sat on the edge of the table and watched\r\nthem. It irritated them more than anything I could have said. I felt\r\nthat. It made them nervous and excited, and they stepped on things, and\r\nput things behind them, and then couldn\xe2\x80\x99t find them when they wanted\r\nthem; and they packed the pies at the bottom, and put heavy things on\r\ntop, and smashed the pies in.\r\n\r\nThey upset salt over everything, and as for the butter! I never saw two\r\nmen do more with one-and-twopence worth of butter in my whole life than\r\nthey did. After George had got it off his slipper, they tried to put it\r\nin the kettle. It wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go in, and what _was_ in wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t come out.\r\nThey did scrape it out at last, and put it down on a chair, and Harris\r\nsat on it, and it stuck to him, and they went looking for it all over\r\nthe room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll take my oath I put it down on that chair,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, staring\r\nat the empty seat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw you do it myself, not a minute ago,\xe2\x80\x9d said Harris.\r\n\r\nThen they started round the room again looking for it; and then they\r\nmet again in the centre, and stared at one another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost extraordinary thing I ever heard of,\xe2\x80\x9d said George.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo mysterious!\xe2\x80\x9d said Harris.\r\n\r\nThen George got round at the back of Harris and saw it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, here it is all the time,\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, indignantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Harris, spinning round.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStand still, can\xe2\x80\x99t you!\xe2\x80\x9d roared George, flying after him.\r\n\r\nAnd they got it off, and packed it in the teapot.\r\n\r\nMontmorency was in it all, of course. Montmorency\xe2\x80\x99s ambition in life,\r\nis to get in the way and be sworn at. If he can squirm in anywhere\r\nwhere he particularly is not wanted, and be a perfect nuisance, and\r\nmake people mad, and have things thrown at his head, then he feels his\r\nday has not been wasted.\r\n\r\nTo get somebody to stumble over him, and curse him steadily for an\r\nhour, is his highest aim and object; and, when he has succeeded in\r\naccomplishing this, his conceit becomes quite unbearable.\r\n\r\nHe came and sat down on things, just when they were wanted to be\r\npacked; and he laboured under the fixed belief that, whenever Harris\r\nor George reached out their hand for anything, it was his cold, damp\r\nnose that they wanted. He put his leg into the jam, and he worried the\r\nteaspoons, and he pretended that the lemons were rats, and got into the\r\nhamper and killed three of them before Harris could land him with the\r\nfrying-pan.\r\n\r\nHarris said I encouraged him. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t encourage him. A dog like that\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t want any encouragement. It\xe2\x80\x99s the natural, original sin that is\r\nborn in him that makes him do things like that.\r\n\r\nThe packing was done at 12.50; and Harris sat on the big hamper, and\r\nsaid he hoped nothing would be found broken. George said that if\r\nanything was broken it was broken, which reflection seemed to comfort\r\nhim. He also said he was ready for bed. We were all ready for bed.\r\nHarris was to sleep with us that night, and we went upstairs.\r\n\r\nWe tossed for beds, and Harris had to sleep with me. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you prefer the inside or the outside, J.?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI said I generally preferred to sleep _inside_ a bed.\r\n\r\nHarris said it was old.\r\n\r\nGeorge said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat time shall I wake you fellows?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHarris said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeven.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94six,\xe2\x80\x9d because I wanted to write some letters.\r\n\r\nHarris and I had a bit of a row over it, but at last split the\r\ndifference, and said half-past six.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWake us at 6.30, George,\xe2\x80\x9d we said.\r\n\r\nGeorge made no answer, and we found, on going over, that he had been\r\nasleep for some time; so we placed the bath where he could tumble into\r\nit on getting out in the morning, and went to bed ourselves.\r\n\r\n                    [Picture: Luggage with dog on top]\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V.\r\n\r\n\r\nMrs. P. arouses us.\xe2\x80\x94George, the sluggard.\xe2\x80\x94The \xe2\x80\x9cweather forecast\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nswindle.\xe2\x80\x94Our luggage.\xe2\x80\x94Depravity of the small boy.\xe2\x80\x94The people\r\ngather round us.\xe2\x80\x94We drive off in great style, and arrive at\r\nWaterloo.\xe2\x80\x94Innocence of South Western Officials concerning such worldly\r\nthings as trains.\xe2\x80\x94We are afloat, afloat in an open boat.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Mrs. Poppets] It was Mrs. Poppets that woke me up next\r\nmorning.\r\n\r\nShe said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know that it\xe2\x80\x99s nearly nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNine o\xe2\x80\x99 what?\xe2\x80\x9d I cried, starting up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNine o\xe2\x80\x99clock,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied, through the keyhole. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought you was\r\na-oversleeping yourselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI woke Harris, and told him. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you wanted to get up at six?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I did,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered; \xe2\x80\x9cwhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t you wake me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could I wake you, when you didn\xe2\x80\x99t wake me?\xe2\x80\x9d he retorted. \xe2\x80\x9cNow\r\nwe shan\xe2\x80\x99t get on the water till after twelve. I wonder you take the\r\ntrouble to get up at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUm,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied, \xe2\x80\x9clucky for you that I do. If I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t woke you, you\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nhave lain there for the whole fortnight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Picture: George snoring] We snarled at one another in this strain for\r\nthe next few minutes, when we were interrupted by a defiant snore from\r\nGeorge. It reminded us, for the first time since our being called, of\r\nhis existence. There he lay\xe2\x80\x94the man who had wanted to know what time\r\nhe should wake us\xe2\x80\x94on his back, with his mouth wide open, and his knees\r\nstuck up.\r\n\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know why it should be, I am sure; but the sight of another man\r\nasleep in bed when I am up, maddens me. It seems to me so shocking to\r\nsee the precious hours of a man\xe2\x80\x99s life\xe2\x80\x94the priceless moments that will\r\nnever come back to him again\xe2\x80\x94being wasted in mere brutish sleep.\r\n\r\nThere was George, throwing away in hideous sloth the inestimable gift\r\nof time; his valuable life, every second of which he would have to\r\naccount for hereafter, passing away from him, unused. He might have\r\nbeen up stuffing himself with eggs and bacon, irritating the dog,\r\nor flirting with the slavey, instead of sprawling there, sunk in\r\nsoul-clogging oblivion.\r\n\r\nIt was a terrible thought. Harris and I appeared to be struck by it\r\nat the same instant. We determined to save him, and, in this noble\r\nresolve, our own dispute was forgotten. We flew across and slung the\r\nclothes off him, and Harris landed him one with a slipper, and I\r\nshouted in his ear, and he awoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWasermarrer?\xe2\x80\x9d he observed, sitting up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet up, you fat-headed chunk!\xe2\x80\x9d roared Harris. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s quarter to ten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat!\xe2\x80\x9d he shrieked, jumping out of bed into the bath; \xe2\x80\x9cWho the thunder\r\nput this thing here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe told him he must have been a fool not to see the bath.\r\n\r\nWe finished dressing, and, when it came to the extras, we remembered\r\nthat we had packed the tooth-brushes and the brush and comb (that\r\ntooth-brush of mine will be the death of me, I know), and we had to go\r\ndownstairs, and fish them out of the bag. And when we had done that\r\nGeorge wanted the shaving tackle.
