We told him that he would have to go\r\nwithout shaving that morning, as we weren\xe2\x80\x99t going to unpack that bag\r\nagain for him, nor for anyone like him.\r\n\r\nHe said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be absurd. How can I go into the City like this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was certainly rather rough on the City, but what cared we for human\r\nsuffering? As Harris said, in his common, vulgar way, the City would\r\nhave to lump it.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Two dogs and umbrella] We went downstairs to breakfast.\r\nMontmorency had invited two other dogs to come and see him off, and\r\nthey were whiling away the time by fighting on the doorstep. We calmed\r\nthem with an umbrella, and sat down to chops and cold beef.\r\n\r\nHarris said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe great thing is to make a good breakfast,\xe2\x80\x9d and he started with a\r\ncouple of chops, saying that he would take these while they were hot,\r\nas the beef could wait.\r\n\r\nGeorge got hold of the paper, and read us out the boating fatalities,\r\nand the weather forecast, which latter prophesied \xe2\x80\x9crain, cold, wet\r\nto fine\xe2\x80\x9d (whatever more than usually ghastly thing in weather that\r\nmay be), \xe2\x80\x9coccasional local thunder-storms, east wind, with general\r\ndepression over the Midland Counties (London and Channel). Bar.\r\nfalling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI do think that, of all the silly, irritating tomfoolishness by which\r\nwe are plagued, this \xe2\x80\x9cweather-forecast\xe2\x80\x9d fraud is about the most\r\naggravating. It \xe2\x80\x9cforecasts\xe2\x80\x9d precisely what happened yesterday or the\r\nday before, and precisely the opposite of what is going to happen\r\nto-day.\r\n\r\nI remember a holiday of mine being completely ruined one late autumn\r\nby our paying attention to the weather report of the local newspaper.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeavy showers, with thunderstorms, may be expected to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d it would\r\nsay on Monday, and so we would give up our picnic, and stop indoors\r\nall day, waiting for the rain.\xe2\x80\x94And people would pass the house, going\r\noff in wagonettes and coaches as jolly and merry as could be, the sun\r\nshining out, and not a cloud to be seen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d we said, as we stood looking out at them through the window,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwon\xe2\x80\x99t they come home soaked!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd we chuckled to think how wet they were going to get, and came back\r\nand stirred the fire, and got our books, and arranged our specimens of\r\nseaweed and cockle shells. By twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock, with the sun pouring into\r\nthe room, the heat became quite oppressive, and we wondered when those\r\nheavy showers and occasional thunderstorms were going to begin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! they\xe2\x80\x99ll come in the afternoon, you\xe2\x80\x99ll find,\xe2\x80\x9d we said to each\r\nother. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, _won\xe2\x80\x99t_ those people get wet. What a lark!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt one o\xe2\x80\x99clock, the landlady would come in to ask if we weren\xe2\x80\x99t going\r\nout, as it seemed such a lovely day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x9d we replied, with a knowing chuckle, \xe2\x80\x9cnot we. _We_ don\xe2\x80\x99t mean\r\nto get wet\xe2\x80\x94no, no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd when the afternoon was nearly gone, and still there was no sign of\r\nrain, we tried to cheer ourselves up with the idea that it would come\r\ndown all at once, just as the people had started for home, and were out\r\nof the reach of any shelter, and that they would thus get more drenched\r\nthan ever. But not a drop ever fell, and it finished a grand day, and a\r\nlovely night after it.\r\n\r\nThe next morning we would read that it was going to be a \xe2\x80\x9cwarm, fine\r\nto set-fair day; much heat;\xe2\x80\x9d and we would dress ourselves in flimsy\r\nthings, and go out, and, half-an-hour after we had started, it would\r\ncommence to rain hard, and a bitterly cold wind would spring up, and\r\nboth would keep on steadily for the whole day, and we would come home\r\nwith colds and rheumatism all over us, and go to bed.\r\n\r\nThe weather is a thing that is beyond me altogether. I never can\r\nunderstand it. The barometer is useless: it is as misleading as the\r\nnewspaper forecast.\r\n\r\nThere was one hanging up in a hotel at Oxford at which I was staying\r\nlast spring, and, when I got there, it was pointing to \xe2\x80\x9cset fair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nIt was simply pouring with rain outside, and had been all day; and I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t quite make matters out. I tapped the barometer, and it jumped\r\nup and pointed to \xe2\x80\x9cvery dry.\xe2\x80\x9d The Boots stopped as he was passing,\r\nand said he expected it meant to-morrow. I fancied that maybe it was\r\nthinking of the week before last, but Boots said, No, he thought not.\r\n\r\nI tapped it again the next morning, and it went up still higher, and\r\nthe rain came down faster than ever. On Wednesday I went and hit it\r\nagain, and the pointer went round towards \xe2\x80\x9cset fair,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cvery dry,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cmuch heat,\xe2\x80\x9d until it was stopped by the peg, and couldn\xe2\x80\x99t go any\r\nfurther. It tried its best, but the instrument was built so that it\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t prophesy fine weather any harder than it did without breaking\r\nitself. It evidently wanted to go on, and prognosticate drought, and\r\nwater famine, and sunstroke, and simooms, and such things, but the\r\npeg prevented it, and it had to be content with pointing to the mere\r\ncommonplace \xe2\x80\x9cvery dry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMeanwhile, the rain came down in a steady torrent, and the lower part\r\nof the town was under water, owing to the river having overflowed.\r\n\r\nBoots said it was evident that we were going to have a prolonged spell\r\nof grand weather _some time_, and read out a poem which was printed\r\nover the top of the oracle, about\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cLong foretold, long last;\r\n    Short notice, soon past.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe fine weather never came that summer. I expect that machine must\r\nhave been referring to the following spring.\r\n\r\nThen there are those new style of barometers, the long straight ones.\r\nI never can make head or tail of those. There is one side for 10 a.m.\r\nyesterday, and one side for 10 a.m. to-day; but you can\xe2\x80\x99t always get\r\nthere as early as ten, you know. It rises or falls for rain and fine,\r\nwith much or less wind, and one end is \xe2\x80\x9cNly\xe2\x80\x9d and the other \xe2\x80\x9cEly\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(what\xe2\x80\x99s Ely got to do with it?), and if you tap it, it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t tell you\r\nanything. And you\xe2\x80\x99ve got to correct it to sea-level, and reduce it to\r\nFahrenheit, and even then I don\xe2\x80\x99t know the answer.\r\n\r\nBut who wants to be foretold the weather? It is bad enough when it\r\ncomes, without our having the misery of knowing about it beforehand.\r\nThe prophet we like is the old man who, on the particularly\r\ngloomy-looking morning of some day when we particularly want it to be\r\nfine, looks round the horizon with a particularly knowing eye, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, sir, I think it will clear up all right. It will break all\r\nright enough, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, he knows\xe2\x80\x9d, we say, as we wish him good-morning, and start off;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwonderful how these old fellows can tell!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd we feel an affection for that man which is not at all lessened by\r\nthe circumstances of its _not_ clearing up, but continuing to rain\r\nsteadily all day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, well,\xe2\x80\x9d we feel, \xe2\x80\x9che did his best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor the man that prophesies us bad weather, on the contrary, we\r\nentertain only bitter and revengeful thoughts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoing to clear up, d\xe2\x80\x99ye think?\xe2\x80\x9d we shout, cheerily, as we pass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, no, sir; I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid it\xe2\x80\x99s settled down for the day,\xe2\x80\x9d he replies,\r\nshaking his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStupid old fool!\xe2\x80\x9d we mutter, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat\xe2\x80\x99s _he_ know about it?\xe2\x80\x9d And, if\r\nhis portent proves correct, we come back feeling still more angry\r\nagainst him, and with a vague notion that, somehow or other, he has had\r\nsomething to do with it.\r\n\r\nIt was too bright and sunny on this especial morning for George\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nblood-curdling readings about \xe2\x80\x9cBar. falling,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9catmospheric disturbance,\r\npassing in an oblique line over Southern Europe,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cpressure\r\nincreasing,\xe2\x80\x9d to very much upset us: and so, finding that he could\r\nnot make us wretched, and was only wasting his time, he sneaked the\r\ncigarette that I had carefully rolled up for myself, and went.\r\n\r\nThen Harris and I, having finished up the few things left on the table,\r\ncarted out our luggage on to the doorstep, and waited for a cab.\r\n\r\n                          [Picture: The luggage]\r\n\r\nThere seemed a good deal of luggage, when we put it all together. There\r\nwas the Gladstone and the small hand-bag, and the two hampers, and a\r\nlarge roll of rugs, and some four or five overcoats and macintoshes,\r\nand a few umbrellas, and then there was a melon by itself in a bag,\r\nbecause it was too bulky to go in anywhere, and a couple of pounds of\r\ngrapes in another bag, and a Japanese paper umbrella, and a frying pan,\r\nwhich, being too long to pack, we had wrapped round with brown paper.\r\n\r\nIt did look a lot, and Harris and I began to feel rather ashamed of it,\r\nthough why we should be, I can\xe2\x80\x99t see. No cab came by, but the street\r\nboys did, and got interested in the show, apparently, and stopped.\r\n\r\nBiggs\xe2\x80\x99s boy was the first to come round. Biggs is our greengrocer, and\r\nhis chief talent lies in securing the services of the most abandoned\r\nand unprincipled errand-boys that civilisation has as yet produced. If\r\nanything more than usually villainous in the boy-line crops up in our\r\nneighbourhood, we know that it is Biggs\xe2\x80\x99s latest. I was told that, at\r\nthe time of the Great Coram Street murder, it was promptly concluded by\r\nour street that Biggs\xe2\x80\x99s boy (for that period) was at the bottom of it,\r\nand had he not been able, in reply to the severe cross-examination to\r\nwhich he was subjected by No. 19, when he called there for orders the\r\nmorning after the crime (assisted by No. 21, who happened to be on the\r\nstep at the time), to prove a complete _alibi_, it would have gone hard\r\nwith him. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know Biggs\xe2\x80\x99s boy at that time, but, from what I have\r\nseen of them since, I should not have attached much importance to that\r\n_alibi_ myself.\r\n\r\nBiggs\xe2\x80\x99s boy, as I have said, came round the corner. He was evidently in\r\na great hurry when he first dawned upon the vision, but, on catching\r\nsight of Harris and me, and Montmorency, and the things, he eased up\r\nand stared. Harris and I frowned at him. This might have wounded a\r\nmore sensitive nature, but Biggs\xe2\x80\x99s boys are not, as a rule, touchy.\r\nHe came to a dead stop, a yard from our step, and, leaning up against\r\nthe railings, and selecting a straw to chew, fixed us with his eye. He\r\nevidently meant to see this thing out.\r\n\r\nIn another moment, the grocer\xe2\x80\x99s boy passed on the opposite side of the\r\nstreet. Biggs\xe2\x80\x99s boy hailed him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHi! ground floor o\xe2\x80\x99 42\xe2\x80\x99s a-moving.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe grocer\xe2\x80\x99s boy came across, and took up a position on the other side\r\nof the step. Then the young gentleman from the boot-shop stopped, and\r\njoined Biggs\xe2\x80\x99s boy; while the empty-can superintendent from \xe2\x80\x9cThe Blue\r\nPosts\xe2\x80\x9d took up an independent position on the curb.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-going to starve, are they?\xe2\x80\x9d said the gentleman from the\r\nboot-shop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! you\xe2\x80\x99d want to take a thing or two with _you_,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted \xe2\x80\x9cThe Blue\r\nPosts,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cif you was a-going to cross the Atlantic in a small boat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-going to cross the Atlantic,\xe2\x80\x9d struck in Biggs\xe2\x80\x99s boy;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthey\xe2\x80\x99re a-going to find Stanley.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy this time, quite a small crowd had collected, and people were asking\r\neach other what was the matter. One party (the young and giddy portion\r\nof the crowd) held that it was a wedding, and pointed out Harris as\r\nthe bridegroom; while the elder and more thoughtful among the populace\r\ninclined to the idea that it was a funeral, and that I was probably the\r\ncorpse\xe2\x80\x99s brother.\r\n\r\nAt last, an empty cab turned up (it is a street where, as a rule,\r\nand when they are not wanted, empty cabs pass at the rate of three a\r\nminute, and hang about, and get in your way), and packing ourselves\r\nand our belongings into it, and shooting out a couple of Montmorency\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfriends, who had evidently sworn never to forsake him, we drove away\r\namidst the cheers of the crowd, Biggs\xe2\x80\x99s boy shying a carrot after us\r\nfor luck.\r\n\r\nWe got to Waterloo at eleven, and asked where the eleven-five started\r\nfrom. Of course nobody knew; nobody at Waterloo ever does know where a\r\ntrain is going to start from, or where a train when it does start is\r\ngoing to, or anything about it. The porter who took our things thought\r\nit would go from number two platform, while another porter, with whom\r\nhe discussed the question, had heard a rumour that it would go from\r\nnumber one. The station-master, on the other hand, was convinced it\r\nwould start from the local.\r\n\r\nTo put an end to the matter, we went upstairs, and asked the traffic\r\nsuperintendent, and he told us that he had just met a man, who said he\r\nhad seen it at number three platform. We went to number three platform,\r\nbut the authorities there said that they rather thought that train was\r\nthe Southampton express, or else the Windsor loop. But they were sure\r\nit wasn\xe2\x80\x99t the Kingston train, though why they were sure it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t they\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say.\r\n\r\nThen our porter said he thought that must be it on the high-level\r\nplatform; said he thought he knew the train. So we went to the\r\nhigh-level platform, and saw the engine-driver, and asked him if he\r\nwas going to Kingston. He said he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t say for certain of course,\r\nbut that he rather thought he was. Anyhow, if he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t the 11.5 for\r\nKingston, he said he was pretty confident he was the 9.32 for Virginia\r\nWater, or the 10 a.m. express for the Isle of Wight, or somewhere in\r\nthat direction, and we should all know when we got there. We slipped\r\nhalf-a-crown into his hand, and begged him to be the 11.5 for Kingston.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody will ever know, on this line,\xe2\x80\x9d we said, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat you are, or\r\nwhere you\xe2\x80\x99re going. You know the way, you slip off quietly and go to\r\nKingston.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know, gents,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the noble fellow, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I suppose\r\n_some_ train\xe2\x80\x99s got to go to Kingston; and I\xe2\x80\x99ll do it. Gimme the\r\nhalf-crown.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus we got to Kingston by the London and South-Western Railway.\r\n\r\nWe learnt, afterwards, that the train we had come by was really the\r\nExeter mail, and that they had spent hours at Waterloo, looking for it,\r\nand nobody knew what had become of it.\r\n\r\nOur boat was waiting for us at Kingston just below bridge, and to it\r\nwe wended our way, and round it we stored our luggage, and into it we\r\nstepped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you all right, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d said the man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRight it is,\xe2\x80\x9d we answered; and with Harris at the sculls and I at the\r\ntiller-lines, and Montmorency, unhappy and deeply suspicious, in the\r\nprow, out we shot on to the waters which, for a fortnight, were to be\r\nour home.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI.\r\n\r\n\r\nKingston.\xe2\x80\x94Instructive remarks on early English history.\xe2\x80\x94Instructive\r\nobservations on carved oak and life in general.\xe2\x80\x94Sad case of Stivvings,\r\njunior.\xe2\x80\x94Musings on antiquity.\xe2\x80\x94I forget that I am steering.\xe2\x80\x94Interesting\r\nresult.\xe2\x80\x94Hampton Court Maze.\xe2\x80\x94Harris as a guide.\r\n\r\nIt was a glorious morning, late spring or early summer, as you care\r\nto take it, when the dainty sheen of grass and leaf is blushing to a\r\ndeeper green; and the year seems like a fair young maid, trembling with\r\nstrange, wakening pulses on the brink of womanhood.\r\n\r\nThe quaint back streets of Kingston, where they came down to the\r\nwater\xe2\x80\x99s edge, looked quite picturesque in the flashing sunlight, the\r\nglinting river with its drifting barges, the wooded towpath, the\r\ntrim-kept villas on the other side, Harris, in a red and orange blazer,\r\ngrunting away at the sculls, the distant glimpses of the grey old\r\npalace of the Tudors, all made a sunny picture, so bright but calm, so\r\nfull of life, and yet so peaceful, that, early in the day though it\r\nwas, I felt myself being dreamily lulled off into a musing fit.\r\n\r\nI mused on Kingston, or \xe2\x80\x9cKyningestun,\xe2\x80\x9d as it was once called in the\r\ndays when Saxon \xe2\x80\x9ckinges\xe2\x80\x9d were crowned there. Great C\xc3\xa6sar crossed the\r\nriver there, and the Roman legions camped upon its sloping uplands.\r\nC\xc3\xa6sar, like, in later years, Elizabeth, seems to have stopped\r\neverywhere: only he was more respectable than good Queen Bess; he\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t put up at the public-houses.\r\n\r\nShe was nuts on public-houses, was England\xe2\x80\x99s Virgin Queen. There\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nscarcely a pub. of any attractions within ten miles of London that she\r\ndoes not seem to have looked in at, or stopped at, or slept at, some\r\ntime or other. I wonder now, supposing Harris, say, turned over a new\r\nleaf, and became a great and good man, and got to be Prime Minister,\r\nand died, if they would put up signs over the public-houses that he had\r\npatronised: \xe2\x80\x9cHarris had a glass of bitter in this house;\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cHarris had\r\ntwo of Scotch cold here in the summer of \xe2\x80\x9988;\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cHarris was chucked from\r\nhere in December, 1886.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo, there would be too many of them! It would be the houses that he had\r\nnever entered that would become famous. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly house in South London\r\nthat Harris never had a drink in!\xe2\x80\x9d The people would flock to it to see\r\nwhat could have been the matter with it.\r\n\r\nHow poor weak-minded King Edwy must have hated Kyningestun! The\r\ncoronation feast had been too much for him. Maybe boar\xe2\x80\x99s head stuffed\r\nwith sugar-plums did not agree with him (it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t with me, I know),\r\nand he had had enough of sack and mead; so he slipped from the noisy\r\nrevel to steal a quiet moonlight hour with his beloved Elgiva.\r\n\r\nPerhaps, from the casement, standing hand-in-hand, they were watching\r\nthe calm moonlight on the river, while from the distant halls the\r\nboisterous revelry floated in broken bursts of faint-heard din and\r\ntumult.\r\n\r\nThen brutal Odo and St. Dunstan force their rude way into the quiet\r\nroom, and hurl coarse insults at the sweet-faced Queen, and drag poor\r\nEdwy back to the loud clamour of the drunken brawl.\r\n\r\nYears later, to the crash of battle-music, Saxon kings and Saxon\r\nrevelry were buried side by side, and Kingston\xe2\x80\x99s greatness passed away\r\nfor a time, to rise once more when Hampton Court became the palace of\r\nthe Tudors and the Stuarts, and the royal barges strained at their\r\nmoorings on the river\xe2\x80\x99s bank, and bright-cloaked gallants swaggered\r\ndown the water-steps to cry: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat Ferry, ho! Gadzooks, gramercy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMany of the old houses, round about, speak very plainly of those days\r\nwhen Kingston was a royal borough, and nobles and courtiers lived\r\nthere, near their King, and the long road to the palace gates was\r\ngay all day with clanking steel and prancing palfreys, and rustling\r\nsilks and velvets, and fair faces. The large and spacious houses, with\r\ntheir oriel, latticed windows, their huge fireplaces, and their gabled\r\nroofs, breathe of the days of hose and doublet, of pearl-embroidered\r\nstomachers, and complicated oaths. They were upraised in the days \xe2\x80\x9cwhen\r\nmen knew how to build.\xe2\x80\x9d The hard red bricks have only grown more firmly\r\nset with time, and their oak stairs do not creak and grunt when you try\r\nto go down them quietly.\r\n\r\nSpeaking of oak staircases reminds me that there is a magnificent\r\ncarved oak staircase in one of the houses in Kingston. It is a shop\r\nnow, in the market-place, but it was evidently once the mansion of some\r\ngreat personage. A friend of mine, who lives at Kingston, went in there\r\nto buy a hat one day, and, in a thoughtless moment, put his hand in his\r\npocket and paid for it then and there.\r\n\r\nThe shopman (he knows my friend) was naturally a little staggered at\r\nfirst; but, quickly recovering himself, and feeling that something\r\nought to be done to encourage this sort of thing, asked our hero if\r\nhe would like to see some fine old carved oak. My friend said he\r\nwould, and the shopman, thereupon, took him through the shop, and\r\nup the staircase of the house. The balusters were a superb piece of\r\nworkmanship, and the wall all the way up was oak-panelled, with carving\r\nthat would have done credit to a palace.\r\n\r\nFrom the stairs, they went into the drawing-room, which was a large,\r\nbright room, decorated with a somewhat startling though cheerful paper\r\nof a blue ground. There was nothing, however, remarkable about the\r\napartment, and my friend wondered why he had been brought there. The\r\nproprietor went up to the paper, and tapped it. It gave forth a wooden\r\nsound.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOak,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained. \xe2\x80\x9cAll carved oak, right up to the ceiling, just the\r\nsame as you saw on the staircase.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, great C\xc3\xa6sar! man,\xe2\x80\x9d expostulated my friend; \xe2\x80\x9cyou don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to say\r\nyou have covered over carved oak with blue wall-paper?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d was the reply: \xe2\x80\x9cit was expensive work. Had to match-board it all\r\nover first, of course. But the room looks cheerful now. It was awful\r\ngloomy before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI can\xe2\x80\x99t say I altogether blame the man (which is doubtless a great\r\nrelief to his mind). From his point of view, which would be that of the\r\naverage householder, desiring to take life as lightly as possible, and\r\nnot that of the old-curiosity-shop maniac, there is reason on his side.\r\nCarved oak is very pleasant to look at, and to have a little of, but it\r\nis no doubt somewhat depressing to live in, for those whose fancy does\r\nnot lie that way. It would be like living in a church.\r\n\r\nNo, what was sad in his case was that he, who didn\xe2\x80\x99t care for carved\r\noak, should have his drawing-room panelled with it, while people who\r\ndo care for it have to pay enormous prices to get it. It seems to be\r\nthe rule of this world. Each person has what he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t want, and other\r\npeople have what he does want.\r\n\r\nMarried men have wives, and don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to want them; and young single\r\nfellows cry out that they can\xe2\x80\x99t get them. Poor people who can hardly\r\nkeep themselves have eight hearty children. Rich old couples, with no\r\none to leave their money to, die childless.\r\n\r\nThen there are girls with lovers. The girls that have lovers never want\r\nthem. They say they would rather be without them, that they bother\r\nthem, and why don\xe2\x80\x99t they go and make love to Miss Smith and Miss Brown,\r\nwho are plain and elderly, and haven\xe2\x80\x99t got any lovers? They themselves\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t want lovers. They never mean to marry.\r\n\r\nIt does not do to dwell on these things; it makes one so sad.\r\n\r\nThere was a boy at our school, we used to call him Sandford and Merton.\r\nHis real name was Stivvings. He was the most extraordinary lad I ever\r\ncame across. I believe he really liked study. He used to get into\r\nawful rows for sitting up in bed and reading Greek; and as for French\r\nirregular verbs there was simply no keeping him away from them. He was\r\nfull of weird and unnatural notions about being a credit to his parents\r\nand an honour to the school; and he yearned to win prizes, and grow up\r\nand be a clever man, and had all those sorts of weak-minded ideas. I\r\nnever knew such a strange creature, yet harmless, mind you, as the babe\r\nunborn.\r\n\r\nWell, that boy used to get ill about twice a week, so that he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngo to school. There never was such a boy to get ill as that Sandford\r\nand Merton. If there was any known disease going within ten miles of\r\nhim, he had it, and had it badly. He would take bronchitis in the\r\ndog-days, and have hay-fever at Christmas. After a six weeks\xe2\x80\x99 period of\r\ndrought, he would be stricken down with rheumatic fever; and he would\r\ngo out in a November fog and come home with a sunstroke.\r\n\r\nThey put him under laughing-gas one year, poor lad, and drew all his\r\nteeth, and gave him a false set, because he suffered so terribly with\r\ntoothache; and then it turned to neuralgia and ear-ache. He was never\r\nwithout a cold, except once for nine weeks while he had scarlet fever;\r\nand he always had chilblains. During the great cholera scare of 1871,\r\nour neighbourhood was singularly free from it. There was only one\r\nreputed case in the whole parish: that case was young Stivvings.\r\n\r\nHe had to stop in bed when he was ill, and eat chicken and custards and\r\nhot-house grapes; and he would lie there and sob, because they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlet him do Latin exercises, and took his German grammar away from him.\r\n\r\nAnd we other boys, who would have sacrificed ten terms of our\r\nschool-life for the sake of being ill for a day, and had no desire\r\nwhatever to give our parents any excuse for being stuck-up about us,\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t catch so much as a stiff neck. We fooled about in draughts,\r\nand it did us good, and freshened us up; and we took things to make us\r\nsick, and they made us fat, and gave us an appetite. Nothing we could\r\nthink of seemed to make us ill until the holidays began. Then, on the\r\nbreaking-up day, we caught colds, and whooping cough, and all kinds of\r\ndisorders, which lasted till the term recommenced; when, in spite of\r\neverything we could man\xc5\x93uvre to the contrary, we would get suddenly\r\nwell again, and be better than ever.\r\n\r\nSuch is life; and we are but as grass that is cut down, and put into\r\nthe oven and baked.\r\n\r\nTo go back to the carved-oak question, they must have had\r\nvery fair notions of the artistic and the beautiful, our\r\ngreat-great-grandfathers. Why, all our art treasures of to-day are only\r\nthe dug-up commonplaces of three or four hundred years ago. I wonder\r\nif there is real intrinsic beauty in the old soup-plates, beer-mugs,\r\nand candle-snuffers that we prize so now, or if it is only the halo\r\nof age glowing around them that gives them their charms in our eyes.\r\nThe \xe2\x80\x9cold blue\xe2\x80\x9d that we hang about our walls as ornaments were the\r\ncommon every-day household utensils of a few centuries ago; and the\r\npink shepherds and the yellow shepherdesses that we hand round now for\r\nall our friends to gush over, and pretend they understand, were the\r\nunvalued mantel-ornaments that the mother of the eighteenth century\r\nwould have given the baby to suck when he cried.\r\n\r\nWill it be the same in the future? Will the prized treasures of to-day\r\nalways be the cheap trifles of the day before? Will rows of our\r\nwillow-pattern dinner-plates be ranged above the chimneypieces of the\r\ngreat in the years 2000 and odd? Will the white cups with the gold rim\r\nand the beautiful gold flower inside (species unknown), that our Sarah\r\nJanes now break in sheer light-heartedness of spirit, be carefully\r\nmended, and stood upon a bracket, and dusted only by the lady of the\r\nhouse?\r\n\r\n[Picture: China dog] That china dog that ornaments the bedroom of my\r\nfurnished lodgings. It is a white dog. Its eyes blue. Its nose is a\r\ndelicate red, with spots. Its head is painfully erect, its expression\r\nis amiability carried to verge of imbecility. I do not admire it\r\nmyself. Considered as a work of art, I may say it irritates me.\r\nThoughtless friends jeer at it, and even my landlady herself has no\r\nadmiration for it, and excuses its presence by the circumstance that\r\nher aunt gave it to her.\r\n\r\nBut in 200 years\xe2\x80\x99 time it is more than probable that that dog will\r\nbe dug up from somewhere or other, minus its legs, and with its tail\r\nbroken, and will be sold for old china, and put in a glass cabinet.\r\nAnd people will pass it round, and admire it. They will be struck by\r\nthe wonderful depth of the colour on the nose, and speculate as to how\r\nbeautiful the bit of the tail that is lost no doubt was.\r\n\r\nWe, in this age, do not see the beauty of that dog. We are too familiar\r\nwith it. It is like the sunset and the stars: we are not awed by their\r\nloveliness because they are common to our eyes. So it is with that\r\nchina dog. In 2288 people will gush over it. The making of such dogs\r\nwill have become a lost art. Our descendants will wonder how we did\r\nit, and say how clever we were. We shall be referred to lovingly as\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthose grand old artists that flourished in the nineteenth century, and\r\nproduced those china dogs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe \xe2\x80\x9csampler\xe2\x80\x9d that the eldest daughter did at school will be spoken\r\nof as \xe2\x80\x9ctapestry of the Victorian era,\xe2\x80\x9d and be almost priceless. The\r\nblue-and-white mugs of the present-day roadside inn will be hunted up,\r\nall cracked and chipped, and sold for their weight in gold, and rich\r\npeople will use them for claret cups; and travellers from Japan will\r\nbuy up all the \xe2\x80\x9cPresents from Ramsgate,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cSouvenirs of Margate,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nthat may have escaped destruction, and take them back to Jedo as\r\nancient English curios.\r\n\r\nAt this point Harris threw away the sculls, got up and left his seat,\r\nand sat on his back, and stuck his legs in the air. Montmorency howled,\r\nand turned a somersault, and the top hamper jumped up, and all the\r\nthings came out.\r\n\r\nI was somewhat surprised, but I did not lose my temper. I said,\r\npleasantly enough:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHulloa! what\xe2\x80\x99s that for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that for? Why\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo, on second thoughts, I will not repeat what Harris said. I may have\r\nbeen to blame, I admit it; but nothing excuses violence of language and\r\ncoarseness of expression, especially in a man who has been carefully\r\nbrought up, as I know Harris has been. I was thinking of other things,\r\nand forgot, as any one might easily understand, that I was steering,\r\nand the consequence was that we had got mixed up a good deal with the\r\ntow-path. It was difficult to say, for the moment, which was us and\r\nwhich was the Middlesex bank of the river; but we found out after a\r\nwhile, and separated ourselves.\r\n\r\nHarris, however, said he had done enough for a bit, and proposed that I\r\nshould take a turn; so, as we were in, I got out and took the tow-line,\r\nand ran the boat on past Hampton Court. What a dear old wall that is\r\nthat runs along by the river there! I never pass it without feeling\r\nbetter for the sight of it. Such a mellow, bright, sweet old wall; what\r\na charming picture it would make, with the lichen creeping here, and\r\nthe moss growing there, a shy young vine peeping over the top at this\r\nspot, to see what is going on upon the busy river, and the sober old\r\nivy clustering a little farther down! There are fifty shades and tints\r\nand hues in every ten yards of that old wall. If I could only draw,\r\nand knew how to paint, I could make a lovely sketch of that old wall,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m sure. I\xe2\x80\x99ve often thought I should like to live at Hampton Court.\r\nIt looks so peaceful and so quiet, and it is such a dear old place to\r\nramble round in the early morning before many people are about.\r\n\r\nBut, there, I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose I should really care for it when it came\r\nto actual practice. It would be so ghastly dull and depressing in the\r\nevening, when your lamp cast uncanny shadows on the panelled walls, and\r\nthe echo of distant feet rang through the cold stone corridors, and now\r\ndrew nearer, and now died away, and all was death-like silence, save\r\nthe beating of one\xe2\x80\x99s own heart.\r\n\r\nWe are creatures of the sun, we men and women. We love light and life.\r\nThat is why we crowd into the towns and cities, and the country grows\r\nmore and more deserted every year. In the sunlight\xe2\x80\x94in the daytime, when\r\nNature is alive and busy all around us, we like the open hill-sides and\r\nthe deep woods well enough: but in the night, when our Mother Earth has\r\ngone to sleep, and left us waking, oh! the world seems so lonesome,\r\nand we get frightened, like children in a silent house. Then we sit\r\nand sob, and long for the gas-lit streets, and the sound of human\r\nvoices, and the answering throb of human life. We feel so helpless and\r\nso little in the great stillness, when the dark trees rustle in the\r\nnight-wind. There are so many ghosts about, and their silent sighs make\r\nus feel so sad. Let us gather together in the great cities, and light\r\nhuge bonfires of a million gas-jets, and shout and sing together, and\r\nfeel brave.\r\n\r\n[Picture: People at Hampton Maze] Harris asked me if I\xe2\x80\x99d ever been in\r\nthe maze at Hampton Court. He said he went in once to show somebody\r\nelse the way. He had studied it up in a map, and it was so simple that\r\nit seemed foolish\xe2\x80\x94hardly worth the twopence charged for admission.\r\nHarris said he thought that map must have been got up as a practical\r\njoke, because it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a bit like the real thing, and only misleading.\r\nIt was a country cousin that Harris took in. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll just go in here, so that you can say you\xe2\x80\x99ve been, but it\xe2\x80\x99s very\r\nsimple. It\xe2\x80\x99s absurd to call it a maze. You keep on taking the first\r\nturning to the right. We\xe2\x80\x99ll just walk round for ten minutes, and then\r\ngo and get some lunch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey met some people soon after they had got inside, who said they had\r\nbeen there for three-quarters of an hour, and had had about enough of\r\nit. Harris told them they could follow him, if they liked; he was just\r\ngoing in, and then should turn round and come out again. They said it\r\nwas very kind of him, and fell behind, and followed.\r\n\r\nThey picked up various other people who wanted to get it over, as they\r\nwent along, until they had absorbed all the persons in the maze. People\r\nwho had given up all hopes of ever getting either in or out, or of ever\r\nseeing their home and friends again, plucked up courage at the sight of\r\nHarris and his party, and joined the procession, blessing him. Harris\r\nsaid he should judge there must have been twenty people, following him,\r\nin all; and one woman with a baby, who had been there all the morning,\r\ninsisted on taking his arm, for fear of losing him.\r\n\r\nHarris kept on turning to the right, but it seemed a long way, and his\r\ncousin said he supposed it was a very big maze.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, one of the largest in Europe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Harris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it must be,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the cousin, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause we\xe2\x80\x99ve walked a good two\r\nmiles already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHarris began to think it rather strange himself, but he held on until,\r\nat last, they passed the half of a penny bun on the ground that\r\nHarris\xe2\x80\x99s cousin swore he had noticed there seven minutes ago. Harris\r\nsaid: \xe2\x80\x9cOh, impossible!\xe2\x80\x9d but the woman with the baby said, \xe2\x80\x9cNot at all,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nas she herself had taken it from the child, and thrown it down there,\r\njust before she met Harris. She also added that she wished she never\r\nhad met Harris, and expressed an opinion that he was an impostor. That\r\nmade Harris mad, and he produced his map, and explained his theory.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe map may be all right enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the party, \xe2\x80\x9cif you know\r\nwhereabouts in it we are now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHarris didn\xe2\x80\x99t know, and suggested that the best thing to do would\r\nbe to go back to the entrance, and begin again. For the beginning\r\nagain part of it there was not much enthusiasm; but with regard to\r\nthe advisability of going back to the entrance there was complete\r\nunanimity, and so they turned, and trailed after Harris again, in the\r\nopposite direction. About ten minutes more passed, and then they found\r\nthemselves in the centre.\r\n\r\nHarris thought at first of pretending that that was what he had been\r\naiming at; but the crowd looked dangerous, and he decided to treat it\r\nas an accident.\r\n\r\nAnyhow, they had got something to start from then. They did know where\r\nthey were, and the map was once more consulted, and the thing seemed\r\nsimpler than ever, and off they started for the third time.\r\n\r\nAnd three minutes later they were back in the centre again.\r\n\r\nAfter that, they simply couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get anywhere else. Whatever way\r\nthey turned brought them back to the middle. It became so regular at\r\nlength, that some of the people stopped there, and waited for the\r\nothers to take a walk round, and come back to them. Harris drew out\r\nhis map again, after a while, but the sight of it only infuriated the\r\nmob, and they told him to go and curl his hair with it. Harris said\r\nthat he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help feeling that, to a certain extent, he had become\r\nunpopular.\r\n\r\nThey all got crazy at last, and sang out for the keeper, and the man\r\ncame and climbed up the ladder outside, and shouted out directions to\r\nthem. But all their heads were, by this time, in such a confused whirl\r\nthat they were incapable of grasping anything, and so the man told\r\nthem to stop where they were, and he would come to them. They huddled\r\ntogether, and waited; and he climbed down, and came in.\r\n\r\nHe was a young keeper, as luck would have it, and new to the business;\r\nand when he got in, he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t find them, and he wandered about,\r\ntrying to get to them, and then _he_ got lost. They caught sight of\r\nhim, every now and then, rushing about the other side of the hedge, and\r\nhe would see them, and rush to get to them, and they would wait there\r\nfor about five minutes, and then he would reappear again in exactly the\r\nsame spot, and ask them where they had been.\r\n\r\nThey had to wait till one of the old keepers came back from his dinner\r\nbefore they got out.\r\n\r\nHarris said he thought it was a very fine maze, so far as he was a\r\njudge; and we agreed that we would try to get George to go into it, on\r\nour way back.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe river in its Sunday garb.\xe2\x80\x94Dress on the river.\xe2\x80\x94A chance for the\r\nmen.\xe2\x80\x94Absence of taste in Harris.\xe2\x80\x94George\xe2\x80\x99s blazer.\xe2\x80\x94A day with the\r\nfashion-plate young lady.\xe2\x80\x94Mrs. Thomas\xe2\x80\x99s tomb.\xe2\x80\x94The man who loves not\r\ngraves and coffins and skulls.\xe2\x80\x94Harris mad.\xe2\x80\x94His views on George and\r\nBanks and lemonade.\xe2\x80\x94He performs tricks.\r\n\r\nIt was while passing through Moulsey Lock that Harris told me about his\r\nmaze experience. It took us some time to pass through, as we were the\r\nonly boat, and it is a big lock. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I ever remember to have\r\nseen Moulsey Lock, before, with only one boat in it. It is, I suppose,\r\nBoulter\xe2\x80\x99s not even excepted, the busiest lock on the river.\r\n\r\nI have stood and watched it, sometimes, when you could not see any\r\nwater at all, but only a brilliant tangle of bright blazers, and gay\r\ncaps, and saucy hats, and many-coloured parasols, and silken rugs, and\r\ncloaks, and streaming ribbons, and dainty whites; when looking down\r\ninto the lock from the quay, you might fancy it was a huge box into\r\nwhich flowers of every hue and shade had been thrown pell-mell, and lay\r\npiled up in a rainbow heap, that covered every corner.\r\n\r\nOn a fine Sunday it presents this appearance nearly all day long,\r\nwhile, up the stream, and down the stream, lie, waiting their turn,\r\noutside the gates, long lines of still more boats; and boats are\r\ndrawing near and passing away, so that the sunny river, from the Palace\r\nup to Hampton Church, is dotted and decked with yellow, and blue, and\r\norange, and white, and red, and pink. All the inhabitants of Hampton\r\nand Moulsey dress themselves up in boating costume, and come and mouch\r\nround the lock with their dogs, and flirt, and smoke, and watch the\r\nboats; and, altogether, what with the caps and jackets of the men, the\r\npretty coloured dresses of the women, the excited dogs, the moving\r\nboats, the white sails, the pleasant landscape, and the sparkling\r\nwater, it is one of the gayest sights I know of near this dull old\r\nLondon town.\r\n\r\nThe river affords a good opportunity for dress. For once in a way, we\r\nmen are able to show _our_ taste in colours, and I think we come out\r\nvery natty, if you ask me. I always like a little red in my things\xe2\x80\x94red\r\nand black. You know my hair is a sort of golden brown, rather a pretty\r\nshade I\xe2\x80\x99ve been told, and a dark red matches it beautifully; and then\r\nI always think a light-blue necktie goes so well with it, and a pair\r\nof those Russian-leather shoes and a red silk handkerchief round the\r\nwaist\xe2\x80\x94a handkerchief looks so much better than a belt.\r\n\r\nHarris always keeps to shades or mixtures of orange or yellow, but I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t think he is at all wise in this. His complexion is too dark for\r\nyellows. Yellows don\xe2\x80\x99t suit him: there can be no question about it.\r\nI want him to take to blue as a background, with white or cream for\r\nrelief; but, there! the less taste a person has in dress, the more\r\nobstinate he always seems to be. It is a great pity, because he will\r\nnever be a success as it is, while there are one or two colours in\r\nwhich he might not really look so bad, with his hat on.\r\n\r\nGeorge has bought some new things for this trip, and I\xe2\x80\x99m rather vexed\r\nabout them. The blazer is loud. I should not like George to know that\r\nI thought so, but there really is no other word for it. He brought it\r\nhome and showed it to us on Thursday evening. We asked him what colour\r\nhe called it, and he said he didn\xe2\x80\x99t know. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t think there was a\r\nname for the colour. The man had told him it was an Oriental design.\r\nGeorge put it on, and asked us what we thought of it. Harris said that,\r\nas an object to hang over a flower-bed in early spring to frighten the\r\nbirds away, he should respect it; but that, considered as an article of\r\ndress for any human being, except a Margate nigger, it made him ill.\r\nGeorge got quite huffy; but, as Harris said, if he didn\xe2\x80\x99t want his\r\nopinion, why did he ask for it?\r\n\r\nWhat troubles Harris and myself, with regard to it, is that we are\r\nafraid it will attract attention to the boat.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Young lady] Girls, also, don\xe2\x80\x99t look half bad in a boat, if\r\nprettily dressed. Nothing is more fetching, to my thinking, than a\r\ntasteful boating costume. But a \xe2\x80\x9cboating costume,\xe2\x80\x9d it would be as well\r\nif all ladies would understand, ought to be a costume that can be worn\r\nin a boat, and not merely under a glass-case. It utterly spoils an\r\nexcursion if you have folk in the boat who are thinking all the time a\r\ngood deal more of their dress than of the trip. It was my misfortune\r\nonce to go for a water picnic with two ladies of this kind. We did have\r\na lively time!\r\n\r\nThey were both beautifully got up\xe2\x80\x94all lace and silky stuff, and\r\nflowers, and ribbons, and dainty shoes, and light gloves. But they were\r\ndressed for a photographic studio, not for a river picnic. They were\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x9cboating costumes\xe2\x80\x9d of a French fashion-plate. It was ridiculous,\r\nfooling about in them anywhere near real earth, air, and water.\r\n\r\nThe first thing was that they thought the boat was not clean. We dusted\r\nall the seats for them, and then assured them that it was, but they\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t believe us. One of them rubbed the cushion with the forefinger\r\nof her glove, and showed the result to the other, and they both\r\nsighed, and sat down, with the air of early Christian martyrs trying\r\nto make themselves comfortable up against the stake. You are liable to\r\noccasionally splash a little when sculling, and it appeared that a drop\r\nof water ruined those costumes. The mark never came out, and a stain\r\nwas left on the dress for ever.\r\n\r\nI was stroke. I did my best. I feathered some two feet high, and\r\nI paused at the end of each stroke to let the blades drip before\r\nreturning them, and I picked out a smooth bit of water to drop them\r\ninto again each time. (Bow said, after a while, that he did not feel\r\nhimself a sufficiently accomplished oarsman to pull with me, but that\r\nhe would sit still, if I would allow him, and study my stroke. He said\r\nit interested him.) But, notwithstanding all this, and try as I would,\r\nI could not help an occasional flicker of water from going over those\r\ndresses.\r\n\r\nThe girls did not complain, but they huddled up close together, and\r\nset their lips firm, and every time a drop touched them, they visibly\r\nshrank and shuddered. It was a noble sight to see them suffering thus\r\nin silence, but it unnerved me altogether. I am too sensitive. I got\r\nwild and fitful in my rowing, and splashed more and more, the harder I\r\ntried not to.\r\n\r\nI gave it up at last; I said I\xe2\x80\x99d row bow. Bow thought the arrangement\r\nwould be better too, and we changed places. The ladies gave an\r\ninvoluntary sigh of relief when they saw me go, and quite brightened\r\nup for a moment. Poor girls! they had better have put up with me. The\r\nman they had got now was a jolly, light-hearted, thick-headed sort of\r\na chap, with about as much sensitiveness in him as there might be in a\r\nNewfoundland puppy. You might look daggers at him for an hour and he\r\nwould not notice it, and it would not trouble him if he did. He set a\r\ngood, rollicking, dashing stroke that sent the spray playing all over\r\nthe boat like a fountain, and made the whole crowd sit up straight\r\nin no time. When he spread more than pint of water over one of those\r\ndresses, he would give a pleasant little laugh, and say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure;\xe2\x80\x9d and offer them his handkerchief to wipe\r\nit off with.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s of no consequence,\xe2\x80\x9d the poor girls would murmur in reply,\r\nand covertly draw rugs and coats over themselves, and try and protect\r\nthemselves with their lace parasols.\r\n\r\nAt lunch they had a very bad time of it. People wanted them to sit\r\non the grass, and the grass was dusty; and the tree-trunks, against\r\nwhich they were invited to lean, did not appear to have been brushed\r\nfor weeks; so they spread their handkerchiefs on the ground and sat\r\non those, bolt upright. Somebody, in walking about with a plate of\r\nbeef-steak pie, tripped up over a root, and sent the pie flying. None\r\nof it went over them, fortunately, but the accident suggested a fresh\r\ndanger to them, and agitated them; and, whenever anybody moved about,\r\nafter that, with anything in his hand that could fall and make a mess,\r\nthey watched that person with growing anxiety until he sat down again.\r\n\r\n                          [Picture: Washing up]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow then, you girls,\xe2\x80\x9d said our friend Bow to them, cheerily, after it\r\nwas all over, \xe2\x80\x9ccome along, you\xe2\x80\x99ve got to wash up!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey didn\xe2\x80\x99t understand him at first. When they grasped the idea, they\r\nsaid they feared they did not know how to wash up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99ll soon show you,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried; \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s rare fun! You lie down on\r\nyour\xe2\x80\x94I mean you lean over the bank, you know, and sloush the things\r\nabout in the water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe elder sister said that she was afraid that they hadn\xe2\x80\x99t got on\r\ndresses suited to the work.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, they\xe2\x80\x99ll be all right,\xe2\x80\x9d said he light-heartedly; \xe2\x80\x9ctuck \xe2\x80\x99em up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he made them do it, too. He told them that that sort of thing was\r\nhalf the fun of a picnic. They said it was very interesting.\r\n\r\nNow I come to think it over, was that young man as dense-headed as we\r\nthought? or was he\xe2\x80\x94no, impossible! there was such a simple, child-like\r\nexpression about him!\r\n\r\nHarris wanted to get out at Hampton Church, to go and see Mrs. Thomas\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntomb.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is Mrs. Thomas?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow should I know?\xe2\x80\x9d replied Harris. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s a lady that\xe2\x80\x99s got a funny\r\ntomb, and I want to see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI objected. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether it is that I am built wrong, but I\r\nnever did seem to hanker after tombstones myself. I know that the\r\nproper thing to do, when you get to a village or town, is to rush off\r\nto the churchyard, and enjoy the graves; but it is a recreation that I\r\nalways deny myself. I take no interest in creeping round dim and chilly\r\nchurches behind wheezy old men, and reading epitaphs. Not even the\r\nsight of a bit of cracked brass let into a stone affords me what I call\r\nreal happiness.\r\n\r\nI shock respectable sextons by the imperturbability I am able to assume\r\nbefore exciting inscriptions, and by my lack of enthusiasm for the\r\nlocal family history, while my ill-concealed anxiety to get outside\r\nwounds their feelings.\r\n\r\nOne golden morning of a sunny day, I leant against the low stone wall\r\nthat guarded a little village church, and I smoked, and drank in deep,\r\ncalm gladness from the sweet, restful scene\xe2\x80\x94the grey old church with\r\nits clustering ivy and its quaint carved wooden porch, the white lane\r\nwinding down the hill between tall rows of elms, the thatched-roof\r\ncottages peeping above their trim-kept hedges, the silver river in the\r\nhollow, the wooded hills beyond!\r\n\r\nIt was a lovely landscape. It was idyllic, poetical, and it inspired\r\nme. I felt good and noble. I felt I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to be sinful and wicked\r\nany more. I would come and live here, and never do any more wrong, and\r\nlead a blameless, beautiful life, and have silver hair when I got old,\r\nand all that sort of thing.\r\n\r\nIn that moment I forgave all my friends and relations for their\r\nwickedness and cussedness, and I blessed them. They did not know that\r\nI blessed them. They went their abandoned way all unconscious of what\r\nI, far away in that peaceful village, was doing for them; but I did it,\r\nand I wished that I could let them know that I had done it, because\r\nI wanted to make them happy. I was going on thinking away all these\r\ngrand, tender thoughts, when my reverie was broken in upon by a shrill\r\npiping voice crying out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, sur, I\xe2\x80\x99m a-coming, I\xe2\x80\x99m a-coming. It\xe2\x80\x99s all right, sur; don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou be in a hurry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI looked up, and saw an old bald-headed man hobbling across the\r\nchurchyard towards me, carrying a huge bunch of keys in his hand that\r\nshook and jingled at every step.\r\n\r\nI motioned him away with silent dignity, but he still advanced,\r\nscreeching out the while:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m a-coming, sur, I\xe2\x80\x99m a-coming. I\xe2\x80\x99m a little lame. I ain\xe2\x80\x99t as spry as\r\nI used to be. This way, sur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo away, you miserable old man,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve come as soon as I could, sur,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied. \xe2\x80\x9cMy missis never see\r\nyou till just this minute. You follow me, sur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo away,\xe2\x80\x9d I repeated; \xe2\x80\x9cleave me before I get over the wall, and slay\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe seemed surprised.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you want to see the tombs?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t. I want to stop here, leaning up against\r\nthis gritty old wall. Go away, and don\xe2\x80\x99t disturb me. I am chock full\r\nof beautiful and noble thoughts, and I want to stop like it, because\r\nit feels nice and good. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you come fooling about, making me mad,\r\nchivying away all my better feelings with this silly tombstone nonsense\r\nof yours. Go away, and get somebody to bury you cheap, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll pay\r\nhalf the expense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was bewildered for a moment. He rubbed his eyes, and looked hard at\r\nme. I seemed human enough on the outside: he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make it out.\r\n\r\nHe said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYuise a stranger in these parts? You don\xe2\x80\x99t live here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Picture: Graves] \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t. _You_ wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t if _I_ did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou want to see the tombs\xe2\x80\x94graves\xe2\x80\x94folks been\r\nburied, you know\xe2\x80\x94coffins!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are an untruther,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied, getting roused; \xe2\x80\x9cI do not want to\r\nsee tombs\xe2\x80\x94not your tombs. Why should I? We have graves of our own, our\r\nfamily has. Why my uncle Podger has a tomb in Kensal Green Cemetery,\r\nthat is the pride of all that country-side; and my grandfather\xe2\x80\x99s vault\r\nat Bow is capable of accommodating eight visitors, while my great-aunt\r\nSusan has a brick grave in Finchley Churchyard, with a headstone with\r\na coffee-pot sort of thing in bas-relief upon it, and a six-inch best\r\nwhite stone coping all the way round, that cost pounds. When I want\r\ngraves, it is to those places that I go and revel. I do not want other\r\nfolk\xe2\x80\x99s. When you yourself are buried, I will come and see yours. That\r\nis all I can do for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe burst into tears. He said that one of the tombs had a bit of stone\r\nupon the top of it that had been said by some to be probably part of\r\nthe remains of the figure of a man, and that another had some words,\r\ncarved upon it, that nobody had ever been able to decipher.\r\n\r\nI still remained obdurate, and, in broken-hearted tones, he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, won\xe2\x80\x99t you come and see the memorial window?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI would not even see that, so he fired his last shot. He drew near, and\r\nwhispered hoarsely:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got a couple of skulls down in the crypt,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9ccome and see\r\nthose. Oh, do come and see the skulls! You are a young man out for a\r\nholiday, and you want to enjoy yourself. Come and see the skulls!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen I turned and fled, and as I sped I heard him calling to me:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come and see the skulls; come back and see the skulls!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHarris, however, revels in tombs, and graves, and epitaphs, and\r\nmonumental inscriptions, and the thought of not seeing Mrs. Thomas\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngrave made him crazy. He said he had looked forward to seeing Mrs.\r\nThomas\xe2\x80\x99s grave from the first moment that the trip was proposed\xe2\x80\x94said\r\nhe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have joined if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been for the idea of seeing Mrs.\r\nThomas\xe2\x80\x99s tomb.\r\n\r\nI reminded him of George, and how we had to get the boat up to\r\nShepperton by five o\xe2\x80\x99clock to meet him, and then he went for George.\r\nWhy was George to fool about all day, and leave us to lug this\r\nlumbering old top-heavy barge up and down the river by ourselves to\r\nmeet him? Why couldn\xe2\x80\x99t George come and do some work? Why couldn\xe2\x80\x99t he\r\nhave got the day off, and come down with us? Bank be blowed! What good\r\nwas he at the bank?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never see him doing any work there,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Harris, \xe2\x80\x9cwhenever I\r\ngo in. He sits behind a bit of glass all day, trying to look as if he\r\nwas doing something. What\xe2\x80\x99s the good of a man behind a bit of glass? I\r\nhave to work for my living. Why can\xe2\x80\x99t he work? What use is he there,\r\nand what\xe2\x80\x99s the good of their banks? They take your money, and then,\r\nwhen you draw a cheque, they send it back smeared all over with \xe2\x80\x98No\r\neffects,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Refer to drawer.\xe2\x80\x99 What\xe2\x80\x99s the good of that? That\xe2\x80\x99s the sort\r\nof trick they served me twice last week. I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to stand it much\r\nlonger. I shall withdraw my account. If he was here, we could go and\r\nsee that tomb. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe he\xe2\x80\x99s at the bank at all. He\xe2\x80\x99s larking\r\nabout somewhere, that\xe2\x80\x99s what he\xe2\x80\x99s doing, leaving us to do all the work.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m going to get out, and have a drink.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI pointed out to him that we were miles away from a pub.; and then he\r\nwent on about the river, and what was the good of the river, and was\r\neveryone who came on the river to die of thirst?\r\n\r\nIt is always best to let Harris have his head when he gets like this.\r\nThen he pumps himself out, and is quiet afterwards.\r\n\r\nI reminded him that there was concentrated lemonade in the hamper, and\r\na gallon-jar of water in the nose of the boat, and that the two only\r\nwanted mixing to make a cool and refreshing beverage.\r\n\r\nThen he flew off about lemonade, and \xe2\x80\x9csuch-like Sunday-school slops,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nas he termed them, ginger-beer, raspberry syrup, &c., &c. He said they\r\nall produced dyspepsia, and ruined body and soul alike, and were the\r\ncause of half the crime in England.\r\n\r\nHe said he must drink something, however, and climbed upon the seat,\r\nand leant over to get the bottle. It was right at the bottom of\r\nthe hamper, and seemed difficult to find, and he had to lean over\r\nfurther and further, and, in trying to steer at the same time, from a\r\ntopsy-turvy point of view, he pulled the wrong line, and sent the boat\r\ninto the bank, and the shock upset him, and he dived down right into\r\nthe hamper, and stood there on his head, holding on to the sides of the\r\nboat like grim death, his legs sticking up into the air. He dared not\r\nmove for fear of going over, and had to stay there till I could get\r\nhold of his legs, and haul him back, and that made him madder than ever.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII.\r\n\r\n\r\nBlackmailing.\xe2\x80\x94The proper course to pursue.\xe2\x80\x94Selfish boorishness of\r\nriver-side landowner.\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cNotice\xe2\x80\x9d boards.\xe2\x80\x94Unchristianlike feelings of\r\nHarris.\xe2\x80\x94How Harris sings a comic song.\xe2\x80\x94A high-class party.\xe2\x80\x94Shameful\r\nconduct of two abandoned young men.\xe2\x80\x94Some useless information.\xe2\x80\x94George\r\nbuys a banjo.\r\n\r\nWe stopped under the willows by Kempton Park, and lunched. It is a\r\npretty little spot there: a pleasant grass plateau, running along\r\nby the water\xe2\x80\x99s edge, and overhung by willows. We had just commenced\r\nthe third course\xe2\x80\x94the bread and jam\xe2\x80\x94when a gentleman in shirt-sleeves\r\nand a short pipe came along, and wanted to know if we knew that we\r\nwere trespassing. We said we hadn\xe2\x80\x99t given the matter sufficient\r\nconsideration as yet to enable us to arrive at a definite conclusion on\r\nthat point, but that, if he assured us on his word as a gentleman that\r\nwe _were_ trespassing, we would, without further hesitation, believe it.\r\n\r\nHe gave us the required assurance, and we thanked him, but he still\r\nhung about, and seemed to be dissatisfied, so we asked him if there\r\nwas anything further that we could do for him; and Harris, who is of a\r\nchummy disposition, offered him a bit of bread and jam.\r\n\r\nI fancy he must have belonged to some society sworn to abstain from\r\nbread and jam; for he declined it quite gruffly, as if he were vexed at\r\nbeing tempted with it, and he added that it was his duty to turn us off.\r\n\r\nHarris said that if it was a duty it ought to be done, and asked the\r\nman what was his idea with regard to the best means for accomplishing\r\nit. Harris is what you would call a well-made man of about number one\r\nsize, and looks hard and bony, and the man measured him up and down,\r\nand said he would go and consult his master, and then come back and\r\nchuck us both into the river.\r\n\r\nOf course, we never saw him any more, and, of course, all he really\r\nwanted was a shilling. There are a certain number of riverside roughs\r\nwho make quite an income, during the summer, by slouching about the\r\nbanks and blackmailing weak-minded noodles in this way. They represent\r\nthemselves as sent by the proprietor. The proper course to pursue is\r\nto offer your name and address, and leave the owner, if he really has\r\nanything to do with the matter, to summon you, and prove what damage\r\nyou have done to his land by sitting down on a bit of it. But the\r\nmajority of people are so intensely lazy and timid, that they prefer to\r\nencourage the imposition by giving in to it rather than put an end to\r\nit by the exertion of a little firmness.\r\n\r\nWhere it is really the owners that are to blame, they ought to be\r\nshown up. The selfishness of the riparian proprietor grows with every\r\nyear. If these men had their way they would close the river Thames\r\naltogether. They actually do this along the minor tributary streams and\r\nin the backwaters. They drive posts into the bed of the stream, and\r\ndraw chains across from bank to bank, and nail huge notice-boards on\r\nevery tree. The sight of those notice-boards rouses every evil instinct\r\nin my nature. I feel I want to tear each one down, and hammer it over\r\nthe head of the man who put it up, until I have killed him, and then I\r\nwould bury him, and put the board up over the grave as a tombstone.\r\n\r\nI mentioned these feelings of mine to Harris, and he said he had them\r\nworse than that. He said he not only felt he wanted to kill the man who\r\ncaused the board to be put up, but that he should like to slaughter the\r\nwhole of his family and all his friends and relations, and then burn\r\ndown his house. This seemed to me to be going too far, and I said so to\r\nHarris; but he answered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a bit of it. Serve \xe2\x80\x99em all jolly well right, and I\xe2\x80\x99d go and sing\r\ncomic songs on the ruins.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was vexed to hear Harris go on in this blood-thirsty strain. We\r\nnever ought to allow our instincts of justice to degenerate into mere\r\nvindictiveness. It was a long while before I could get Harris to take\r\na more Christian view of the subject, but I succeeded at last, and\r\nhe promised me that he would spare the friends and relations at all\r\nevents, and would not sing comic songs on the ruins.\r\n\r\nYou have never heard Harris sing a comic song, or you would understand\r\nthe service I had rendered to mankind. It is one of Harris\xe2\x80\x99s fixed\r\nideas that he _can_ sing a comic song; the fixed idea, on the contrary,\r\namong those of Harris\xe2\x80\x99s friends who have heard him try, is that he\r\n_can\xe2\x80\x99t_ and never will be able to, and that he ought not to be allowed\r\nto try.\r\n\r\nWhen Harris is at a party, and is asked to sing, he replies: \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\r\ncan only sing a _comic_ song, you know;\xe2\x80\x9d and he says it in a tone that\r\nimplies that his singing of _that_, however, is a thing that you ought\r\nto hear once, and then die.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that _is_ nice,\xe2\x80\x9d says the hostess. \xe2\x80\x9cDo sing one, Mr. Harris;\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nHarris gets up, and makes for the piano, with the beaming cheeriness of\r\na generous-minded man who is just about to give somebody something.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, silence, please, everybody\xe2\x80\x9d says the hostess, turning round; \xe2\x80\x9cMr.\r\nHarris is going to sing a comic song!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, how jolly!\xe2\x80\x9d they murmur; and they hurry in from the conservatory,\r\nand come up from the stairs, and go and fetch each other from all over\r\nthe house, and crowd into the drawing-room, and sit round, all smirking\r\nin anticipation.\r\n\r\nThen Harris begins.\r\n\r\nWell, you don\xe2\x80\x99t look for much of a voice in a comic song. You don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nexpect correct phrasing or vocalization. You don\xe2\x80\x99t mind if a man does\r\nfind out, when in the middle of a note, that he is too high, and comes\r\ndown with a jerk. You don\xe2\x80\x99t bother about time. You don\xe2\x80\x99t mind a man\r\nbeing two bars in front of the accompaniment, and easing up in the\r\nmiddle of a line to argue it out with the pianist, and then starting\r\nthe verse afresh. But you do expect the words.\r\n\r\nYou don\xe2\x80\x99t expect a man to never remember more than the first three\r\nlines of the first verse, and to keep on repeating these until it is\r\ntime to begin the chorus. You don\xe2\x80\x99t expect a man to break off in the\r\nmiddle of a line, and snigger, and say, it\xe2\x80\x99s very funny, but he\xe2\x80\x99s blest\r\nif he can think of the rest of it, and then try and make it up for\r\nhimself, and, afterwards, suddenly recollect it, when he has got to an\r\nentirely different part of the song, and break off, without a word of\r\nwarning, to go back and let you have it then and there. You don\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94well,\r\nI will just give you an idea of Harris\xe2\x80\x99s comic singing, and then you\r\ncan judge of it for yourself.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Harris] HARRIS (_standing up in front of piano and addressing\r\nthe expectant mob_): \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid it\xe2\x80\x99s a very old thing, you know. I\r\nexpect you all know it, you know. But it\xe2\x80\x99s the only thing I know.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s song out of _Pinafore_\xe2\x80\x94no, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean _Pinafore_\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nmean\xe2\x80\x94you know what I mean\xe2\x80\x94the other thing, you know. You must all join\r\nin the chorus, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[_Murmurs of delight and anxiety to join in the chorus_. _Brilliant\r\nperformance of prelude to the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s song in_ \xe2\x80\x9c_Trial by Jury_\xe2\x80\x9d _by\r\nnervous Pianist_. _Moment arrives for Harris to join in_. _Harris takes\r\nno notice of it_. _Nervous pianist commences prelude over again, and\r\nHarris_, _commencing singing at the same time_, _dashes off the first\r\ntwo lines of the First Lord\xe2\x80\x99s song out of_ \xe2\x80\x9c_Pinafore_.\xe2\x80\x9d _Nervous\r\npianist tries to push on with prelude_, _gives it up_, _and tries to\r\nfollow Harris with accompaniment to Judge\xe2\x80\x99s song out of_ \xe2\x80\x9c_Trial by\r\nJury_,\xe2\x80\x9d _finds that doesn\xe2\x80\x99t answer_, _and tries to recollect what he\r\nis doing_, _and where he is_, _feels his mind giving way_, _and stops\r\nshort_.]\r\n\r\nHARRIS (_with kindly encouragement_): \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all right. You\xe2\x80\x99re doing it\r\nvery well, indeed\xe2\x80\x94go on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNERVOUS PIANIST: \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid there\xe2\x80\x99s a mistake somewhere. What are you\r\nsinging?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHARRIS (_promptly_): \xe2\x80\x9cWhy the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s song out of Trial by Jury. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou know it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSOME FRIEND OF HARRIS\xe2\x80\x99S (_from the back of the room_): \xe2\x80\x9cNo, you\xe2\x80\x99re not,\r\nyou chuckle-head, you\xe2\x80\x99re singing the Admiral\xe2\x80\x99s song from _Pinafore_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[_Long argument between Harris and Harris\xe2\x80\x99s friend as to what Harris\r\nis really singing_. _Friend finally suggests that it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter\r\nwhat Harris is singing so long as Harris gets on and sings it_, _and\r\nHarris_, _with an evident sense of injustice rankling inside him_,\r\n_requests pianist to begin again_. _Pianist_, _thereupon_, _starts\r\nprelude to the Admiral\xe2\x80\x99s song_, _and Harris_, _seizing what he\r\nconsiders to be a favourable opening in the music_, _begins_.]\r\n\r\nHARRIS:\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98When I was young and called to the Bar.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[_General roar of laughter_, _taken by Harris as a compliment_.\r\n_Pianist_, _thinking of his wife and family_, _gives up the unequal\r\ncontest and retires_; _his place being taken by a stronger-nerved man_.\r\n\r\nTHE NEW PIANIST (_cheerily_): \xe2\x80\x9cNow then, old man, you start off, and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll follow. We won\xe2\x80\x99t bother about any prelude.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHARRIS (_upon whom the explanation of matters has slowly\r\ndawned\xe2\x80\x94laughing_): \xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove! I beg your pardon. Of course\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve been\r\nmixing up the two songs. It was Jenkins confused me, you know. Now then.\r\n\r\n[_Singing_; _his voice appearing to come from the cellar_, _and\r\nsuggesting the first low warnings of an approaching earthquake_.\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98When I was young I served a term\r\n    As office-boy to an attorney\xe2\x80\x99s firm.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n(_Aside to pianist_): \xe2\x80\x9cIt is too low, old man; we\xe2\x80\x99ll have that over\r\nagain, if you don\xe2\x80\x99t mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[_Sings first two lines over again_, _in a high falsetto this time_.\r\n_Great surprise on the part of the audience_. _Nervous old lady near\r\nthe fire begins to cry_, _and has to be led out_.]\r\n\r\nHARRIS (_continuing_):\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98I swept the windows and I swept the door,\r\n    And I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nNo\xe2\x80\x94no, I cleaned the windows of the big front door. And I polished up\r\nthe floor\xe2\x80\x94no, dash it\xe2\x80\x94I beg your pardon\xe2\x80\x94funny thing, I can\xe2\x80\x99t think of\r\nthat line. And I\xe2\x80\x94and I\xe2\x80\x94Oh, well, we\xe2\x80\x99ll get on to the chorus, and chance\r\nit (_sings_):\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98And I diddle-diddle-diddle-diddle-diddle-diddle-de,\r\n    Till now I am the ruler of the Queen\xe2\x80\x99s navee.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nNow then, chorus\xe2\x80\x94it is the last two lines repeated, you know.\r\n\r\nGENERAL CHORUS:\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cAnd he diddle-diddle-diddle-diddle-diddle-diddle-dee\xe2\x80\x99d,\r\n    Till now he is the ruler of the Queen\xe2\x80\x99s navee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Harris never sees what an ass he is making of himself, and how he\r\nis annoying a lot of people who never did him any harm. He honestly\r\nimagines that he has given them a treat, and says he will sing another\r\ncomic song after supper.\r\n\r\nSpeaking of comic songs and parties, reminds me of a rather curious\r\nincident at which I once assisted; which, as it throws much light upon\r\nthe inner mental working of human nature in general, ought, I think, to\r\nbe recorded in these pages.\r\n\r\nWe were a fashionable and highly cultured party. We had on our best\r\nclothes, and we talked pretty, and were very happy\xe2\x80\x94all except two young\r\nfellows, students, just returned from Germany, commonplace young men,\r\nwho seemed restless and uncomfortable, as if they found the proceedings\r\nslow. The truth was, we were too clever for them. Our brilliant but\r\npolished conversation, and our high-class tastes, were beyond them.\r\nThey were out of place, among us. They never ought to have been there\r\nat all. Everybody agreed upon that, later on.\r\n\r\nWe played _morceaux_ from the old German masters. We discussed\r\nphilosophy and ethics. We flirted with graceful dignity. We were even\r\nhumorous\xe2\x80\x94in a high-class way.\r\n\r\nSomebody recited a French poem after supper, and we said it was\r\nbeautiful; and then a lady sang a sentimental ballad in Spanish, and it\r\nmade one or two of us weep\xe2\x80\x94it was so pathetic.\r\n\r\nAnd then those two young men got up, and asked us if we had ever heard\r\nHerr Slossenn Boschen (who had just arrived, and was then down in the\r\nsupper-room) sing his great German comic song.\r\n\r\nNone of us had heard it, that we could remember.\r\n\r\nThe young men said it was the funniest song that had ever been written,\r\nand that, if we liked, they would get Herr Slossenn Boschen, whom they\r\nknew very well, to sing it. They said it was so funny that, when Herr\r\nSlossenn Boschen had sung it once before the German Emperor, he (the\r\nGerman Emperor) had had to be carried off to bed.\r\n\r\nThey said nobody could sing it like Herr Slossenn Boschen; he was so\r\nintensely serious all through it that you might fancy he was reciting\r\na tragedy, and that, of course, made it all the funnier. They said he\r\nnever once suggested by his tone or manner that he was singing anything\r\nfunny\xe2\x80\x94that would spoil it. It was his air of seriousness, almost of\r\npathos, that made it so irresistibly amusing.\r\n\r\nWe said we yearned to hear it, that we wanted a good laugh; and they\r\nwent downstairs, and fetched Herr Slossenn Boschen.\r\n\r\nHe appeared to be quite pleased to sing it, for he came up at once, and\r\nsat down to the piano without another word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it will amuse you. You will laugh,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered the two young men,\r\nas they passed through the room, and took up an unobtrusive position\r\nbehind the Professor\xe2\x80\x99s back.\r\n\r\nHerr Slossenn Boschen accompanied himself. The prelude did not suggest\r\na comic song exactly. It was a weird, soulful air. It quite made one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nflesh creep; but we murmured to one another that it was the German\r\nmethod, and prepared to enjoy it.\r\n\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand German myself. I learned it at school, but forgot\r\nevery word of it two years after I had left, and have felt much\r\nbetter ever since. Still, I did not want the people there to guess my\r\nignorance; so I hit upon what I thought to be rather a good idea. I\r\nkept my eye on the two young students, and followed them. When they\r\ntittered, I tittered; when they roared, I roared; and I also threw in\r\na little snigger all by myself now and then, as if I had seen a bit\r\nof humour that had escaped the others. I considered this particularly\r\nartful on my part.\r\n\r\nI noticed, as the song progressed, that a good many other people seemed\r\nto have their eye fixed on the two young men, as well as myself. These\r\nother people also tittered when the young men tittered, and roared when\r\nthe young men roared; and, as the two young men tittered and roared and\r\nexploded with laughter pretty continuously all through the song, it\r\nwent exceedingly well.\r\n\r\nAnd yet that German Professor did not seem happy. At first, when we\r\nbegan to laugh, the expression of his face was one of intense surprise,\r\nas if laughter were the very last thing he had expected to be greeted\r\nwith. We thought this very funny: we said his earnest manner was half\r\nthe humour. The slightest hint on his part that he knew how funny he\r\nwas would have completely ruined it all. As we continued to laugh,\r\nhis surprise gave way to an air of annoyance and indignation, and he\r\nscowled fiercely round upon us all (except upon the two young men who,\r\nbeing behind him, he could not see). That sent us into convulsions. We\r\ntold each other that it would be the death of us, this thing. The words\r\nalone, we said, were enough to send us into fits, but added to his mock\r\nseriousness\xe2\x80\x94oh, it was too much!\r\n\r\nIn the last verse, he surpassed himself. He glowered round upon us with\r\na look of such concentrated ferocity that, but for our being forewarned\r\nas to the German method of comic singing, we should have been nervous;\r\nand he threw such a wailing note of agony into the weird music that, if\r\nwe had not known it was a funny song, we might have wept.\r\n\r\nHe finished amid a perfect shriek of laughter. We said it was the\r\nfunniest thing we had ever heard in all our lives. We said how strange\r\nit was that, in the face of things like these, there should be a\r\npopular notion that the Germans hadn\xe2\x80\x99t any sense of humour. And we\r\nasked the Professor why he didn\xe2\x80\x99t translate the song into English, so\r\nthat the common people could understand it, and hear what a real comic\r\nsong was like.\r\n\r\nThen Herr Slossenn Boschen got up, and went on awful. He swore at us in\r\nGerman (which I should judge to be a singularly effective language for\r\nthat purpose), and he danced, and shook his fists, and called us all\r\nthe English he knew. He said he had never been so insulted in all his\r\nlife.\r\n\r\nIt appeared that the song was not a comic song at all. It was about\r\na young girl who lived in the Hartz Mountains, and who had given up\r\nher life to save her lover\xe2\x80\x99s soul; and he died, and met her spirit in\r\nthe air; and then, in the last verse, he jilted her spirit, and went\r\non with another spirit\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m not quite sure of the details, but it was\r\nsomething very sad, I know. Herr Boschen said he had sung it once\r\nbefore the German Emperor, and he (the German Emperor) had sobbed like\r\na little child. He (Herr Boschen) said it was generally acknowledged to\r\nbe one of the most tragic and pathetic songs in the German language.\r\n\r\nIt was a trying situation for us\xe2\x80\x94very trying. There seemed to be no\r\nanswer. We looked around for the two young men who had done this thing,\r\nbut they had left the house in an unostentatious manner immediately\r\nafter the end of the song.\r\n\r\nThat was the end of that party.
