I never saw a party break up so\r\nquietly, and with so little fuss. We never said good-night even to\r\none another. We came downstairs one at a time, walking softly, and\r\nkeeping the shady side. We asked the servant for our hats and coats in\r\nwhispers, and opened the door for ourselves, and slipped out, and got\r\nround the corner quickly, avoiding each other as much as possible.\r\n\r\nI have never taken much interest in German songs since then.\r\n\r\nWe reached Sunbury Lock at half-past three. The river is sweetly pretty\r\njust there before you come to the gates, and the backwater is charming;\r\nbut don\xe2\x80\x99t attempt to row up it.\r\n\r\nI tried to do so once. I was sculling, and asked the fellows who were\r\nsteering if they thought it could be done, and they said, oh, yes, they\r\nthought so, if I pulled hard. We were just under the little foot-bridge\r\nthat crosses it between the two weirs, when they said this, and I bent\r\ndown over the sculls, and set myself up, and pulled.\r\n\r\nI pulled splendidly. I got well into a steady rhythmical swing. I put\r\nmy arms, and my legs, and my back into it. I set myself a good, quick,\r\ndashing stroke, and worked in really grand style. My two friends said\r\nit was a pleasure to watch me. At the end of five minutes, I thought we\r\nought to be pretty near the weir, and I looked up. We were under the\r\nbridge, in exactly the same spot that we were when I began, and there\r\nwere those two idiots, injuring themselves by violent laughing. I had\r\nbeen grinding away like mad to keep that boat stuck still under that\r\nbridge. I let other people pull up backwaters against strong streams\r\nnow.\r\n\r\nWe sculled up to Walton, a rather large place for a riverside town. As\r\nwith all riverside places, only the tiniest corner of it comes down to\r\nthe water, so that from the boat you might fancy it was a village of\r\nsome half-dozen houses, all told. Windsor and Abingdon are the only\r\ntowns between London and Oxford that you can really see anything of\r\nfrom the stream. All the others hide round corners, and merely peep at\r\nthe river down one street: my thanks to them for being so considerate,\r\nand leaving the river-banks to woods and fields and water-works.\r\n\r\nEven Reading, though it does its best to spoil and sully and make\r\nhideous as much of the river as it can reach, is good-natured enough to\r\nkeep its ugly face a good deal out of sight.\r\n\r\nC\xc3\xa6sar, of course, had a little place at Walton\xe2\x80\x94a camp, or an\r\nentrenchment, or something of that sort. C\xc3\xa6sar was a regular up-river\r\nman. Also Queen Elizabeth, she was there, too. You can never get away\r\nfrom that woman, go where you will. Cromwell and Bradshaw (not the\r\nguide man, but the King Charles\xe2\x80\x99s head man) likewise sojourned here.\r\nThey must have been quite a pleasant little party, altogether.\r\n\r\nThere is an iron \xe2\x80\x9cscold\xe2\x80\x99s bridle\xe2\x80\x9d in Walton Church. They used these\r\nthings in ancient days for curbing women\xe2\x80\x99s tongues. They have given up\r\nthe attempt now. I suppose iron was getting scarce, and nothing else\r\nwould be strong enough.\r\n\r\nThere are also tombs of note in the church, and I was afraid I should\r\nnever get Harris past them; but he didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to think of them, and we\r\nwent on. Above the bridge the river winds tremendously. This makes it\r\nlook picturesque; but it irritates you from a towing or sculling point\r\nof view, and causes argument between the man who is pulling and the man\r\nwho is steering.\r\n\r\nYou pass Oatlands Park on the right bank here. It is a famous old\r\nplace. Henry VIII. stole it from some one or the other, I forget whom\r\nnow, and lived in it. There is a grotto in the park which you can see\r\nfor a fee, and which is supposed to be very wonderful; but I cannot see\r\nmuch in it myself. The late Duchess of York, who lived at Oatlands,\r\nwas very fond of dogs, and kept an immense number. She had a special\r\ngraveyard made, in which to bury them when they died, and there they\r\nlie, about fifty of them, with a tombstone over each, and an epitaph\r\ninscribed thereon.\r\n\r\nWell, I dare say they deserve it quite as much as the average Christian\r\ndoes.\r\n\r\nAt \xe2\x80\x9cCorway Stakes\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the first bend above Walton Bridge\xe2\x80\x94was fought a\r\nbattle between C\xc3\xa6sar and Cassivelaunus. Cassivelaunus had prepared the\r\nriver for C\xc3\xa6sar, by planting it full of stakes (and had, no doubt, put\r\nup a notice-board). But C\xc3\xa6sar crossed in spite of this. You couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nchoke C\xc3\xa6sar off that river. He is the sort of man we want round the\r\nbackwaters now.\r\n\r\nHalliford and Shepperton are both pretty little spots where they touch\r\nthe river; but there is nothing remarkable about either of them. There\r\nis a tomb in Shepperton churchyard, however, with a poem on it, and\r\nI was nervous lest Harris should want to get out and fool round it.\r\nI saw him fix a longing eye on the landing-stage as we drew near it,\r\nso I managed, by an adroit movement, to jerk his cap into the water,\r\nand in the excitement of recovering that, and his indignation at my\r\nclumsiness, he forgot all about his beloved graves.\r\n\r\nAt Weybridge, the Wey (a pretty little stream, navigable for small\r\nboats up to Guildford, and one which I have always been making up my\r\nmind to explore, and never have), the Bourne, and the Basingstoke Canal\r\nall enter the Thames together. The lock is just opposite the town, and\r\nthe first thing that we saw, when we came in view of it, was George\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nblazer on one of the lock gates, closer inspection showing that George\r\nwas inside it.\r\n\r\nMontmorency set up a furious barking, I shrieked, Harris roared; George\r\nwaved his hat, and yelled back. The lock-keeper rushed out with a\r\ndrag, under the impression that somebody had fallen into the lock, and\r\nappeared annoyed at finding that no one had.\r\n\r\nGeorge had rather a curious oilskin-covered parcel in his hand. It was\r\nround and flat at one end, with a long straight handle sticking out of\r\nit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Harris\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9ca frying-pan?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, with a strange, wild look glittering in his eyes;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthey are all the rage this season; everybody has got them up the\r\nriver. It\xe2\x80\x99s a banjo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never knew you played the banjo!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Harris and I, in one breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot exactly,\xe2\x80\x9d replied George: \xe2\x80\x9cbut it\xe2\x80\x99s very easy, they tell me; and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve got the instruction book!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n                     [Picture: George and the banjo]\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX.\r\n\r\n\r\nGeorge is introduced to work.\xe2\x80\x94Heathenish instincts of\r\ntow-lines.\xe2\x80\x94Ungrateful conduct of a double-sculling skiff.\xe2\x80\x94Towers\r\nand towed.\xe2\x80\x94A use discovered for lovers.\xe2\x80\x94Strange disappearance of an\r\nelderly lady.\xe2\x80\x94Much haste, less speed.\xe2\x80\x94Being towed by girls: exciting\r\nsensation.\xe2\x80\x94The missing lock or the haunted river.\xe2\x80\x94Music.\xe2\x80\x94Saved!\r\n\r\nWe made George work, now we had got him. He did not want to work, of\r\ncourse; that goes without saying. He had had a hard time in the City,\r\nso he explained. Harris, who is callous in his nature, and not prone to\r\npity, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! and now you are going to have a hard time on the river for a\r\nchange; change is good for everyone. Out you get!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe could not in conscience\xe2\x80\x94not even George\xe2\x80\x99s conscience\xe2\x80\x94object, though\r\nhe did suggest that, perhaps, it would be better for him to stop in the\r\nboat, and get tea ready, while Harris and I towed, because getting tea\r\nwas such a worrying work, and Harris and I looked tired. The only reply\r\nwe made to this, however, was to pass him over the tow-line, and he\r\ntook it, and stepped out.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Dog wrapped in tow-line] There is something very strange and\r\nunaccountable about a tow-line. You roll it up with as much patience\r\nand care as you would take to fold up a new pair of trousers, and\r\nfive minutes afterwards, when you pick it up, it is one ghastly,\r\nsoul-revolting tangle.\r\n\r\nI do not wish to be insulting, but I firmly believe that if you took\r\nan average tow-line, and stretched it out straight across the middle\r\nof a field, and then turned your back on it for thirty seconds, that,\r\nwhen you looked round again, you would find that it had got itself\r\naltogether in a heap in the middle of the field, and had twisted itself\r\nup, and tied itself into knots, and lost its two ends, and become all\r\nloops; and it would take you a good half-hour, sitting down there on\r\nthe grass and swearing all the while, to disentangle it again.\r\n\r\nThat is my opinion of tow-lines in general. Of course, there may be\r\nhonourable exceptions; I do not say that there are not. There may\r\nbe tow-lines that are a credit to their profession\xe2\x80\x94conscientious,\r\nrespectable tow-lines\xe2\x80\x94tow-lines that do not imagine they are\r\ncrochet-work, and try to knit themselves up into antimacassars the\r\ninstant they are left to themselves. I say there _may_ be such\r\ntow-lines; I sincerely hope there are. But I have not met with them.\r\n\r\nThis tow-line I had taken in myself just before we had got to the lock.\r\nI would not let Harris touch it, because he is careless. I had looped\r\nit round slowly and cautiously, and tied it up in the middle, and\r\nfolded it in two, and laid it down gently at the bottom of the boat.\r\nHarris had lifted it up scientifically, and had put it into George\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhand. George had taken it firmly, and held it away from him, and had\r\nbegun to unravel it as if he were taking the swaddling clothes off a\r\nnew-born infant; and, before he had unwound a dozen yards, the thing\r\nwas more like a badly-made door-mat than anything else.\r\n\r\nIt is always the same, and the same sort of thing always goes on in\r\nconnection with it. The man on the bank, who is trying to disentangle\r\nit, thinks all the fault lies with the man who rolled it up; and when a\r\nman up the river thinks a thing, he says it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you been trying to do with it, make a fishing-net of it?\r\nYou\xe2\x80\x99ve made a nice mess you have; why couldn\xe2\x80\x99t you wind it up properly,\r\nyou silly dummy?\xe2\x80\x9d he grunts from time to time as he struggles wildly\r\nwith it, and lays it out flat on the tow-path, and runs round and round\r\nit, trying to find the end.\r\n\r\nOn the other hand, the man who wound it up thinks the whole cause of\r\nthe muddle rests with the man who is trying to unwind it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was all right when you took it!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaims indignantly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t you think what you are doing? You go about things in such a\r\nslap-dash style. You\xe2\x80\x99d get a scaffolding pole entangled _you_ would!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd they feel so angry with one another that they would like to hang\r\neach other with the thing. Ten minutes go by, and the first man gives\r\na yell and goes mad, and dances on the rope, and tries to pull it\r\nstraight by seizing hold of the first piece that comes to his hand and\r\nhauling at it. Of course, this only gets it into a tighter tangle than\r\never. Then the second man climbs out of the boat and comes to help him,\r\nand they get in each other\xe2\x80\x99s way, and hinder one another. They both get\r\nhold of the same bit of line, and pull at it in opposite directions,\r\nand wonder where it is caught. In the end, they do get it clear, and\r\nthen turn round and find that the boat has drifted off, and is making\r\nstraight for the weir.\r\n\r\nThis really happened once to my own knowledge. It was up by Boveney,\r\none rather windy morning. We were pulling down stream, and, as we came\r\nround the bend, we noticed a couple of men on the bank. They were\r\nlooking at each other with as bewildered and helplessly miserable\r\nexpression as I have ever witnessed on any human countenance before or\r\nsince, and they held a long tow-line between them. It was clear that\r\nsomething had happened, so we eased up and asked them what was the\r\nmatter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, our boat\xe2\x80\x99s gone off!\xe2\x80\x9d they replied in an indignant tone. \xe2\x80\x9cWe just\r\ngot out to disentangle the tow-line, and when we looked round, it was\r\ngone!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd they seemed hurt at what they evidently regarded as a mean and\r\nungrateful act on the part of the boat.\r\n\r\nWe found the truant for them half a mile further down, held by some\r\nrushes, and we brought it back to them. I bet they did not give that\r\nboat another chance for a week.\r\n\r\nI shall never forget the picture of those two men walking up and down\r\nthe bank with a tow-line, looking for their boat.\r\n\r\nOne sees a good many funny incidents up the river in connection with\r\ntowing. One of the most common is the sight of a couple of towers,\r\nwalking briskly along, deep in an animated discussion, while the man\r\nin the boat, a hundred yards behind them, is vainly shrieking to them\r\nto stop, and making frantic signs of distress with a scull. Something\r\nhas gone wrong; the rudder has come off, or the boat-hook has slipped\r\noverboard, or his hat has dropped into the water and is floating\r\nrapidly down stream.\r\n\r\nHe calls to them to stop, quite gently and politely at first.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Hat in the water] \xe2\x80\x9cHi! stop a minute, will you?\xe2\x80\x9d he shouts\r\ncheerily. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve dropped my hat over-board.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen: \xe2\x80\x9cHi! Tom\xe2\x80\x94Dick! can\xe2\x80\x99t you hear?\xe2\x80\x9d not quite so affably this time.\r\n\r\nThen: \xe2\x80\x9cHi! Confound _you_, you dunder-headed idiots! Hi! stop! Oh you\xe2\x80\x94!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter that he springs up, and dances about, and roars himself red in\r\nthe face, and curses everything he knows. And the small boys on the\r\nbank stop and jeer at him, and pitch stones at him as he is pulled\r\nalong past them, at the rate of four miles an hour, and can\xe2\x80\x99t get out.\r\n\r\nMuch of this sort of trouble would be saved if those who are towing\r\nwould keep remembering that they are towing, and give a pretty frequent\r\nlook round to see how their man is getting on. It is best to let one\r\nperson tow. When two are doing it, they get chattering, and forget, and\r\nthe boat itself, offering, as it does, but little resistance, is of no\r\nreal service in reminding them of the fact.\r\n\r\nAs an example of how utterly oblivious a pair of towers can be to their\r\nwork, George told us, later on in the evening, when we were discussing\r\nthe subject after supper, of a very curious instance.\r\n\r\n                [Picture: Two people towing, boat adrift]\r\n\r\nHe and three other men, so he said, were sculling a very heavily laden\r\nboat up from Maidenhead one evening, and a little above Cookham lock\r\nthey noticed a fellow and a girl, walking along the towpath, both deep\r\nin an apparently interesting and absorbing conversation. They were\r\ncarrying a boat-hook between them, and, attached to the boat-hook was\r\na tow-line, which trailed behind them, its end in the water. No boat\r\nwas near, no boat was in sight. There must have been a boat attached\r\nto that tow-line at some time or other, that was certain; but what had\r\nbecome of it, what ghastly fate had overtaken it, and those who had\r\nbeen left in it, was buried in mystery. Whatever the accident may have\r\nbeen, however, it had in no way disturbed the young lady and gentleman,\r\nwho were towing. They had the boat-hook and they had the line, and that\r\nseemed to be all that they thought necessary to their work.\r\n\r\nGeorge was about to call out and wake them up, but, at that moment,\r\na bright idea flashed across him, and he didn\xe2\x80\x99t. He got the hitcher\r\ninstead, and reached over, and drew in the end of the tow-line; and\r\nthey made a loop in it, and put it over their mast, and then they\r\ntidied up the sculls, and went and sat down in the stern, and lit their\r\npipes.\r\n\r\nAnd that young man and young woman towed those four hulking chaps and a\r\nheavy boat up to Marlow.\r\n\r\nGeorge said he never saw so much thoughtful sadness concentrated into\r\none glance before, as when, at the lock, that young couple grasped the\r\nidea that, for the last two miles, they had been towing the wrong boat.\r\nGeorge fancied that, if it had not been for the restraining influence\r\nof the sweet woman at his side, the young man might have given way to\r\nviolent language.\r\n\r\nThe maiden was the first to recover from her surprise, and, when she\r\ndid, she clasped her hands, and said, wildly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Henry, then _where_ is auntie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid they ever recover the old lady?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Harris.\r\n\r\nGeorge replied he did not know.\r\n\r\nAnother example of the dangerous want of sympathy between tower and\r\ntowed was witnessed by George and myself once up near Walton. It was\r\nwhere the tow-path shelves gently down into the water, and we were\r\ncamping on the opposite bank, noticing things in general. By-and-by a\r\nsmall boat came in sight, towed through the water at a tremendous pace\r\nby a powerful barge horse, on which sat a very small boy. Scattered\r\nabout the boat, in dreamy and reposeful attitudes, lay five fellows,\r\nthe man who was steering having a particularly restful appearance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to see him pull the wrong line,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured George, as\r\nthey passed. And at that precise moment the man did it, and the boat\r\nrushed up the bank with a noise like the ripping up of forty thousand\r\nlinen sheets. Two men, a hamper, and three oars immediately left the\r\nboat on the larboard side, and reclined on the bank, and one and a half\r\nmoments afterwards, two other men disembarked from the starboard, and\r\nsat down among boat-hooks and sails and carpet-bags and bottles. The\r\nlast man went on twenty yards further, and then got out on his head.\r\n\r\nThis seemed to sort of lighten the boat, and it went on much easier,\r\nthe small boy shouting at the top of his voice, and urging his steed\r\ninto a gallop. The fellows sat up and stared at one another. It was\r\nsome seconds before they realised what had happened to them, but, when\r\nthey did, they began to shout lustily for the boy to stop. He, however,\r\nwas too much occupied with the horse to hear them, and we watched them,\r\nflying after him, until the distance hid them from view.\r\n\r\nI cannot say I was sorry at their mishap. Indeed, I only wish that all\r\nthe young fools who have their boats towed in this fashion\xe2\x80\x94and plenty\r\ndo\xe2\x80\x94could meet with similar misfortunes. Besides the risk they run\r\nthemselves, they become a danger and an annoyance to every other boat\r\nthey pass. Going at the pace they do, it is impossible for them to get\r\nout of anybody else\xe2\x80\x99s way, or for anybody else to get out of theirs.\r\nTheir line gets hitched across your mast, and overturns you, or it\r\ncatches somebody in the boat, and either throws them into the water,\r\nor cuts their face open. The best plan is to stand your ground, and be\r\nprepared to keep them off with the butt-end of a mast.\r\n\r\nOf all experiences in connection with towing, the most exciting is\r\nbeing towed by girls. It is a sensation that nobody ought to miss. It\r\ntakes three girls to tow always; two hold the rope, and the other one\r\nruns round and round, and giggles. They generally begin by getting\r\nthemselves tied up. They get the line round their legs, and have to\r\nsit down on the path and undo each other, and then they twist it round\r\ntheir necks, and are nearly strangled. They fix it straight, however,\r\nat last, and start off at a run, pulling the boat along at quite a\r\ndangerous pace. At the end of a hundred yards they are naturally\r\nbreathless, and suddenly stop, and all sit down on the grass and laugh,\r\nand your boat drifts out to mid-stream and turns round, before you know\r\nwhat has happened, or can get hold of a scull. Then they stand up, and\r\nare surprised.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, look!\xe2\x80\x9d they say; \xe2\x80\x9che\xe2\x80\x99s gone right out into the middle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Picture: Lady pinning up frock] They pull on pretty steadily for a\r\nbit, after this, and then it all at once occurs to one of them that she\r\nwill pin up her frock, and they ease up for the purpose, and the boat\r\nruns aground.\r\n\r\nYou jump up, and push it off, and you shout to them not to stop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. What\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d they shout back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t stop,\xe2\x80\x9d you roar.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t stop\xe2\x80\x94go on\xe2\x80\x94go on!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo back, Emily, and see what it is they want,\xe2\x80\x9d says one; and Emily\r\ncomes back, and asks what it is.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d she says; \xe2\x80\x9canything happened?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d you reply, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s all right; only go on, you know\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t stop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, we can\xe2\x80\x99t steer, if you keep stopping. You must keep some way on\r\nthe boat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep some what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome way\xe2\x80\x94you must keep the boat moving.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, all right, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell \xe2\x80\x99em. Are we doing it all right?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, very nicely, indeed, only don\xe2\x80\x99t stop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem difficult at all. I thought it was so hard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, it\xe2\x80\x99s simple enough. You want to keep on steady at it, that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see. Give me out my red shawl, it\xe2\x80\x99s under the cushion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYou find the shawl, and hand it out, and by this time another one has\r\ncome back and thinks she will have hers too, and they take Mary\xe2\x80\x99s on\r\nchance, and Mary does not want it, so they bring it back and have a\r\npocket-comb instead. It is about twenty minutes before they get off\r\nagain, and, at the next corner, they see a cow, and you have to leave\r\nthe boat to chivy the cow out of their way.\r\n\r\nThere is never a dull moment in the boat while girls are towing it.\r\n\r\nGeorge got the line right after a while, and towed us steadily on to\r\nPenton Hook. There we discussed the important question of camping. We\r\nhad decided to sleep on board that night, and we had either to lay\r\nup just about there, or go on past Staines. It seemed early to think\r\nabout shutting up then, however, with the sun still in the heavens, and\r\nwe settled to push straight on for Runnymead, three and a half miles\r\nfurther, a quiet wooded part of the river, and where there is good\r\nshelter.\r\n\r\nWe all wished, however, afterward that we had stopped at Penton Hook.\r\nThree or four miles up stream is a trifle, early in the morning, but it\r\nis a weary pull at the end of a long day. You take no interest in the\r\nscenery during these last few miles. You do not chat and laugh. Every\r\nhalf-mile you cover seems like two. You can hardly believe you are only\r\nwhere you are, and you are convinced that the map must be wrong; and,\r\nwhen you have trudged along for what seems to you at least ten miles,\r\nand still the lock is not in sight, you begin to seriously fear that\r\nsomebody must have sneaked it, and run off with it.\r\n\r\nI remember being terribly upset once up the river (in a figurative\r\nsense, I mean). I was out with a young lady\xe2\x80\x94cousin on my mother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nside\xe2\x80\x94and we were pulling down to Goring. It was rather late, and we\r\nwere anxious to get in\xe2\x80\x94at least _she_ was anxious to get in. It was\r\nhalf-past six when we reached Benson\xe2\x80\x99s lock, and dusk was drawing on,\r\nand she began to get excited then. She said she must be in to supper.\r\nI said it was a thing I felt I wanted to be in at, too; and I drew out\r\na map I had with me to see exactly how far it was. I saw it was just a\r\nmile and a half to the next lock\xe2\x80\x94Wallingford\xe2\x80\x94and five on from there to\r\nCleeve.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s all right!\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll be through the next lock before\r\nseven, and then there is only one more;\xe2\x80\x9d and I settled down and pulled\r\nsteadily away.\r\n\r\nWe passed the bridge, and soon after that I asked if she saw the lock.\r\nShe said no, she did not see any lock; and I said, \xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d and pulled on.\r\nAnother five minutes went by, and then I asked her to look again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t see any signs of a lock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x94you are sure you know a lock, when you do see one?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked\r\nhesitatingly, not wishing to offend her.\r\n\r\nThe question did offend her, however, and she suggested that I had\r\nbetter look for myself; so I laid down the sculls, and took a view.\r\nThe river stretched out straight before us in the twilight for about a\r\nmile; not a ghost of a lock was to be seen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t think we have lost our way, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d asked my companion.\r\n\r\nI did not see how that was possible; though, as I suggested, we might\r\nhave somehow got into the weir stream, and be making for the falls.\r\n\r\nThis idea did not comfort her in the least, and she began to cry. She\r\nsaid we should both be drowned, and that it was a judgment on her for\r\ncoming out with me.\r\n\r\nIt seemed an excessive punishment, I thought; but my cousin thought\r\nnot, and hoped it would all soon be over.\r\n\r\nI tried to reassure her, and to make light of the whole affair. I said\r\nthat the fact evidently was that I was not rowing as fast as I fancied\r\nI was, but that we should soon reach the lock now; and I pulled on for\r\nanother mile.\r\n\r\nThen I began to get nervous myself. I looked again at the map. There\r\nwas Wallingford lock, clearly marked, a mile and a half below Benson\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\nIt was a good, reliable map; and, besides, I recollected the lock\r\nmyself. I had been through it twice. Where were we? What had happened\r\nto us? I began to think it must be all a dream, and that I was really\r\nasleep in bed, and should wake up in a minute, and be told it was past\r\nten.\r\n\r\nI asked my cousin if she thought it could be a dream, and she replied\r\nthat she was just about to ask me the same question; and then we both\r\nwondered if we were both asleep, and if so, who was the real one that\r\nwas dreaming, and who was the one that was only a dream; it got quite\r\ninteresting.\r\n\r\nI still went on pulling, however, and still no lock came in sight, and\r\nthe river grew more and more gloomy and mysterious under the gathering\r\nshadows of night, and things seemed to be getting weird and uncanny. I\r\nthought of hobgoblins and banshees, and will-o\xe2\x80\x99-the-wisps, and those\r\nwicked girls who sit up all night on rocks, and lure people into\r\nwhirl-pools and things; and I wished I had been a better man, and knew\r\nmore hymns; and in the middle of these reflections I heard the blessed\r\nstrains of \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s got \xe2\x80\x99em on,\xe2\x80\x9d played, badly, on a concertina, and knew\r\nthat we were saved.\r\n\r\nI do not admire the tones of a concertina, as a rule; but, oh! how\r\nbeautiful the music seemed to us both then\xe2\x80\x94far, far more beautiful\r\nthan the voice of Orpheus or the lute of Apollo, or anything of that\r\nsort could have sounded. Heavenly melody, in our then state of mind,\r\nwould only have still further harrowed us. A soul-moving harmony,\r\ncorrectly performed, we should have taken as a spirit-warning, and\r\nhave given up all hope. But about the strains of \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s got \xe2\x80\x99em on,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\njerked spasmodically, and with involuntary variations, out of a wheezy\r\naccordion, there was something singularly human and reassuring.\r\n\r\nThe sweet sounds drew nearer, and soon the boat from which they were\r\nworked lay alongside us.\r\n\r\nIt contained a party of provincial \xe2\x80\x99Arrys and \xe2\x80\x99Arriets, out for a\r\nmoonlight sail. (There was not any moon, but that was not their fault.)\r\nI never saw more attractive, lovable people in all my life. I hailed\r\nthem, and asked if they could tell me the way to Wallingford lock; and\r\nI explained that I had been looking for it for the last two hours.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWallingford lock!\xe2\x80\x9d they answered. \xe2\x80\x9cLor\xe2\x80\x99 love you, sir, that\xe2\x80\x99s been\r\ndone away with for over a year. There ain\xe2\x80\x99t no Wallingford lock\r\nnow, sir. You\xe2\x80\x99re close to Cleeve now. Blow me tight if \xe2\x80\x99ere ain\xe2\x80\x99t a\r\ngentleman been looking for Wallingford lock, Bill!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had never thought of that. I wanted to fall upon all their necks and\r\nbless them; but the stream was running too strong just there to allow\r\nof this, so I had to content myself with mere cold-sounding words of\r\ngratitude.\r\n\r\nWe thanked them over and over again, and we said it was a lovely night,\r\nand we wished them a pleasant trip, and, I think, I invited them all\r\nto come and spend a week with me, and my cousin said her mother would\r\nbe so pleased to see them. And we sang the soldiers\xe2\x80\x99 chorus out of\r\n_Faust_, and got home in time for supper, after all.\r\n\r\n                     [Picture: People in rowing boat]\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X.\r\n\r\n\r\nOur first night.\xe2\x80\x94Under canvas.\xe2\x80\x94An appeal for help.\xe2\x80\x94Contrariness of\r\ntea-kettles, how to overcome.\xe2\x80\x94Supper.\xe2\x80\x94How to feel virtuous.\xe2\x80\x94Wanted! a\r\ncomfortably-appointed, well-drained desert island, neighbourhood of\r\nSouth Pacific Ocean preferred.\xe2\x80\x94Funny thing that happened to George\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfather.\xe2\x80\x94a restless night.\r\n\r\nHarris and I began to think that Bell Weir lock must have been done\r\naway with after the same manner. George had towed us up to Staines, and\r\nwe had taken the boat from there, and it seemed that we were dragging\r\nfifty tons after us, and were walking forty miles. It was half-past\r\nseven when we were through, and we all got in, and sculled up close to\r\nthe left bank, looking out for a spot to haul up in.\r\n\r\nWe had originally intended to go on to Magna Charta Island, a sweetly\r\npretty part of the river, where it winds through a soft, green valley,\r\nand to camp in one of the many picturesque inlets to be found round\r\nthat tiny shore. But, somehow, we did not feel that we yearned for the\r\npicturesque nearly so much now as we had earlier in the day. A bit of\r\nwater between a coal-barge and a gas-works would have quite satisfied\r\nus for that night. We did not want scenery. We wanted to have our\r\nsupper and go to bed. However, we did pull up to the point\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cPicnic\r\nPoint,\xe2\x80\x9d it is called\xe2\x80\x94and dropped into a very pleasant nook under a\r\ngreat elm-tree, to the spreading roots of which we fastened the boat.\r\n\r\nThen we thought we were going to have supper (we had dispensed with\r\ntea, so as to save time), but George said no; that we had better get\r\nthe canvas up first, before it got quite dark, and while we could see\r\nwhat we were doing. Then, he said, all our work would be done, and we\r\ncould sit down to eat with an easy mind.\r\n\r\nThat canvas wanted more putting up than I think any of us had bargained\r\nfor. It looked so simple in the abstract. You took five iron arches,\r\nlike gigantic croquet hoops, and fitted them up over the boat, and then\r\nstretched the canvas over them, and fastened it down: it would take\r\nquite ten minutes, we thought.\r\n\r\nThat was an under-estimate.\r\n\r\nWe took up the hoops, and began to drop them into the sockets placed\r\nfor them. You would not imagine this to be dangerous work; but, looking\r\nback now, the wonder to me is that any of us are alive to tell the\r\ntale. They were not hoops, they were demons. First they would not fit\r\ninto their sockets at all, and we had to jump on them, and kick them,\r\nand hammer at them with the boat-hook; and, when they were in, it\r\nturned out that they were the wrong hoops for those particular sockets,\r\nand they had to come out again.\r\n\r\nBut they would not come out, until two of us had gone and struggled\r\nwith them for five minutes, when they would jump up suddenly, and\r\ntry and throw us into the water and drown us. They had hinges in the\r\nmiddle, and, when we were not looking, they nipped us with these hinges\r\nin delicate parts of the body; and, while we were wrestling with one\r\nside of the hoop, and endeavouring to persuade it to do its duty, the\r\nother side would come behind us in a cowardly manner, and hit us over\r\nthe head.\r\n\r\nWe got them fixed at last, and then all that was to be done was to\r\narrange the covering over them. George unrolled it, and fastened one\r\nend over the nose of the boat. Harris stood in the middle to take it\r\nfrom George and roll it on to me, and I kept by the stern to receive\r\nit. It was a long time coming down to me. George did his part all\r\nright, but it was new work to Harris, and he bungled it.\r\n\r\nHow he managed it I do not know, he could not explain himself; but\r\nby some mysterious process or other he succeeded, after ten minutes\r\nof superhuman effort, in getting himself completely rolled up in\r\nit. He was so firmly wrapped round and tucked in and folded over,\r\nthat he could not get out. He, of course, made frantic struggles for\r\nfreedom\xe2\x80\x94the birthright of every Englishman,\xe2\x80\x94and, in doing so (I learned\r\nthis afterwards), knocked over George; and then George, swearing at\r\nHarris, began to struggle too, and got _himself_ entangled and rolled\r\nup.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Watching and waiting] I knew nothing about all this at the\r\ntime. I did not understand the business at all myself. I had been\r\ntold to stand where I was, and wait till the canvas came to me, and\r\nMontmorency and I stood there and waited, both as good as gold. We\r\ncould see the canvas being violently jerked and tossed about, pretty\r\nconsiderably; but we supposed this was part of the method, and did not\r\ninterfere.\r\n\r\nWe also heard much smothered language coming from underneath it, and\r\nwe guessed that they were finding the job rather troublesome, and\r\nconcluded that we would wait until things had got a little simpler\r\nbefore we joined in.\r\n\r\nWe waited some time, but matters seemed to get only more and more\r\ninvolved, until, at last, George\xe2\x80\x99s head came wriggling out over the\r\nside of the boat, and spoke up.\r\n\r\nIt said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive us a hand here, can\xe2\x80\x99t you, you cuckoo; standing there like a\r\nstuffed mummy, when you see we are both being suffocated, you dummy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI never could withstand an appeal for help, so I went and undid them;\r\nnot before it was time, either, for Harris was nearly black in the face.\r\n\r\nIt took us half an hour\xe2\x80\x99s hard labour, after that, before it was\r\nproperly up, and then we cleared the decks, and got out supper. We put\r\nthe kettle on to boil, up in the nose of the boat, and went down to the\r\nstern and pretended to take no notice of it, but set to work to get the\r\nother things out.\r\n\r\nThat is the only way to get a kettle to boil up the river. If it sees\r\nthat you are waiting for it and are anxious, it will never even sing.\r\nYou have to go away and begin your meal, as if you were not going to\r\nhave any tea at all. You must not even look round at it. Then you will\r\nsoon hear it sputtering away, mad to be made into tea.\r\n\r\nIt is a good plan, too, if you are in a great hurry, to talk very\r\nloudly to each other about how you don\xe2\x80\x99t need any tea, and are not\r\ngoing to have any. You get near the kettle, so that it can overhear\r\nyou, and then you shout out, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want any tea; do you, George?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nto which George shouts back, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, I don\xe2\x80\x99t like tea; we\xe2\x80\x99ll have\r\nlemonade instead\xe2\x80\x94tea\xe2\x80\x99s so indigestible.\xe2\x80\x9d Upon which the kettle boils\r\nover, and puts the stove out.\r\n\r\nWe adopted this harmless bit of trickery, and the result was that, by\r\nthe time everything else was ready, the tea was waiting. Then we lit\r\nthe lantern, and squatted down to supper.\r\n\r\nWe wanted that supper.\r\n\r\nFor five-and-thirty minutes not a sound was heard throughout the\r\nlength and breadth of that boat, save the clank of cutlery and\r\ncrockery, and the steady grinding of four sets of molars. At the end of\r\nfive-and-thirty minutes, Harris said, \xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d and took his left leg out\r\nfrom under him and put his right one there instead.\r\n\r\nFive minutes afterwards, George said, \xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d too, and threw his plate\r\nout on the bank; and, three minutes later than that, Montmorency gave\r\nthe first sign of contentment he had exhibited since we had started,\r\nand rolled over on his side, and spread his legs out; and then I said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d and bent my head back, and bumped it against one of the hoops,\r\nbut I did not mind it. I did not even swear.\r\n\r\nHow good one feels when one is full\xe2\x80\x94how satisfied with ourselves\r\nand with the world! People who have tried it, tell me that a clear\r\nconscience makes you very happy and contented; but a full stomach\r\ndoes the business quite as well, and is cheaper, and more easily\r\nobtained. One feels so forgiving and generous after a substantial and\r\nwell-digested meal\xe2\x80\x94so noble-minded, so kindly-hearted.\r\n\r\nIt is very strange, this domination of our intellect by our digestive\r\norgans. We cannot work, we cannot think, unless our stomach wills so.\r\nIt dictates to us our emotions, our passions. After eggs and bacon,\r\nit says, \xe2\x80\x9cWork!\xe2\x80\x9d After beefsteak and porter, it says, \xe2\x80\x9cSleep!\xe2\x80\x9d After\r\na cup of tea (two spoonsful for each cup, and don\xe2\x80\x99t let it stand\r\nmore than three minutes), it says to the brain, \xe2\x80\x9cNow, rise, and show\r\nyour strength. Be eloquent, and deep, and tender; see, with a clear\r\neye, into Nature and into life; spread your white wings of quivering\r\nthought, and soar, a god-like spirit, over the whirling world beneath\r\nyou, up through long lanes of flaming stars to the gates of eternity!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter hot muffins, it says, \xe2\x80\x9cBe dull and soulless, like a beast of the\r\nfield\xe2\x80\x94a brainless animal, with listless eye, unlit by any ray of fancy,\r\nor of hope, or fear, or love, or life.\xe2\x80\x9d And after brandy, taken in\r\nsufficient quantity, it says, \xe2\x80\x9cNow, come, fool, grin and tumble, that\r\nyour fellow-men may laugh\xe2\x80\x94drivel in folly, and splutter in senseless\r\nsounds, and show what a helpless ninny is poor man whose wit and will\r\nare drowned, like kittens, side by side, in half an inch of alcohol.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe are but the veriest, sorriest slaves of our stomach. Reach not after\r\nmorality and righteousness, my friends; watch vigilantly your stomach,\r\nand diet it with care and judgment. Then virtue and contentment will\r\ncome and reign within your heart, unsought by any effort of your own;\r\nand you will be a good citizen, a loving husband, and a tender father\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nnoble, pious man.\r\n\r\nBefore our supper, Harris and George and I were quarrelsome and\r\nsnappy and ill-tempered; after our supper, we sat and beamed on one\r\nanother, and we beamed upon the dog, too. We loved each other, we loved\r\neverybody. Harris, in moving about, trod on George\xe2\x80\x99s corn. Had this\r\nhappened before supper, George would have expressed wishes and desires\r\nconcerning Harris\xe2\x80\x99s fate in this world and the next that would have\r\nmade a thoughtful man shudder.\r\n\r\nAs it was, he said: \xe2\x80\x9cSteady, old man; \xe2\x80\x99ware wheat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Harris, instead of merely observing, in his most unpleasant tones,\r\nthat a fellow could hardly help treading on some bit of George\xe2\x80\x99s foot,\r\nif he had to move about at all within ten yards of where George was\r\nsitting, suggesting that George never ought to come into an ordinary\r\nsized boat with feet that length, and advising him to hang them over\r\nthe side, as he would have done before supper, now said: \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m so\r\nsorry, old chap; I hope I haven\xe2\x80\x99t hurt you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Picture: Smoking pipes] And George said: \xe2\x80\x9cNot at all;\xe2\x80\x9d that it was his\r\nfault; and Harris said no, it was his.\r\n\r\nIt was quite pretty to hear them.\r\n\r\nWe lit our pipes, and sat, looking out on the quiet night, and talked.\r\n\r\nGeorge said why could not we be always like this\xe2\x80\x94away from the world,\r\nwith its sin and temptation, leading sober, peaceful lives, and doing\r\ngood. I said it was the sort of thing I had often longed for myself;\r\nand we discussed the possibility of our going away, we four, to some\r\nhandy, well-fitted desert island, and living there in the woods.\r\n\r\nHarris said that the danger about desert islands, as far as he had\r\nheard, was that they were so damp: but George said no, not if properly\r\ndrained.\r\n\r\nAnd then we got on to drains, and that put George in mind of a very\r\nfunny thing that happened to his father once. He said his father was\r\ntravelling with another fellow through Wales, and, one night, they\r\nstopped at a little inn, where there were some other fellows, and they\r\njoined the other fellows, and spent the evening with them.\r\n\r\nThey had a very jolly evening, and sat up late, and, by the time they\r\ncame to go to bed, they (this was when George\xe2\x80\x99s father was a very young\r\nman) were slightly jolly, too. They (George\xe2\x80\x99s father and George\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s friend) were to sleep in the same room, but in different beds.\r\nThey took the candle, and went up. The candle lurched up against the\r\nwall when they got into the room, and went out, and they had to undress\r\nand grope into bed in the dark. This they did; but, instead of getting\r\ninto separate beds, as they thought they were doing, they both climbed\r\ninto the same one without knowing it\xe2\x80\x94one getting in with his head\r\nat the top, and the other crawling in from the opposite side of the\r\ncompass, and lying with his feet on the pillow.\r\n\r\nThere was silence for a moment, and then George\xe2\x80\x99s father said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJoe!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d replied Joe\xe2\x80\x99s voice from the other end of the\r\nbed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, there\xe2\x80\x99s a man in my bed,\xe2\x80\x9d said George\xe2\x80\x99s father; \xe2\x80\x9chere\xe2\x80\x99s his feet\r\non my pillow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s an extraordinary thing, Tom,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the other; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nblest if there isn\xe2\x80\x99t a man in my bed, too!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you going to do?\xe2\x80\x9d asked George\xe2\x80\x99s father.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99m going to chuck him out,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Joe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo am I,\xe2\x80\x9d said George\xe2\x80\x99s father, valiantly.\r\n\r\nThere was a brief struggle, followed by two heavy bumps on the floor,\r\nand then a rather doleful voice said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, Tom!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow have you got on?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, to tell you the truth, my man\xe2\x80\x99s chucked _me_ out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo\xe2\x80\x99s mine! I say, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think much of this inn, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was the name of that inn?\xe2\x80\x9d said Harris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Pig and Whistle,\xe2\x80\x9d said George. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, no, then it isn\xe2\x80\x99t the same,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Harris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d queried George.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy it\xe2\x80\x99s so curious,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Harris, \xe2\x80\x9cbut precisely that very same\r\nthing happened to _my_ father once at a country inn. I\xe2\x80\x99ve often heard\r\nhim tell the tale. I thought it might have been the same inn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe turned in at ten that night, and I thought I should sleep well,\r\nbeing tired; but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t. As a rule, I undress and put my head on the\r\npillow, and then somebody bangs at the door, and says it is half-past\r\neight: but, to-night, everything seemed against me; the novelty of\r\nit all, the hardness of the boat, the cramped position (I was lying\r\nwith my feet under one seat, and my head on another), the sound of the\r\nlapping water round the boat, and the wind among the branches, kept me\r\nrestless and disturbed.\r\n\r\nI did get to sleep for a few hours, and then some part of the boat\r\nwhich seemed to have grown up in the night\xe2\x80\x94for it certainly was not\r\nthere when we started, and it had disappeared by the morning\xe2\x80\x94kept\r\ndigging into my spine. I slept through it for a while, dreaming that I\r\nhad swallowed a sovereign, and that they were cutting a hole in my back\r\nwith a gimlet, so as to try and get it out. I thought it very unkind\r\nof them, and I told them I would owe them the money, and they should\r\nhave it at the end of the month. But they would not hear of that, and\r\nsaid it would be much better if they had it then, because otherwise the\r\ninterest would accumulate so. I got quite cross with them after a bit,\r\nand told them what I thought of them, and then they gave the gimlet\r\nsuch an excruciating wrench that I woke up.\r\n\r\nThe boat seemed stuffy, and my head ached; so I thought I would step\r\nout into the cool night-air. I slipped on what clothes I could find\r\nabout\xe2\x80\x94some of my own, and some of George\xe2\x80\x99s and Harris\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94and crept under\r\nthe canvas on to the bank.\r\n\r\nIt was a glorious night. The moon had sunk, and left the quiet earth\r\nalone with the stars. It seemed as if, in the silence and the hush,\r\nwhile we her children slept, they were talking with her, their\r\nsister\xe2\x80\x94conversing of mighty mysteries in voices too vast and deep for\r\nchildish human ears to catch the sound.\r\n\r\nThey awe us, these strange stars, so cold, so clear. We are as children\r\nwhose small feet have strayed into some dim-lit temple of the god they\r\nhave been taught to worship but know not; and, standing where the\r\nechoing dome spans the long vista of the shadowy light, glance up, half\r\nhoping, half afraid to see some awful vision hovering there.\r\n\r\nAnd yet it seems so full of comfort and of strength, the night. In its\r\ngreat presence, our small sorrows creep away, ashamed. The day has been\r\nso full of fret and care, and our hearts have been so full of evil and\r\nof bitter thoughts, and the world has seemed so hard and wrong to us.\r\nThen Night, like some great loving mother, gently lays her hand upon\r\nour fevered head, and turns our little tear-stained faces up to hers,\r\nand smiles; and, though she does not speak, we know what she would say,\r\nand lay our hot flushed cheek against her bosom, and the pain is gone.\r\n\r\nSometimes, our pain is very deep and real, and we stand before her very\r\nsilent, because there is no language for our pain, only a moan. Night\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nheart is full of pity for us: she cannot ease our aching; she takes our\r\nhand in hers, and the little world grows very small and very far away\r\nbeneath us, and, borne on her dark wings, we pass for a moment into a\r\nmightier Presence than her own, and in the wondrous light of that great\r\nPresence, all human life lies like a book before us, and we know that\r\nPain and Sorrow are but the angels of God.\r\n\r\nOnly those who have worn the crown of suffering can look upon that\r\nwondrous light; and they, when they return, may not speak of it, or\r\ntell the mystery they know.\r\n\r\nOnce upon a time, through a strange country, there rode some goodly\r\nknights, and their path lay by a deep wood, where tangled briars grew\r\nvery thick and strong, and tore the flesh of them that lost their way\r\ntherein. And the leaves of the trees that grew in the wood were very\r\ndark and thick, so that no ray of light came through the branches to\r\nlighten the gloom and sadness.\r\n\r\nAnd, as they passed by that dark wood, one knight of those that rode,\r\nmissing his comrades, wandered far away, and returned to them no more;\r\nand they, sorely grieving, rode on without him, mourning him as one\r\ndead.\r\n\r\nNow, when they reached the fair castle towards which they had been\r\njourneying, they stayed there many days, and made merry; and one night,\r\nas they sat in cheerful ease around the logs that burned in the great\r\nhall, and drank a loving measure, there came the comrade they had lost,\r\nand greeted them. His clothes were ragged, like a beggar\xe2\x80\x99s, and many\r\nsad wounds were on his sweet flesh, but upon his face there shone a\r\ngreat radiance of deep joy.\r\n\r\nAnd they questioned him, asking him what had befallen him: and he told\r\nthem how in the dark wood he had lost his way, and had wandered many\r\ndays and nights, till, torn and bleeding, he had lain him down to die.\r\n\r\nThen, when he was nigh unto death, lo! through the savage gloom there\r\ncame to him a stately maiden, and took him by the hand and led him on\r\nthrough devious paths, unknown to any man, until upon the darkness of\r\nthe wood there dawned a light such as the light of day was unto but as\r\na little lamp unto the sun; and, in that wondrous light, our way-worn\r\nknight saw as in a dream a vision, and so glorious, so fair the vision\r\nseemed, that of his bleeding wounds he thought no more, but stood as\r\none entranced, whose joy is deep as is the sea, whereof no man can tell\r\nthe depth.\r\n\r\nAnd the vision faded, and the knight, kneeling upon the ground, thanked\r\nthe good saint who into that sad wood had strayed his steps, so he had\r\nseen the vision that lay there hid.\r\n\r\nAnd the name of the dark forest was Sorrow; but of the vision that the\r\ngood knight saw therein we may not speak nor tell.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI.\r\n\r\n\r\nHow George, once upon a time, got up early in the morning.\xe2\x80\x94George,\r\nHarris, and Montmorency do not like the look of the cold water.\xe2\x80\x94Heroism\r\nand determination on the part of J.\xe2\x80\x94George and his shirt: story with a\r\nmoral.\xe2\x80\x94Harris as cook.\xe2\x80\x94Historical retrospect, specially inserted for\r\nthe use of schools.\r\n\r\nI woke at six the next morning; and found George awake too. We both\r\nturned round, and tried to go to sleep again, but we could not. Had\r\nthere been any particular reason why we should not have gone to sleep\r\nagain, but have got up and dressed then and there, we should have\r\ndropped off while we were looking at our watches, and have slept till\r\nten. As there was no earthly necessity for our getting up under another\r\ntwo hours at the very least, and our getting up at that time was an\r\nutter absurdity, it was only in keeping with the natural cussedness of\r\nthings in general that we should both feel that lying down for five\r\nminutes more would be death to us.\r\n\r\nGeorge said that the same kind of thing, only worse, had happened to\r\nhim some eighteen months ago, when he was lodging by himself in the\r\nhouse of a certain Mrs. Gippings. He said his watch went wrong one\r\nevening, and stopped at a quarter-past eight. He did not know this at\r\nthe time because, for some reason or other, he forgot to wind it up\r\nwhen he went to bed (an unusual occurrence with him), and hung it up\r\nover his pillow without ever looking at the thing.\r\n\r\nIt was in the winter when this happened, very near the shortest day,\r\nand a week of fog into the bargain, so the fact that it was still very\r\ndark when George woke in the morning was no guide to him as to the\r\ntime. He reached up, and hauled down his watch. It was a quarter-past\r\neight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAngels and ministers of grace defend us!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed George; \xe2\x80\x9cand here\r\nhave I got to be in the City by nine. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t somebody call me?\r\nOh, this is a shame!\xe2\x80\x9d And he flung the watch down, and sprang out of\r\nbed, and had a cold bath, and washed himself, and dressed himself, and\r\nshaved himself in cold water because there was not time to wait for the\r\nhot, and then rushed and had another look at the watch.\r\n\r\nWhether the shaking it had received in being thrown down on the bed had\r\nstarted it, or how it was, George could not say, but certain it was\r\nthat from a quarter-past eight it had begun to go, and now pointed to\r\ntwenty minutes to nine.\r\n\r\nGeorge snatched it up, and rushed downstairs. In the sitting-room, all\r\nwas dark and silent: there was no fire, no breakfast. George said it\r\nwas a wicked shame of Mrs. G., and he made up his mind to tell her what\r\nhe thought of her when he came home in the evening. Then he dashed on\r\nhis great-coat and hat, and, seizing his umbrella, made for the front\r\ndoor. The door was not even unbolted. George anathematized Mrs. G. for\r\na lazy old woman, and thought it was very strange that people could not\r\nget up at a decent, respectable time, unlocked and unbolted the door,\r\nand ran out.\r\n\r\nHe ran hard for a quarter of a mile, and at the end of that distance\r\nit began to be borne in upon him as a strange and curious thing that\r\nthere were so few people about, and that there were no shops open. It\r\nwas certainly a very dark and foggy morning, but still it seemed an\r\nunusual course to stop all business on that account. _He_ had to go to\r\nbusiness: why should other people stop in bed merely because it was\r\ndark and foggy!\r\n\r\nAt length he reached Holborn. Not a shutter was down! not a bus was\r\nabout! There were three men in sight, one of whom was a policeman; a\r\nmarket-cart full of cabbages, and a dilapidated looking cab. George\r\npulled out his watch and looked at it: it was five minutes to nine! He\r\nstood still and counted his pulse. He stooped down and felt his legs.\r\nThen, with his watch still in his hand, he went up to the policeman,\r\nand asked him if he knew what the time was.\r\n\r\n[Picture: George and the policeman] \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the time?\xe2\x80\x9d said the man,\r\neyeing George up and down with evident suspicion; \xe2\x80\x9cwhy, if you listen\r\nyou will hear it strike.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGeorge listened, and a neighbouring clock immediately obliged.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it\xe2\x80\x99s only gone three!\xe2\x80\x9d said George in an injured tone, when it had\r\nfinished.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, and how many did you want it to go?\xe2\x80\x9d replied the constable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, nine,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, showing his watch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know where you live?\xe2\x80\x9d said the guardian of public order,\r\nseverely.\r\n\r\nGeorge thought, and gave the address.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! that\xe2\x80\x99s where it is, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d replied the man; \xe2\x80\x9cwell, you take my\r\nadvice and go there quietly, and take that watch of yours with you; and\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t let\xe2\x80\x99s have any more of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd George went home again, musing as he walked along, and let himself\r\nin.\r\n\r\nAt first, when he got in, he determined to undress and go to bed again;\r\nbut when he thought of the redressing and re-washing, and the having of\r\nanother bath, he determined he would not, but would sit up and go to\r\nsleep in the easy-chair.\r\n\r\nBut he could not get to sleep: he never felt more wakeful in his life;\r\nso he lit the lamp and got out the chess-board, and played himself\r\na game of chess. But even that did not enliven him: it seemed slow\r\nsomehow; so he gave chess up and tried to read. He did not seem able\r\nto take any sort of interest in reading either, so he put on his coat\r\nagain and went out for a walk.\r\n\r\nIt was horribly lonesome and dismal, and all the policemen he met\r\nregarded him with undisguised suspicion, and turned their lanterns on\r\nhim and followed him about, and this had such an effect upon him at\r\nlast that he began to feel as if he really had done something, and he\r\ngot to slinking down the by-streets and hiding in dark doorways when he\r\nheard the regulation flip-flop approaching.\r\n\r\nOf course, this conduct made the force only more distrustful of him\r\nthan ever, and they would come and rout him out and ask him what he was\r\ndoing there; and when he answered, \xe2\x80\x9cNothing,\xe2\x80\x9d he had merely come out\r\nfor a stroll (it was then four o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning), they looked\r\nas though they did not believe him, and two plain-clothes constables\r\ncame home with him to see if he really did live where he had said he\r\ndid. They saw him go in with his key, and then they took up a position\r\nopposite and watched the house.\r\n\r\nHe thought he would light the fire when he got inside, and make himself\r\nsome breakfast, just to pass away the time; but he did not seem able\r\nto handle anything from a scuttleful of coals to a teaspoon without\r\ndropping it or falling over it, and making such a noise that he was in\r\nmortal fear that it would wake Mrs. G. up, and that she would think it\r\nwas burglars and open the window and call \xe2\x80\x9cPolice!\xe2\x80\x9d and then these two\r\ndetectives would rush in and handcuff him, and march him off to the\r\npolice-court.\r\n\r\nHe was in a morbidly nervous state by this time, and he pictured the\r\ntrial, and his trying to explain the circumstances to the jury, and\r\nnobody believing him, and his being sentenced to twenty years\xe2\x80\x99 penal\r\nservitude, and his mother dying of a broken heart. So he gave up trying\r\nto get breakfast, and wrapped himself up in his overcoat and sat in the\r\neasy-chair till Mrs. G came down at half-past seven.\r\n\r\nHe said he had never got up too early since that morning: it had been\r\nsuch a warning to him.\r\n\r\nWe had been sitting huddled up in our rugs while George had been\r\ntelling me this true story, and on his finishing it I set to work to\r\nwake up Harris with a scull. The third prod did it: and he turned over\r\non the other side, and said he would be down in a minute, and that\r\nhe would have his lace-up boots. We soon let him know where he was,\r\nhowever, by the aid of the hitcher, and he sat up suddenly, sending\r\nMontmorency, who had been sleeping the sleep of the just right on the\r\nmiddle of his chest, sprawling across the boat.\r\n\r\nThen we pulled up the canvas, and all four of us poked our heads out\r\nover the off-side, and looked down at the water and shivered. The idea,\r\novernight, had been that we should get up early in the morning, fling\r\noff our rugs and shawls, and, throwing back the canvas, spring into the\r\nriver with a joyous shout, and revel in a long delicious swim. Somehow,\r\nnow the morning had come, the notion seemed less tempting. The water\r\nlooked damp and chilly: the wind felt cold.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, who\xe2\x80\x99s going to be first in?\xe2\x80\x9d said Harris at last.\r\n\r\nThere was no rush for precedence. George settled the matter so far as\r\nhe was concerned by retiring into the boat and pulling on his socks.\r\nMontmorency gave vent to an involuntary howl, as if merely thinking of\r\nthe thing had given him the horrors; and Harris said it would be so\r\ndifficult to get into the boat again, and went back and sorted out his\r\ntrousers.\r\n\r\nI did not altogether like to give in, though I did not relish the\r\nplunge. There might be snags about, or weeds, I thought. I meant to\r\ncompromise matters by going down to the edge and just throwing the\r\nwater over myself; so I took a towel and crept out on the bank and\r\nwormed my way along on to the branch of a tree that dipped down into\r\nthe water.\r\n\r\n[Picture: In the Thames] It was bitterly cold. The wind cut like a\r\nknife. I thought I would not throw the water over myself after all. I\r\nwould go back into the boat and dress; and I turned to do so; and, as I\r\nturned, the silly branch gave way, and I and the towel went in together\r\nwith a tremendous splash, and I was out mid-stream with a gallon of\r\nThames water inside me before I knew what had happened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove! old J.\xe2\x80\x99s gone in,\xe2\x80\x9d I heard Harris say, as I came blowing to\r\nthe surface. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t think he\xe2\x80\x99d have the pluck to do it. Did you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it all right?\xe2\x80\x9d sung out George.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLovely,\xe2\x80\x9d I spluttered back. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are duffers not to come in. I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have missed this for worlds. Why won\xe2\x80\x99t you try it? It only\r\nwants a little determination.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut I could not persuade them.\r\n\r\nRather an amusing thing happened while dressing that morning. I was\r\nvery cold when I got back into the boat, and, in my hurry to get my\r\nshirt on, I accidentally jerked it into the water. It made me awfully\r\nwild, especially as George burst out laughing. I could not see anything\r\nto laugh at, and I told George so, and he only laughed the more. I\r\nnever saw a man laugh so much. I quite lost my temper with him at last,\r\nand I pointed out to him what a drivelling maniac of an imbecile idiot\r\nhe was; but he only roared the louder. And then, just as I was landing\r\nthe shirt, I noticed that it was not my shirt at all, but George\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\nwhich I had mistaken for mine; whereupon the humour of the thing struck\r\nme for the first time, and I began to laugh. And the more I looked from\r\nGeorge\xe2\x80\x99s wet shirt to George, roaring with laughter, the more I was\r\namused, and I laughed so much that I had to let the shirt fall back\r\ninto the water again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAr\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t you\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x94going to get it out?\xe2\x80\x9d said George, between his shrieks.\r\n\r\nI could not answer him at all for a while, I was laughing so, but, at\r\nlast, between my peals I managed to jerk out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t my shirt\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s _yours_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI never saw a man\xe2\x80\x99s face change from lively to severe so suddenly in\r\nall my life before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat!\xe2\x80\x9d he yelled, springing up. \xe2\x80\x9cYou silly cuckoo! Why can\xe2\x80\x99t you be\r\nmore careful what you\xe2\x80\x99re doing? Why the deuce don\xe2\x80\x99t you go and dress\r\non the bank? You\xe2\x80\x99re not fit to be in a boat, you\xe2\x80\x99re not. Gimme the\r\nhitcher.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI tried to make him see the fun of the thing, but he could not. George\r\nis very dense at seeing a joke sometimes.\r\n\r\nHarris proposed that we should have scrambled eggs for breakfast. He\r\nsaid he would cook them. It seemed, from his account, that he was very\r\ngood at doing scrambled eggs. He often did them at picnics and when out\r\non yachts. He was quite famous for them. People who had once tasted his\r\nscrambled eggs, so we gathered from his conversation, never cared for\r\nany other food afterwards, but pined away and died when they could not\r\nget them.\r\n\r\nIt made our mouths water to hear him talk about the things, and we\r\nhanded him out the stove and the frying-pan and all the eggs that had\r\nnot smashed and gone over everything in the hamper, and begged him to\r\nbegin.\r\n\r\nHe had some trouble in breaking the eggs\xe2\x80\x94or rather not so much trouble\r\nin breaking them exactly as in getting them into the frying-pan when\r\nbroken, and keeping them off his trousers, and preventing them from\r\nrunning up his sleeve; but he fixed some half-a-dozen into the pan at\r\nlast, and then squatted down by the side of the stove and chivied them\r\nabout with a fork.\r\n\r\nIt seemed harassing work, so far as George and I could judge. Whenever\r\nhe went near the pan he burned himself, and then he would drop\r\neverything and dance round the stove, flicking his fingers about and\r\ncursing the things. Indeed, every time George and I looked round at him\r\nhe was sure to be performing this feat. We thought at first that it was\r\na necessary part of the culinary arrangements.\r\n\r\nWe did not know what scrambled eggs were, and we fancied that it must\r\nbe some Red Indian or Sandwich Islands sort of dish that required\r\ndances and incantations for its proper cooking. Montmorency went and\r\nput his nose over it once, and the fat spluttered up and scalded him,\r\nand then _he_ began dancing and cursing. Altogether it was one of the\r\nmost interesting and exciting operations I have ever witnessed. George\r\nand I were both quite sorry when it was over.\r\n\r\nThe result was not altogether the success that Harris had anticipated.\r\nThere seemed so little to show for the business. Six eggs had gone into\r\nthe frying-pan, and all that came out was a teaspoonful of burnt and\r\nunappetizing looking mess.\r\n\r\nHarris said it was the fault of the frying-pan, and thought it would\r\nhave gone better if we had had a fish-kettle and a gas-stove; and\r\nwe decided not to attempt the dish again until we had those aids to\r\nhousekeeping by us.\r\n\r\nThe sun had got more powerful by the time we had finished breakfast,\r\nand the wind had dropped, and it was as lovely a morning as one could\r\ndesire. Little was in sight to remind us of the nineteenth century;\r\nand, as we looked out upon the river in the morning sunlight, we could\r\nalmost fancy that the centuries between us and that ever-to-be-famous\r\nJune morning of 1215 had been drawn aside, and that we, English\r\nyeomen\xe2\x80\x99s sons in homespun cloth, with dirk at belt, were waiting there\r\nto witness the writing of that stupendous page of history, the meaning\r\nwhereof was to be translated to the common people some four hundred and\r\nodd years later by one Oliver Cromwell, who had deeply studied it.\r\n\r\nIt is a fine summer morning\xe2\x80\x94sunny, soft, and still. But through the air\r\nthere runs a thrill of coming stir. King John has slept at Duncroft\r\nHall, and all the day before the little town of Staines has echoed to\r\nthe clang of armed men, and the clatter of great horses over its rough\r\nstones, and the shouts of captains, and the grim oaths and surly jests\r\nof bearded bowmen, billmen, pikemen, and strange-speaking foreign\r\nspearmen.\r\n\r\nGay-cloaked companies of knights and squires have ridden in, all\r\ntravel-stained and dusty. And all the evening long the timid townsmen\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndoors have had to be quick opened to let in rough groups of soldiers,\r\nfor whom there must be found both board and lodging, and the best of\r\nboth, or woe betide the house and all within; for the sword is judge\r\nand jury, plaintiff and executioner, in these tempestuous times, and\r\npays for what it takes by sparing those from whom it takes it, if it\r\npleases it to do so.\r\n\r\nRound the camp-fire in the market-place gather still more of the\r\nBarons\xe2\x80\x99 troops, and eat and drink deep, and bellow forth roystering\r\ndrinking songs, and gamble and quarrel as the evening grows and deepens\r\ninto night. The firelight sheds quaint shadows on their piled-up arms\r\nand on their uncouth forms. The children of the town steal round to\r\nwatch them, wondering; and brawny country wenches, laughing, draw near\r\nto bandy ale-house jest and jibe with the swaggering troopers, so\r\nunlike the village swains, who, now despised, stand apart behind, with\r\nvacant grins upon their broad, peering faces. And out from the fields\r\naround, glitter the faint lights of more distant camps, as here some\r\ngreat lord\xe2\x80\x99s followers lie mustered, and there false John\xe2\x80\x99s French\r\nmercenaries hover like crouching wolves without the town.\r\n\r\nAnd so, with sentinel in each dark street, and twinkling watch-fires on\r\neach height around, the night has worn away, and over this fair valley\r\nof old Thame has broken the morning of the great day that is to close\r\nso big with the fate of ages yet unborn.\r\n\r\nEver since grey dawn, in the lower of the two islands, just above where\r\nwe are standing, there has been great clamour, and the sound of many\r\nworkmen. The great pavilion brought there yester eve is being raised,\r\nand carpenters are busy nailing tiers of seats, while \xe2\x80\x99prentices from\r\nLondon town are there with many-coloured stuffs and silks and cloth of\r\ngold and silver.\r\n\r\nAnd now, lo! down upon the road that winds along the river\xe2\x80\x99s bank from\r\nStaines there come towards us, laughing and talking together in deep\r\nguttural bass, a half-a-score of stalwart halbert-men\xe2\x80\x94Barons\xe2\x80\x99 men,\r\nthese\xe2\x80\x94and halt at a hundred yards or so above us, on the other bank,\r\nand lean upon their arms, and wait.\r\n\r\nAnd so, from hour to hour, march up along the road ever fresh groups\r\nand bands of armed men, their casques and breastplates flashing back\r\nthe long low lines of morning sunlight, until, as far as eye can reach,\r\nthe way seems thick with glittering steel and prancing steeds. And\r\nshouting horsemen are galloping from group to group, and little banners\r\nare fluttering lazily in the warm breeze, and every now and then there\r\nis a deeper stir as the ranks make way on either side, and some great\r\nBaron on his war-horse, with his guard of squires around him, passes\r\nalong to take his station at the head of his serfs and vassals.\r\n\r\nAnd up the slope of Cooper\xe2\x80\x99s Hill, just opposite, are gathered the\r\nwondering rustics and curious townsfolk, who have run from Staines,\r\nand none are quite sure what the bustle is about, but each one has a\r\ndifferent version of the great event that they have come to see; and\r\nsome say that much good to all the people will come from this day\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwork; but the old men shake their heads, for they have heard such tales\r\nbefore.\r\n\r\nAnd all the river down to Staines is dotted with small craft and boats\r\nand tiny coracles\xe2\x80\x94which last are growing out of favour now, and are\r\nused only by the poorer folk. Over the rapids, where in after years\r\ntrim Bell Weir lock will stand, they have been forced or dragged by\r\ntheir sturdy rowers, and now are crowding up as near as they dare come\r\nto the great covered barges, which lie in readiness to bear King John\r\nto where the fateful Charter waits his signing.\r\n\r\nIt is noon, and we and all the people have been waiting patient for\r\nmany an hour, and the rumour has run round that slippery John has again\r\nescaped from the Barons\xe2\x80\x99 grasp, and has stolen away from Duncroft Hall\r\nwith his mercenaries at his heels, and will soon be doing other work\r\nthan signing charters for his people\xe2\x80\x99s liberty.\r\n\r\nNot so! This time the grip upon him has been one of iron, and he has\r\nslid and wriggled in vain. Far down the road a little cloud of dust\r\nhas risen, and draws nearer and grows larger, and the pattering of\r\nmany hoofs grows louder, and in and out between the scattered groups\r\nof drawn-up men, there pushes on its way a brilliant cavalcade of\r\ngay-dressed lords and knights. And front and rear, and either flank,\r\nthere ride the yeomen of the Barons, and in the midst King John.\r\n\r\nHe rides to where the barges lie in readiness, and the great Barons\r\nstep forth from their ranks to meet him. He greets them with a smile\r\nand laugh, and pleasant honeyed words, as though it were some feast in\r\nhis honour to which he had been invited. But as he rises to dismount,\r\nhe casts one hurried glance from his own French mercenaries drawn up in\r\nthe rear to the grim ranks of the Barons\xe2\x80\x99 men that hem him in.\r\n\r\nIs it too late? One fierce blow at the unsuspecting horseman at his\r\nside, one cry to his French troops, one desperate charge upon the\r\nunready lines before him, and these rebellious Barons might rue the\r\nday they dared to thwart his plans! A bolder hand might have turned\r\nthe game even at that point. Had it been a Richard there! the cup of\r\nliberty might have been dashed from England\xe2\x80\x99s lips, and the taste of\r\nfreedom held back for a hundred years.\r\n\r\nBut the heart of King John sinks before the stern faces of the English\r\nfighting men, and the arm of King John drops back on to his rein, and\r\nhe dismounts and takes his seat in the foremost barge. And the Barons\r\nfollow in, with each mailed hand upon the sword-hilt, and the word is\r\ngiven to let go.\r\n\r\nSlowly the heavy, bright-decked barges leave the shore of Runningmede.\r\nSlowly against the swift current they work their ponderous way, till,\r\nwith a low grumble, they grate against the bank of the little island\r\nthat from this day will bear the name of Magna Charta Island. And King\r\nJohn has stepped upon the shore, and we wait in breathless silence till\r\na great shout cleaves the air, and the great cornerstone in England\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntemple of liberty has, now we know, been firmly laid.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII.\r\n\r\n\r\nHenry VIII. and Anne Boleyn.\xe2\x80\x94Disadvantages of living in same house\r\nwith pair of lovers.\xe2\x80\x94A trying time for the English nation.\xe2\x80\x94A\r\nnight search for the picturesque.\xe2\x80\x94Homeless and houseless.\xe2\x80\x94Harris\r\nprepares to die.\xe2\x80\x94An angel comes along.\xe2\x80\x94Effect of sudden joy on\r\nHarris.\xe2\x80\x94A little supper.\xe2\x80\x94Lunch.\xe2\x80\x94High price for mustard.\xe2\x80\x94A fearful\r\nbattle.\xe2\x80\x94Maidenhead.\xe2\x80\x94Sailing.\xe2\x80\x94Three fishers.\xe2\x80\x94We are cursed.\r\n\r\nI was sitting on the bank, conjuring up this scene to myself, when\r\nGeorge remarked that when I was quite rested, perhaps I would not mind\r\nhelping to wash up; and, thus recalled from the days of the glorious\r\npast to the prosaic present, with all its misery and sin, I slid down\r\ninto the boat and cleaned out the frying-pan with a stick of wood and a\r\ntuft of grass, polishing it up finally with George\xe2\x80\x99s wet shirt.\r\n\r\nWe went over to Magna Charta Island, and had a look at the stone which\r\nstands in the cottage there and on which the great Charter is said to\r\nhave been signed; though, as to whether it really was signed there, or,\r\nas some say, on the other bank at \xe2\x80\x9cRunningmede,\xe2\x80\x9d I decline to commit\r\nmyself. As far as my own personal opinion goes, however, I am inclined\r\nto give weight to the popular island theory. Certainly, had I been\r\none of the Barons, at the time, I should have strongly urged upon my\r\ncomrades the advisability of our getting such a slippery customer as\r\nKing John on to the island, where there was less chance of surprises\r\nand tricks.\r\n\r\nThere are the ruins of an old priory in the grounds of Ankerwyke House,\r\nwhich is close to Picnic Point, and it was round about the grounds of\r\nthis old priory that Henry VIII. is said to have waited for and met\r\nAnne Boleyn. He also used to meet her at Hever Castle in Kent, and also\r\nsomewhere near St. Albans. It must have been difficult for the people\r\nof England in those days to have found a spot where these thoughtless\r\nyoung folk were _not_ spooning.\r\n\r\nHave you ever been in a house where there are a couple courting? It is\r\nmost trying. You think you will go and sit in the drawing-room, and you\r\nmarch off there. As you open the door, you hear a noise as if somebody\r\nhad suddenly recollected something, and, when you get in, Emily is\r\nover by the window, full of interest in the opposite side of the road,\r\nand your friend, John Edward, is at the other end of the room with his\r\nwhole soul held in thrall by photographs of other people\xe2\x80\x99s relatives.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d you say, pausing at the door, \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know anybody was here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! didn\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d says Emily, coldly, in a tone which implies that she\r\ndoes not believe you.\r\n\r\nYou hang about for a bit, then you say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very dark. Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you light the gas?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn Edward says, \xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t noticed it; and Emily says that papa\r\ndoes not like the gas lit in the afternoon.\r\n\r\nYou tell them one or two items of news, and give them your views and\r\nopinions on the Irish question; but this does not appear to interest\r\nthem. All they remark on any subject is, \xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cIs it?\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cDid he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t say so!\xe2\x80\x9d And, after ten minutes of such style of\r\nconversation, you edge up to the door, and slip out, and are surprised\r\nto find that the door immediately closes behind you, and shuts itself,\r\nwithout your having touched it.\r\n\r\nHalf an hour later, you think you will try a pipe in the conservatory.\r\nThe only chair in the place is occupied by Emily; and John Edward, if\r\nthe language of clothes can be relied upon, has evidently been sitting\r\non the floor. They do not speak, but they give you a look that says all\r\nthat can be said in a civilised community; and you back out promptly\r\nand shut the door behind you.\r\n\r\nYou are afraid to poke your nose into any room in the house now; so,\r\nafter walking up and down the stairs for a while, you go and sit in\r\nyour own bedroom. This becomes uninteresting, however, after a time,\r\nand so you put on your hat and stroll out into the garden.
