You walk\r\ndown the path, and as you pass the summer-house you glance in, and\r\nthere are those two young idiots, huddled up into one corner of it; and\r\nthey see you, and are evidently under the idea that, for some wicked\r\npurpose of your own, you are following them about.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t they have a special room for this sort of thing, and make\r\npeople keep to it?\xe2\x80\x9d you mutter; and you rush back to the hall and get\r\nyour umbrella and go out.\r\n\r\nIt must have been much like this when that foolish boy Henry VIII.\r\nwas courting his little Anne. People in Buckinghamshire would have\r\ncome upon them unexpectedly when they were mooning round Windsor and\r\nWraysbury, and have exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cOh! you here!\xe2\x80\x9d and Henry would have\r\nblushed and said, \xe2\x80\x9cYes; he\xe2\x80\x99d just come over to see a man;\xe2\x80\x9d and Anne\r\nwould have said, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad to see you! Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it funny? I\xe2\x80\x99ve just\r\nmet Mr. Henry VIII. in the lane, and he\xe2\x80\x99s going the same way I am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen those people would have gone away and said to themselves: \xe2\x80\x9cOh!\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99d better get out of here while this billing and cooing is on. We\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ngo down to Kent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd they would go to Kent, and the first thing they would see in Kent,\r\nwhen they got there, would be Henry and Anne fooling round Hever Castle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, drat this!\xe2\x80\x9d they would have said. \xe2\x80\x9cHere, let\xe2\x80\x99s go away. I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nstand any more of it. Let\xe2\x80\x99s go to St. Albans\xe2\x80\x94nice quiet place, St.\r\nAlbans.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n                          [Picture: River scene]\r\n\r\nAnd when they reached St. Albans, there would be that wretched couple,\r\nkissing under the Abbey walls. Then these folks would go and be pirates\r\nuntil the marriage was over.\r\n\r\nFrom Picnic Point to Old Windsor Lock is a delightful bit of the river.\r\nA shady road, dotted here and there with dainty little cottages, runs\r\nby the bank up to the \xe2\x80\x9cBells of Ouseley,\xe2\x80\x9d a picturesque inn, as most\r\nup-river inns are, and a place where a very good glass of ale may\r\nbe drunk\xe2\x80\x94so Harris says; and on a matter of this kind you can take\r\nHarris\xe2\x80\x99s word. Old Windsor is a famous spot in its way. Edward the\r\nConfessor had a palace here, and here the great Earl Godwin was proved\r\nguilty by the justice of that age of having encompassed the death of\r\nthe King\xe2\x80\x99s brother. Earl Godwin broke a piece of bread and held it in\r\nhis hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I am guilty,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl, \xe2\x80\x9cmay this bread choke me when I eat\r\nit!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he put the bread into his mouth and swallowed it, and it choked\r\nhim, and he died.\r\n\r\nAfter you pass Old Windsor, the river is somewhat uninteresting, and\r\ndoes not become itself again until you are nearing Boveney. George and\r\nI towed up past the Home Park, which stretches along the right bank\r\nfrom Albert to Victoria Bridge; and as we were passing Datchet, George\r\nasked me if I remembered our first trip up the river, and when we\r\nlanded at Datchet at ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock at night, and wanted to go to bed.\r\n\r\nI answered that I did remember it. It will be some time before I forget\r\nit.\r\n\r\nIt was the Saturday before the August Bank Holiday. We were tired and\r\nhungry, we same three, and when we got to Datchet we took out the\r\nhamper, the two bags, and the rugs and coats, and such like things, and\r\nstarted off to look for diggings. We passed a very pretty little hotel,\r\nwith clematis and creeper over the porch; but there was no honeysuckle\r\nabout it, and, for some reason or other, I had got my mind fixed on\r\nhoneysuckle, and I said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t let\xe2\x80\x99s go in there! Let\xe2\x80\x99s go on a bit further, and see if\r\nthere isn\xe2\x80\x99t one with honeysuckle over it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo we went on till we came to another hotel. That was a very nice\r\nhotel, too, and it had honey-suckle on it, round at the side; but\r\nHarris did not like the look of a man who was leaning against the front\r\ndoor. He said he didn\xe2\x80\x99t look a nice man at all, and he wore ugly boots:\r\nso we went on further. We went a goodish way without coming across any\r\nmore hotels, and then we met a man, and asked him to direct us to a few.\r\n\r\nHe said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you are coming away from them. You must turn right round and go\r\nback, and then you will come to the Stag.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we had been there, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it\xe2\x80\x94no honeysuckle over it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s the Manor House, just opposite. Have\r\nyou tried that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHarris replied that we did not want to go there\xe2\x80\x94didn\xe2\x80\x99t like the looks\r\nof a man who was stopping there\xe2\x80\x94Harris did not like the colour of his\r\nhair, didn\xe2\x80\x99t like his boots, either.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you\xe2\x80\x99ll do, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure,\xe2\x80\x9d said our informant;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbecause they are the only two inns in the place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo other inns!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Harris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat on earth are we to do?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Harris.\r\n\r\nThen George spoke up. He said Harris and I could get an hotel built for\r\nus, if we liked, and have some people made to put in. For his part, he\r\nwas going back to the Stag.\r\n\r\nThe greatest minds never realise their ideals in any matter; and Harris\r\nand I sighed over the hollowness of all earthly desires, and followed\r\nGeorge.\r\n\r\nWe took our traps into the Stag, and laid them down in the hall.\r\n\r\nThe landlord came up and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood evening, gentlemen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, good evening,\xe2\x80\x9d said George; \xe2\x80\x9cwe want three beds, please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery sorry, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the landlord; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid we can\xe2\x80\x99t manage\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, well, never mind,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, \xe2\x80\x9ctwo will do. Two of us can sleep\r\nin one bed, can\xe2\x80\x99t we?\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, turning to Harris and me.\r\n\r\nHarris said, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes;\xe2\x80\x9d he thought George and I could sleep in one bed\r\nvery easily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery sorry, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d again repeated the landlord: \xe2\x80\x9cbut we really haven\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngot a bed vacant in the whole house. In fact, we are putting two, and\r\neven three gentlemen in one bed, as it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis staggered us for a bit.\r\n\r\nBut Harris, who is an old traveller, rose to the occasion, and,\r\nlaughing cheerily, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, well, we can\xe2\x80\x99t help it. We must rough it. You must give us a\r\nshake-down in the billiard-room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery sorry, sir. Three gentlemen sleeping on the billiard-table\r\nalready, and two in the coffee-room. Can\xe2\x80\x99t possibly take you in\r\nto-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe picked up our things, and went over to the Manor House. It was a\r\npretty little place. I said I thought I should like it better than the\r\nother house; and Harris said, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d it would be all right, and we\r\nneedn\xe2\x80\x99t look at the man with the red hair; besides, the poor fellow\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t help having red hair.\r\n\r\nHarris spoke quite kindly and sensibly about it.\r\n\r\nThe people at the Manor House did not wait to hear us talk. The\r\nlandlady met us on the doorstep with the greeting that we were the\r\nfourteenth party she had turned away within the last hour and a half.\r\nAs for our meek suggestions of stables, billiard-room, or coal-cellars,\r\nshe laughed them all to scorn: all these nooks had been snatched up\r\nlong ago.\r\n\r\nDid she know of any place in the whole village where we could get\r\nshelter for the night?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if we didn\xe2\x80\x99t mind roughing it\xe2\x80\x94she did not recommend it, mind\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nthere was a little beershop half a mile down the Eton road\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe waited to hear no more; we caught up the hamper and the bags, and\r\nthe coats and rugs, and parcels, and ran. The distance seemed more like\r\na mile than half a mile, but we reached the place at last, and rushed,\r\npanting, into the bar.\r\n\r\nThe people at the beershop were rude. They merely laughed at us.\r\nThere were only three beds in the whole house, and they had seven\r\nsingle gentlemen and two married couples sleeping there already. A\r\nkind-hearted bargeman, however, who happened to be in the tap-room,\r\nthought we might try the grocer\xe2\x80\x99s, next door to the Stag, and we went\r\nback.\r\n\r\nThe grocer\xe2\x80\x99s was full. An old woman we met in the shop then kindly took\r\nus along with her for a quarter of a mile, to a lady friend of hers,\r\nwho occasionally let rooms to gentlemen.\r\n\r\nThis old woman walked very slowly, and we were twenty minutes getting\r\nto her lady friend\xe2\x80\x99s. She enlivened the journey by describing to us, as\r\nwe trailed along, the various pains she had in her back.\r\n\r\nHer lady friend\xe2\x80\x99s rooms were let. From there we were recommended to No.\r\n27. No. 27 was full, and sent us to No. 32, and 32 was full.\r\n\r\nThen we went back into the high road, and Harris sat down on the hamper\r\nand said he would go no further. He said it seemed a quiet spot, and he\r\nwould like to die there. He requested George and me to kiss his mother\r\nfor him, and to tell all his relations that he forgave them and died\r\nhappy.\r\n\r\nAt that moment an angel came by in the disguise of a small boy (and\r\nI cannot think of any more effective disguise an angel could have\r\nassumed), with a can of beer in one hand, and in the other something\r\nat the end of a string, which he let down on to every flat stone he\r\ncame across, and then pulled up again, this producing a peculiarly\r\nunattractive sound, suggestive of suffering.\r\n\r\nWe asked this heavenly messenger (as we discovered him afterwards to\r\nbe) if he knew of any lonely house, whose occupants were few and feeble\r\n(old ladies or paralysed gentlemen preferred), who could be easily\r\nfrightened into giving up their beds for the night to three desperate\r\nmen; or, if not this, could he recommend us to an empty pigstye, or a\r\ndisused limekiln, or anything of that sort. He did not know of any such\r\nplace\xe2\x80\x94at least, not one handy; but he said that, if we liked to come\r\nwith him, his mother had a room to spare, and could put us up for the\r\nnight.\r\n\r\nWe fell upon his neck there in the moonlight and blessed him, and it\r\nwould have made a very beautiful picture if the boy himself had not\r\nbeen so over-powered by our emotion as to be unable to sustain himself\r\nunder it, and sunk to the ground, letting us all down on top of him.\r\nHarris was so overcome with joy that he fainted, and had to seize the\r\nboy\xe2\x80\x99s beer-can and half empty it before he could recover consciousness,\r\nand then he started off at a run, and left George and me to bring on\r\nthe luggage.\r\n\r\nIt was a little four-roomed cottage where the boy lived, and his\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x94good soul!\xe2\x80\x94gave us hot bacon for supper, and we ate it all\xe2\x80\x94five\r\npounds\xe2\x80\x94and a jam tart afterwards, and two pots of tea, and then we went\r\nto bed. There were two beds in the room; one was a 2ft. 6in. truckle\r\nbed, and George and I slept in that, and kept in by tying ourselves\r\ntogether with a sheet; and the other was the little boy\xe2\x80\x99s bed, and\r\nHarris had that all to himself, and we found him, in the morning, with\r\ntwo feet of bare leg sticking out at the bottom, and George and I used\r\nit to hang the towels on while we bathed.\r\n\r\nWe were not so uppish about what sort of hotel we would have, next time\r\nwe went to Datchet.\r\n\r\nTo return to our present trip: nothing exciting happened, and we tugged\r\nsteadily on to a little below Monkey Island, where we drew up and\r\nlunched. We tackled the cold beef for lunch, and then we found that we\r\nhad forgotten to bring any mustard. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I ever in my life,\r\nbefore or since, felt I wanted mustard as badly as I felt I wanted it\r\nthen. I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for mustard as a rule, and it is very seldom that I\r\ntake it at all, but I would have given worlds for it then.\r\n\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know how many worlds there may be in the universe, but anyone\r\nwho had brought me a spoonful of mustard at that precise moment could\r\nhave had them all. I grow reckless like that when I want a thing and\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t get it.\r\n\r\nHarris said he would have given worlds for mustard too. It would have\r\nbeen a good thing for anybody who had come up to that spot with a can\r\nof mustard, then: he would have been set up in worlds for the rest of\r\nhis life.\r\n\r\nBut there! I daresay both Harris and I would have tried to back out of\r\nthe bargain after we had got the mustard. One makes these extravagant\r\noffers in moments of excitement, but, of course, when one comes to\r\nthink of it, one sees how absurdly out of proportion they are with the\r\nvalue of the required article. I heard a man, going up a mountain in\r\nSwitzerland, once say he would give worlds for a glass of beer, and,\r\nwhen he came to a little shanty where they kept it, he kicked up a most\r\nfearful row because they charged him five francs for a bottle of Bass.\r\nHe said it was a scandalous imposition, and he wrote to the _Times_\r\nabout it.\r\n\r\nIt cast a gloom over the boat, there being no mustard. We ate our beef\r\nin silence. Existence seemed hollow and uninteresting. We thought\r\nof the happy days of childhood, and sighed. We brightened up a bit,\r\nhowever, over the apple-tart, and, when George drew out a tin of\r\npine-apple from the bottom of the hamper, and rolled it into the middle\r\nof the boat, we felt that life was worth living after all.\r\n\r\nWe are very fond of pine-apple, all three of us. We looked at the\r\npicture on the tin; we thought of the juice. We smiled at one another,\r\nand Harris got a spoon ready.\r\n\r\nThen we looked for the knife to open the tin with. We turned out\r\neverything in the hamper. We turned out the bags. We pulled up the\r\nboards at the bottom of the boat. We took everything out on to the bank\r\nand shook it. There was no tin-opener to be found.\r\n\r\nThen Harris tried to open the tin with a pocket-knife, and broke the\r\nknife and cut himself badly; and George tried a pair of scissors, and\r\nthe scissors flew up, and nearly put his eye out. While they were\r\ndressing their wounds, I tried to make a hole in the thing with the\r\nspiky end of the hitcher, and the hitcher slipped and jerked me out\r\nbetween the boat and the bank into two feet of muddy water, and the tin\r\nrolled over, uninjured, and broke a teacup.\r\n\r\nThen we all got mad. We took that tin out on the bank, and Harris went\r\nup into a field and got a big sharp stone, and I went back into the\r\nboat and brought out the mast, and George held the tin and Harris held\r\nthe sharp end of his stone against the top of it, and I took the mast\r\nand poised it high up in the air, and gathered up all my strength and\r\nbrought it down.\r\n\r\nIt was George\xe2\x80\x99s straw hat that saved his life that day. He keeps that\r\nhat now (what is left of it), and, of a winter\xe2\x80\x99s evening, when the\r\npipes are lit and the boys are telling stretchers about the dangers\r\nthey have passed through, George brings it down and shows it round, and\r\nthe stirring tale is told anew, with fresh exaggerations every time.\r\n\r\nHarris got off with merely a flesh wound.\r\n\r\nAfter that, I took the tin off myself, and hammered at it with the mast\r\ntill I was worn out and sick at heart, whereupon Harris took it in hand.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Flattened tin] We beat it out flat; we beat it back square;\r\nwe battered it into every form known to geometry\xe2\x80\x94but we could not make\r\na hole in it. Then George went at it, and knocked it into a shape, so\r\nstrange, so weird, so unearthly in its wild hideousness, that he got\r\nfrightened and threw away the mast. Then we all three sat round it on\r\nthe grass and looked at it.\r\n\r\nThere was one great dent across the top that had the appearance of a\r\nmocking grin, and it drove us furious, so that Harris rushed at the\r\nthing, and caught it up, and flung it far into the middle of the river,\r\nand as it sank we hurled our curses at it, and we got into the boat and\r\nrowed away from the spot, and never paused till we reached Maidenhead.\r\n\r\nMaidenhead itself is too snobby to be pleasant. It is the haunt of\r\nthe river swell and his overdressed female companion. It is the\r\ntown of showy hotels, patronised chiefly by dudes and ballet girls.\r\nIt is the witch\xe2\x80\x99s kitchen from which go forth those demons of the\r\nriver\xe2\x80\x94steam-launches. The _London Journal_ duke always has his \xe2\x80\x9clittle\r\nplace\xe2\x80\x9d at Maidenhead; and the heroine of the three-volume novel always\r\ndines there when she goes out on the spree with somebody else\xe2\x80\x99s husband.\r\n\r\n                          [Picture: River scene]\r\n\r\nWe went through Maidenhead quickly, and then eased up, and took\r\nleisurely that grand reach beyond Boulter\xe2\x80\x99s and Cookham locks.\r\nClieveden Woods still wore their dainty dress of spring, and rose\r\nup, from the water\xe2\x80\x99s edge, in one long harmony of blended shades of\r\nfairy green. In its unbroken loveliness this is, perhaps, the sweetest\r\nstretch of all the river, and lingeringly we slowly drew our little\r\nboat away from its deep peace.\r\n\r\nWe pulled up in the backwater, just below Cookham, and had tea; and,\r\nwhen we were through the lock, it was evening. A stiffish breeze had\r\nsprung up\xe2\x80\x94in our favour, for a wonder; for, as a rule on the river,\r\nthe wind is always dead against you whatever way you go. It is against\r\nyou in the morning, when you start for a day\xe2\x80\x99s trip, and you pull a\r\nlong distance, thinking how easy it will be to come back with the sail.\r\nThen, after tea, the wind veers round, and you have to pull hard in its\r\nteeth all the way home.\r\n\r\nWhen you forget to take the sail at all, then the wind is consistently\r\nin your favour both ways. But there! this world is only a probation,\r\nand man was born to trouble as the sparks fly upward.\r\n\r\nThis evening, however, they had evidently made a mistake, and had put\r\nthe wind round at our back instead of in our face. We kept very quiet\r\nabout it, and got the sail up quickly before they found it out, and\r\nthen we spread ourselves about the boat in thoughtful attitudes, and\r\nthe sail bellied out, and strained, and grumbled at the mast, and the\r\nboat flew.\r\n\r\nI steered.\r\n\r\nThere is no more thrilling sensation I know of than sailing. It comes\r\nas near to flying as man has got to yet\xe2\x80\x94except in dreams. The wings of\r\nthe rushing wind seem to be bearing you onward, you know not where.\r\nYou are no longer the slow, plodding, puny thing of clay, creeping\r\ntortuously upon the ground; you are a part of Nature! Your heart is\r\nthrobbing against hers! Her glorious arms are round you, raising you\r\nup against her heart! Your spirit is at one with hers; your limbs\r\ngrow light! The voices of the air are singing to you. The earth seems\r\nfar away and little; and the clouds, so close above your head, are\r\nbrothers, and you stretch your arms to them.\r\n\r\nWe had the river to ourselves, except that, far in the distance,\r\nwe could see a fishing-punt, moored in mid-stream, on which three\r\nfishermen sat; and we skimmed over the water, and passed the wooded\r\nbanks, and no one spoke.\r\n\r\nI was steering.\r\n\r\nAs we drew nearer, we could see that the three men fishing seemed\r\nold and solemn-looking men. They sat on three chairs in the punt,\r\nand watched intently their lines. And the red sunset threw a mystic\r\nlight upon the waters, and tinged with fire the towering woods, and\r\nmade a golden glory of the piled-up clouds. It was an hour of deep\r\nenchantment, of ecstatic hope and longing. The little sail stood out\r\nagainst the purple sky, the gloaming lay around us, wrapping the world\r\nin rainbow shadows; and, behind us, crept the night.\r\n\r\nWe seemed like knights of some old legend, sailing across some mystic\r\nlake into the unknown realm of twilight, unto the great land of the\r\nsunset.\r\n\r\nWe did not go into the realm of twilight; we went slap into that punt,\r\nwhere those three old men were fishing. We did not know what had\r\nhappened at first, because the sail shut out the view, but from the\r\nnature of the language that rose up upon the evening air, we gathered\r\nthat we had come into the neighbourhood of human beings, and that they\r\nwere vexed and discontented.\r\n\r\nHarris let the sail down, and then we saw what had happened. We had\r\nknocked those three old gentlemen off their chairs into a general\r\nheap at the bottom of the boat, and they were now slowly and\r\npainfully sorting themselves out from each other, and picking fish\r\noff themselves; and as they worked, they cursed us\xe2\x80\x94not with a common\r\ncursory curse, but with long, carefully-thought-out, comprehensive\r\ncurses, that embraced the whole of our career, and went away into the\r\ndistant future, and included all our relations, and covered everything\r\nconnected with us\xe2\x80\x94good, substantial curses.\r\n\r\nHarris told them they ought to be grateful for a little excitement,\r\nsitting there fishing all day, and he also said that he was shocked and\r\ngrieved to hear men their age give way to temper so.\r\n\r\nBut it did not do any good.\r\n\r\nGeorge said he would steer, after that. He said a mind like mine ought\r\nnot to be expected to give itself away in steering boats\xe2\x80\x94better let a\r\nmere commonplace human being see after that boat, before we jolly well\r\nall got drowned; and he took the lines, and brought us up to Marlow.\r\n\r\nAnd at Marlow we left the boat by the bridge, and went and put up for\r\nthe night at\r\n the \xe2\x80\x9cCrown.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n                           [Picture: The boat]\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII.\r\n\r\n\r\nMarlow.\xe2\x80\x94Bisham Abbey.\xe2\x80\x94The Medmenham Monks.\xe2\x80\x94Montmorency thinks he\r\nwill murder an old Tom cat.\xe2\x80\x94But eventually decides that he will let\r\nit live.\xe2\x80\x94Shameful conduct of a fox terrier at the Civil Service\r\nStores.\xe2\x80\x94Our departure from Marlow.\xe2\x80\x94An imposing procession.\xe2\x80\x94The steam\r\nlaunch, useful receipts for annoying and hindering it.\xe2\x80\x94We decline to\r\ndrink the river.\xe2\x80\x94A peaceful dog.\xe2\x80\x94Strange disappearance of Harris and a\r\npie.\r\n\r\nMarlow is one of the pleasantest river centres I know of. It is a\r\nbustling, lively little town; not very picturesque on the whole, it\r\nis true, but there are many quaint nooks and corners to be found in\r\nit, nevertheless\xe2\x80\x94standing arches in the shattered bridge of Time, over\r\nwhich our fancy travels back to the days when Marlow Manor owned Saxon\r\nAlgar for its lord, ere conquering William seized it to give to Queen\r\nMatilda, ere it passed to the Earls of Warwick or to worldly-wise Lord\r\nPaget, the councillor of four successive sovereigns.\r\n\r\nThere is lovely country round about it, too, if, after boating, you are\r\nfond of a walk, while the river itself is at its best here. Down to\r\nCookham, past the Quarry Woods and the meadows, is a lovely reach. Dear\r\nold Quarry Woods! with your narrow, climbing paths, and little winding\r\nglades, how scented to this hour you seem with memories of sunny summer\r\ndays! How haunted are your shadowy vistas with the ghosts of laughing\r\nfaces! how from your whispering leaves there softly fall the voices of\r\nlong ago!\r\n\r\n[Picture: Bisham Abbey] From Marlow up to Sonning is even fairer yet.\r\nGrand old Bisham Abbey, whose stone walls have rung to the shouts of\r\nthe Knights Templars, and which, at one time, was the home of Anne\r\nof Cleves and at another of Queen Elizabeth, is passed on the right\r\nbank just half a mile above Marlow Bridge. Bisham Abbey is rich in\r\nmelodramatic properties. It contains a tapestry bed-chamber, and a\r\nsecret room hid high up in the thick walls. The ghost of the Lady Holy,\r\nwho beat her little boy to death, still walks there at night, trying to\r\nwash its ghostly hands clean in a ghostly basin.\r\n\r\nWarwick, the king-maker, rests there, careless now about such trivial\r\nthings as earthly kings and earthly kingdoms; and Salisbury, who did\r\ngood service at Poitiers. Just before you come to the abbey, and right\r\non the river\xe2\x80\x99s bank, is Bisham Church, and, perhaps, if any tombs are\r\nworth inspecting, they are the tombs and monuments in Bisham Church. It\r\nwas while floating in his boat under the Bisham beeches that Shelley,\r\nwho was then living at Marlow (you can see his house now, in West\r\nstreet), composed _The Revolt of Islam_.\r\n\r\nBy Hurley Weir, a little higher up, I have often thought that I could\r\nstay a month without having sufficient time to drink in all the beauty\r\nof the scene. The village of Hurley, five minutes\xe2\x80\x99 walk from the lock,\r\nis as old a little spot as there is on the river, dating, as it does,\r\nto quote the quaint phraseology of those dim days, \xe2\x80\x9cfrom the times of\r\nKing Sebert and King Offa.\xe2\x80\x9d Just past the weir (going up) is Danes\xe2\x80\x99\r\nField, where the invading Danes once encamped, during their march to\r\nGloucestershire; and a little further still, nestling by a sweet corner\r\nof the stream, is what is left of Medmenham Abbey.\r\n\r\nThe famous Medmenham monks, or \xe2\x80\x9cHell Fire Club,\xe2\x80\x9d as they were\r\ncommonly called, and of whom the notorious Wilkes was a member, were\r\na fraternity whose motto was \xe2\x80\x9cDo as you please,\xe2\x80\x9d and that invitation\r\nstill stands over the ruined doorway of the abbey. Many years before\r\nthis bogus abbey, with its congregation of irreverent jesters, was\r\nfounded, there stood upon this same spot a monastery of a sterner kind,\r\nwhose monks were of a somewhat different type to the revellers that\r\nwere to follow them, five hundred years afterwards.\r\n\r\nThe Cistercian monks, whose abbey stood there in the thirteenth\r\ncentury, wore no clothes but rough tunics and cowls, and ate no flesh,\r\nnor fish, nor eggs. They lay upon straw, and they rose at midnight to\r\nmass. They spent the day in labour, reading, and prayer; and over all\r\ntheir lives there fell a silence as of death, for no one spoke.\r\n\r\nA grim fraternity, passing grim lives in that sweet spot, that God had\r\nmade so bright! Strange that Nature\xe2\x80\x99s voices all around them\xe2\x80\x94the soft\r\nsinging of the waters, the whisperings of the river grass, the music of\r\nthe rushing wind\xe2\x80\x94should not have taught them a truer meaning of life\r\nthan this. They listened there, through the long days, in silence,\r\nwaiting for a voice from heaven; and all day long and through the\r\nsolemn night it spoke to them in myriad tones, and they heard it not.\r\n\r\nFrom Medmenham to sweet Hambledon Lock the river is full of peaceful\r\nbeauty, but, after it passes Greenlands, the rather uninteresting\r\nlooking river residence of my newsagent\xe2\x80\x94a quiet unassuming old\r\ngentleman, who may often be met with about these regions, during the\r\nsummer months, sculling himself along in easy vigorous style, or\r\nchatting genially to some old lock-keeper, as he passes through\xe2\x80\x94until\r\nwell the other side of Henley, it is somewhat bare and dull.\r\n\r\nWe got up tolerably early on the Monday morning at Marlow, and went for\r\na bathe before breakfast; and, coming back, Montmorency made an awful\r\nass of himself. The only subject on which Montmorency and I have any\r\nserious difference of opinion is cats. I like cats; Montmorency does\r\nnot.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Cat] When I meet a cat, I say, \xe2\x80\x9cPoor Pussy!\xe2\x80\x9d and stop down\r\nand tickle the side of its head; and the cat sticks up its tail in a\r\nrigid, cast-iron manner, arches its back, and wipes its nose up against\r\nmy trousers; and all is gentleness and peace. When Montmorency meets a\r\ncat, the whole street knows about it; and there is enough bad language\r\nwasted in ten seconds to last an ordinarily respectable man all his\r\nlife, with care.\r\n\r\nI do not blame the dog (contenting myself, as a rule, with merely\r\nclouting his head or throwing stones at him), because I take it that\r\nit is his nature. Fox-terriers are born with about four times as much\r\noriginal sin in them as other dogs are, and it will take years and\r\nyears of patient effort on the part of us Christians to bring about any\r\nappreciable reformation in the rowdiness of the fox-terrier nature.\r\n\r\nI remember being in the lobby of the Haymarket Stores one day, and all\r\nround about me were dogs, waiting for the return of their owners, who\r\nwere shopping inside. There were a mastiff, and one or two collies,\r\nand a St. Bernard, a few retrievers and Newfoundlands, a boar-hound, a\r\nFrench poodle, with plenty of hair round its head, but mangy about the\r\nmiddle; a bull-dog, a few Lowther Arcade sort of animals, about the\r\nsize of rats, and a couple of Yorkshire tykes.\r\n\r\nThere they sat, patient, good, and thoughtful. A solemn peacefulness\r\nseemed to reign in that lobby. An air of calmness and resignation\xe2\x80\x94of\r\ngentle sadness pervaded the room.\r\n\r\nThen a sweet young lady entered, leading a meek-looking little\r\nfox-terrier, and left him, chained up there, between the bull-dog and\r\nthe poodle. He sat and looked about him for a minute. Then he cast up\r\nhis eyes to the ceiling, and seemed, judging from his expression, to\r\nbe thinking of his mother. Then he yawned. Then he looked round at the\r\nother dogs, all silent, grave, and dignified.\r\n\r\nHe looked at the bull-dog, sleeping dreamlessly on his right. He looked\r\nat the poodle, erect and haughty, on his left. Then, without a word of\r\nwarning, without the shadow of a provocation, he bit that poodle\xe2\x80\x99s near\r\nfore-leg, and a yelp of agony rang through the quiet shades of that\r\nlobby.\r\n\r\nThe result of his first experiment seemed highly satisfactory to him,\r\nand he determined to go on and make things lively all round. He sprang\r\nover the poodle and vigorously attacked a collie, and the collie woke\r\nup, and immediately commenced a fierce and noisy contest with the\r\npoodle. Then Foxey came back to his own place, and caught the bull-dog\r\nby the ear, and tried to throw him away; and the bull-dog, a curiously\r\nimpartial animal, went for everything he could reach, including the\r\nhall-porter, which gave that dear little terrier the opportunity\r\nto enjoy an uninterrupted fight of his own with an equally willing\r\nYorkshire tyke.\r\n\r\nAnyone who knows canine nature need hardly, be told that, by this\r\ntime, all the other dogs in the place were fighting as if their\r\nhearths and homes depended on the fray. The big dogs fought each other\r\nindiscriminately; and the little dogs fought among themselves, and\r\nfilled up their spare time by biting the legs of the big dogs.\r\n\r\nThe whole lobby was a perfect pandemonium, and the din was terrific. A\r\ncrowd assembled outside in the Haymarket, and asked if it was a vestry\r\nmeeting; or, if not, who was being murdered, and why? Men came with\r\npoles and ropes, and tried to separate the dogs, and the police were\r\nsent for.\r\n\r\nAnd in the midst of the riot that sweet young lady returned, and\r\nsnatched up that sweet little dog of hers (he had laid the tyke up for\r\na month, and had on the expression, now, of a new-born lamb) into her\r\narms, and kissed him, and asked him if he was killed, and what those\r\ngreat nasty brutes of dogs had been doing to him; and he nestled up\r\nagainst her, and gazed up into her face with a look that seemed to say:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad you\xe2\x80\x99ve come to take me away from this disgraceful\r\nscene!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe said that the people at the Stores had no right to allow great\r\nsavage things like those other dogs to be put with respectable people\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndogs, and that she had a great mind to summon somebody.\r\n\r\nSuch is the nature of fox-terriers; and, therefore, I do not blame\r\nMontmorency for his tendency to row with cats; but he wished he had not\r\ngiven way to it that morning.\r\n\r\nWe were, as I have said, returning from a dip, and half-way up the High\r\nStreet a cat darted out from one of the houses in front of us, and\r\nbegan to trot across the road. Montmorency gave a cry of joy\xe2\x80\x94the cry of\r\na stern warrior who sees his enemy given over to his hands\xe2\x80\x94the sort of\r\ncry Cromwell might have uttered when the Scots came down the hill\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nflew after his prey.\r\n\r\nHis victim was a large black Tom. I never saw a larger cat, nor a\r\nmore disreputable-looking cat. It had lost half its tail, one of its\r\nears, and a fairly appreciable proportion of its nose. It was a long,\r\nsinewy-looking animal. It had a calm, contented air about it.\r\n\r\nMontmorency went for that poor cat at the rate of twenty miles an hour;\r\nbut the cat did not hurry up\xe2\x80\x94did not seem to have grasped the idea\r\nthat its life was in danger. It trotted quietly on until its would-be\r\nassassin was within a yard of it, and then it turned round and sat down\r\nin the middle of the road, and looked at Montmorency with a gentle,\r\ninquiring expression, that said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes! You want me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMontmorency does not lack pluck; but there was something about the look\r\nof that cat that might have chilled the heart of the boldest dog. He\r\nstopped abruptly, and looked back at Tom.\r\n\r\nNeither spoke; but the conversation that one could imagine was clearly\r\nas follows:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nTHE CAT: \xe2\x80\x9cCan I do anything for you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMONTMORENCY: \xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94no, thanks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTHE CAT: \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you mind speaking, if you really want anything, you\r\nknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMONTMORENCY (_backing down the High Street_): \xe2\x80\x9cOh, no\xe2\x80\x94not at\r\nall\xe2\x80\x94certainly\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t you trouble. I\xe2\x80\x94I am afraid I\xe2\x80\x99ve made a mistake. I\r\nthought I knew you. Sorry I disturbed you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTHE CAT: \xe2\x80\x9cNot at all\xe2\x80\x94quite a pleasure. Sure you don\xe2\x80\x99t want anything,\r\nnow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMONTMORENCY (_still backing_): \xe2\x80\x9cNot at all, thanks\xe2\x80\x94not at all\xe2\x80\x94very kind\r\nof you. Good morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTHE CAT: \xe2\x80\x9cGood-morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the cat rose, and continued his trot; and Montmorency, fitting\r\nwhat he calls his tail carefully into its groove, came back to us, and\r\ntook up an unimportant position in the rear.\r\n\r\nTo this day, if you say the word \xe2\x80\x9cCats!\xe2\x80\x9d to Montmorency, he will\r\nvisibly shrink and look up piteously at you, as if to say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe did our marketing after breakfast, and revictualled the boat for\r\nthree days. George said we ought to take vegetables\xe2\x80\x94that it was\r\nunhealthy not to eat vegetables. He said they were easy enough to cook,\r\nand that he would see to that; so we got ten pounds of potatoes, a\r\nbushel of peas, and a few cabbages. We got a beefsteak pie, a couple of\r\ngooseberry tarts, and a leg of mutton from the hotel; and fruit, and\r\ncakes, and bread and butter, and jam, and bacon and eggs, and other\r\nthings we foraged round about the town for.\r\n\r\nOur departure from Marlow I regard as one of our greatest successes.\r\nIt was dignified and impressive, without being ostentatious. We had\r\ninsisted at all the shops we had been to that the things should be sent\r\nwith us then and there. None of your \xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, I will send them off at\r\nonce: the boy will be down there before you are, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d and then fooling\r\nabout on the landing-stage, and going back to the shop twice to have a\r\nrow about them, for us. We waited while the basket was packed, and took\r\nthe boy with us.\r\n\r\nWe went to a good many shops, adopting this principle at each one; and\r\nthe consequence was that, by the time we had finished, we had as fine\r\na collection of boys with baskets following us around as heart could\r\ndesire; and our final march down the middle of the High Street, to the\r\nriver, must have been as imposing a spectacle as Marlow had seen for\r\nmany a long day.\r\n\r\nThe order of the procession was as follows:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n                      Montmorency, carrying a stick.\r\n         Two disreputable-looking curs, friends of Montmorency\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n        George, carrying coats and rugs, and smoking a short pipe.\r\n                 Harris, trying to walk with easy grace,\r\n          while carrying a bulged-out Gladstone bag in one hand\r\n                 and a bottle of lime-juice in the other.\r\n                    Greengrocer\xe2\x80\x99s boy and baker\xe2\x80\x99s boy,\r\n                              with baskets.\r\n                  Boots from the hotel, carrying hamper.\r\n                     Confectioner\xe2\x80\x99s boy, with basket.\r\n                        Grocer\xe2\x80\x99s boy, with basket.\r\n                             Long-haired dog.\r\n                     Cheesemonger\xe2\x80\x99s boy, with basket.\r\n                         Odd man carrying a bag.\r\n        Bosom companion of odd man, with his hands in his pockets,\r\n                          smoking a short clay.\r\n                      Fruiterer\xe2\x80\x99s boy, with basket.\r\n             Myself, carrying three hats and a pair of boots,\r\n                and trying to look as if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know it.\r\n                   Six small boys, and four stray dogs.\r\n\r\nWhen we got down to the landing-stage, the boatman said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me see, sir; was yours a steam-launch or a house-boat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Picture: The bring of the provisions] On our informing him it was a\r\ndouble-sculling skiff, he seemed surprised.\r\n\r\nWe had a good deal of trouble with steam launches that morning. It was\r\njust before the Henley week, and they were going up in large numbers;\r\nsome by themselves, some towing houseboats. I do hate steam launches: I\r\nsuppose every rowing man does. I never see a steam launch but I feel I\r\nshould like to lure it to a lonely part of the river, and there, in the\r\nsilence and the solitude, strangle it.\r\n\r\nThere is a blatant bumptiousness about a steam launch that has the\r\nknack of rousing every evil instinct in my nature, and I yearn for the\r\ngood old days, when you could go about and tell people what you thought\r\nof them with a hatchet and a bow and arrows. The expression on the face\r\nof the man who, with his hands in his pockets, stands by the stern,\r\nsmoking a cigar, is sufficient to excuse a breach of the peace by\r\nitself; and the lordly whistle for you to get out of the way would, I\r\nam confident, ensure a verdict of \xe2\x80\x9cjustifiable homicide\xe2\x80\x9d from any jury\r\nof river men.\r\n\r\nThey used to _have_ to whistle for us to get out of their way. If I\r\nmay do so, without appearing boastful, I think I can honestly say that\r\nour one small boat, during that week, caused more annoyance and delay\r\nand aggravation to the steam launches that we came across than all the\r\nother craft on the river put together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSteam launch, coming!\xe2\x80\x9d one of us would cry out, on sighting the enemy\r\nin the distance; and, in an instant, everything was got ready to\r\nreceive her. I would take the lines, and Harris and George would sit\r\ndown beside me, all of us with our backs to the launch, and the boat\r\nwould drift out quietly into mid-stream.\r\n\r\nOn would come the launch, whistling, and on we would go, drifting. At\r\nabout a hundred yards off, she would start whistling like mad, and the\r\npeople would come and lean over the side, and roar at us; but we never\r\nheard them! Harris would be telling us an anecdote about his mother,\r\nand George and I would not have missed a word of it for worlds.\r\n\r\nThen that launch would give one final shriek of a whistle that would\r\nnearly burst the boiler, and she would reverse her engines, and blow\r\noff steam, and swing round and get aground; everyone on board of it\r\nwould rush to the bow and yell at us, and the people on the bank would\r\nstand and shout to us, and all the other passing boats would stop and\r\njoin in, till the whole river for miles up and down was in a state\r\nof frantic commotion. And then Harris would break off in the most\r\ninteresting part of his narrative, and look up with mild surprise, and\r\nsay to George:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, George, bless me, if here isn\xe2\x80\x99t a steam launch!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd George would answer:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, do you know, I _thought_ I heard something!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUpon which we would get nervous and confused, and not know how to get\r\nthe boat out of the way, and the people in the launch would crowd round\r\nand instruct us:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPull your right\xe2\x80\x94you, you idiot! back with your left. No, not _you_\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nother one\xe2\x80\x94leave the lines alone, can\xe2\x80\x99t you\xe2\x80\x94now, both together. NOT\r\n_that_ way. Oh, you\xe2\x80\x94!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen they would lower a boat and come to our assistance; and, after\r\nquarter of an hour\xe2\x80\x99s effort, would get us clean out of their way, so\r\nthat they could go on; and we would thank them so much, and ask them to\r\ngive us a tow. But they never would.\r\n\r\nAnother good way we discovered of irritating the aristocratic type of\r\nsteam launch, was to mistake them for a beanfeast, and ask them if they\r\nwere Messrs. Cubit\xe2\x80\x99s lot or the Bermondsey Good Templars, and could\r\nthey lend us a saucepan.\r\n\r\nOld ladies, not accustomed to the river, are always intensely nervous\r\nof steam launches. I remember going up once from Staines to Windsor\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nstretch of water peculiarly rich in these mechanical monstrosities\xe2\x80\x94with\r\na party containing three ladies of this description. It was very\r\nexciting. At the first glimpse of every steam launch that came in view,\r\nthey insisted on landing and sitting down on the bank until it was out\r\nof sight again. They said they were very sorry, but that they owed it\r\nto their families not to be fool-hardy.\r\n\r\nWe found ourselves short of water at Hambledon Lock; so we took our jar\r\nand went up to the lock-keeper\xe2\x80\x99s house to beg for some.\r\n\r\nGeorge was our spokesman. He put on a winning smile, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, please could you spare us a little water?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the old gentleman; \xe2\x80\x9ctake as much as you want, and\r\nleave the rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you so much,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured George, looking about him. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x94where\r\ndo you keep it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s always in the same place my boy,\xe2\x80\x9d was the stolid reply: \xe2\x80\x9cjust\r\nbehind you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see it,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, turning round.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, bless us, where\xe2\x80\x99s your eyes?\xe2\x80\x9d was the man\xe2\x80\x99s comment, as he\r\ntwisted George round and pointed up and down the stream. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nenough of it to see, ain\xe2\x80\x99t there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed George, grasping the idea; \xe2\x80\x9cbut we can\xe2\x80\x99t drink the\r\nriver, you know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; but you can drink _some_ of it,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the old fellow. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhat _I\xe2\x80\x99ve_ drunk for the last fifteen years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGeorge told him that his appearance, after the course, did not seem a\r\nsufficiently good advertisement for the brand; and that he would prefer\r\nit out of a pump.\r\n\r\nWe got some from a cottage a little higher up. I daresay _that_ was\r\nonly river water, if we had known. But we did not know, so it was all\r\nright. What the eye does not see, the stomach does not get upset over.\r\n\r\nWe tried river water once, later on in the season, but it was not a\r\nsuccess. We were coming down stream, and had pulled up to have tea in a\r\nbackwater near Windsor. Our jar was empty, and it was a case of going\r\nwithout our tea or taking water from the river. Harris was for chancing\r\nit. He said it must be all right if we boiled the water. He said that\r\nthe various germs of poison present in the water would be killed by the\r\nboiling. So we filled our kettle with Thames backwater, and boiled it;\r\nand very careful we were to see that it did boil.\r\n\r\nWe had made the tea, and were just settling down comfortably to\r\ndrink it, when George, with his cup half-way to his lips, paused and\r\nexclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s what?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Harris and I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy that!\xe2\x80\x9d said George, looking westward.\r\n\r\n[Picture: The dog] Harris and I followed his gaze, and saw, coming down\r\ntowards us on the sluggish current, a dog. It was one of the quietest\r\nand peacefullest dogs I have ever seen. I never met a dog who seemed\r\nmore contented\xe2\x80\x94more easy in its mind. It was floating dreamily on its\r\nback, with its four legs stuck up straight into the air. It was what I\r\nshould call a full-bodied dog, with a well-developed chest. On he came,\r\nserene, dignified, and calm, until he was abreast of our boat, and\r\nthere, among the rushes, he eased up, and settled down cosily for the\r\nevening.\r\n\r\nGeorge said he didn\xe2\x80\x99t want any tea, and emptied his cup into the water.\r\nHarris did not feel thirsty, either, and followed suit. I had drunk\r\nhalf mine, but I wished I had not.\r\n\r\nI asked George if he thought I was likely to have typhoid.\r\n\r\nHe said: \xe2\x80\x9cOh, no;\xe2\x80\x9d he thought I had a very good chance indeed of\r\nescaping it. Anyhow, I should know in about a fortnight, whether I had\r\nor had not.\r\n\r\nWe went up the backwater to Wargrave. It is a short cut, leading out\r\nof the right-hand bank about half a mile above Marsh Lock, and is well\r\nworth taking, being a pretty, shady little piece of stream, besides\r\nsaving nearly half a mile of distance.\r\n\r\nOf course, its entrance is studded with posts and chains, and\r\nsurrounded with notice boards, menacing all kinds of torture,\r\nimprisonment, and death to everyone who dares set scull upon its\r\nwaters\xe2\x80\x94I wonder some of these riparian boors don\xe2\x80\x99t claim the air of\r\nthe river and threaten everyone with forty shillings fine who breathes\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94but the posts and chains a little skill will easily avoid; and as\r\nfor the boards, you might, if you have five minutes to spare, and there\r\nis nobody about, take one or two of them down and throw them into the\r\nriver.\r\n\r\nHalf-way up the backwater, we got out and lunched; and it was during\r\nthis lunch that George and I received rather a trying shock.\r\n\r\nHarris received a shock, too; but I do not think Harris\xe2\x80\x99s shock could\r\nhave been anything like so bad as the shock that George and I had over\r\nthe business.\r\n\r\nYou see, it was in this way: we were sitting in a meadow, about ten\r\nyards from the water\xe2\x80\x99s edge, and we had just settled down comfortably\r\nto feed. Harris had the beefsteak pie between his knees, and was\r\ncarving it, and George and I were waiting with our plates ready.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you got a spoon there?\xe2\x80\x9d says Harris; \xe2\x80\x9cI want a spoon to help the\r\ngravy with.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe hamper was close behind us, and George and I both turned round to\r\nreach one out. We were not five seconds getting it. When we looked\r\nround again, Harris and the pie were gone!\r\n\r\nIt was a wide, open field. There was not a tree or a bit of hedge for\r\nhundreds of yards. He could not have tumbled into the river, because we\r\nwere on the water side of him, and he would have had to climb over us\r\nto do it.\r\n\r\nGeorge and I gazed all about. Then we gazed at each other.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas he been snatched up to heaven?\xe2\x80\x9d I queried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99d hardly have taken the pie too,\xe2\x80\x9d said George.\r\n\r\nThere seemed weight in this objection, and we discarded the heavenly\r\ntheory.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose the truth of the matter is,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested George, descending to\r\nthe commonplace and practicable, \xe2\x80\x9cthat there has been an earthquake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then he added, with a touch of sadness in his voice: \xe2\x80\x9cI wish he\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t been carving that pie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a sigh, we turned our eyes once more towards the spot where Harris\r\nand the pie had last been seen on earth; and there, as our blood froze\r\nin our veins and our hair stood up on end, we saw Harris\xe2\x80\x99s head\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nnothing but his head\xe2\x80\x94sticking bolt upright among the tall grass, the\r\nface very red, and bearing upon it an expression of great indignation!\r\n\r\nGeorge was the first to recover.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, \xe2\x80\x9cand tell us whether you are alive or dead\xe2\x80\x94and where\r\nis the rest of you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t be a stupid ass!\xe2\x80\x9d said Harris\xe2\x80\x99s head. \xe2\x80\x9cI believe you did it\r\non purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid what?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed George and I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, put me to sit here\xe2\x80\x94darn silly trick! Here, catch hold of the pie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Picture: Rescuing the pie] And out of the middle of the earth, as it\r\nseemed to us, rose the pie\xe2\x80\x94very much mixed up and damaged; and, after\r\nit, scrambled Harris\xe2\x80\x94tumbled, grubby, and wet.\r\n\r\nHe had been sitting, without knowing it, on the very verge of a small\r\ngully, the long grass hiding it from view; and in leaning a little back\r\nhe had shot over, pie and all.\r\n\r\nHe said he had never felt so surprised in all his life, as when he\r\nfirst felt himself going, without being able to conjecture in the\r\nslightest what had happened. He thought at first that the end of the\r\nworld had come.\r\n\r\nHarris believes to this day that George and I planned it all\r\nbeforehand. Thus does unjust suspicion follow even the most blameless\r\nfor, as the poet says, \xe2\x80\x9cWho shall escape calumny?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWho, indeed!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV.\r\n\r\n\r\n    Wargrave.\xe2\x80\x94Waxworks.\xe2\x80\x94Sonning.\xe2\x80\x94Our stew.\xe2\x80\x94Montmorency is\r\n    sarcastic.\xe2\x80\x94Fight between Montmorency and the tea-kettle.\xe2\x80\x94George\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n    banjo studies.\xe2\x80\x94Meet with discouragement.\xe2\x80\x94Difficulties in the way of\r\n    the musical amateur.\xe2\x80\x94Learning to play the bagpipes.\xe2\x80\x94Harris feels sad\r\n    after supper.\xe2\x80\x94George and I go for a walk.\xe2\x80\x94Return hungry and\r\n    wet.\xe2\x80\x94There is a strangeness about Harris.\xe2\x80\x94Harris and the swans, a\r\n    remarkable story.\xe2\x80\x94Harris has a troubled night.\r\n\r\nWe caught a breeze, after lunch, which took us gently up past Wargrave\r\nand Shiplake. Mellowed in the drowsy sunlight of a summer\xe2\x80\x99s afternoon,\r\nWargrave, nestling where the river bends, makes a sweet old picture as\r\nyou pass it, and one that lingers long upon the retina of memory.\r\n\r\nThe \xe2\x80\x9cGeorge and Dragon\xe2\x80\x9d at Wargrave boasts a sign, painted on the one\r\nside by Leslie, R.A., and on the other by Hodgson of that ilk. Leslie\r\nhas depicted the fight; Hodgson has imagined the scene, \xe2\x80\x9cAfter the\r\nFight\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94George, the work done, enjoying his pint of beer.\r\n\r\nDay, the author of _Sandford and Merton_, lived and\xe2\x80\x94more credit to the\r\nplace still\xe2\x80\x94was killed at Wargrave. In the church is a memorial to Mrs.\r\nSarah Hill, who bequeathed 1 pound annually, to be divided at Easter,\r\nbetween two boys and two girls who \xe2\x80\x9chave never been undutiful to their\r\nparents; who have never been known to swear or to tell untruths,\r\nto steal, or to break windows.\xe2\x80\x9d Fancy giving up all that for five\r\nshillings a year! It is not worth it.\r\n\r\nIt is rumoured in the town that once, many years ago, a boy appeared\r\nwho really never had done these things\xe2\x80\x94or at all events, which was all\r\nthat was required or could be expected, had never been known to do\r\nthem\xe2\x80\x94and thus won the crown of glory. He was exhibited for three weeks\r\nafterwards in the Town Hall, under a glass case.\r\n\r\nWhat has become of the money since no one knows. They say it is always\r\nhanded over to the nearest wax-works show.\r\n\r\nShiplake is a pretty village, but it cannot be seen from the river,\r\nbeing upon the hill. Tennyson was married in Shiplake Church.\r\n\r\nThe river up to Sonning winds in and out through many islands, and\r\nis very placid, hushed, and lonely. Few folk, except at twilight, a\r\npair or two of rustic lovers, walk along its banks. \xe2\x80\x99Arry and Lord\r\nFitznoodle have been left behind at Henley, and dismal, dirty Reading\r\nis not yet reached. It is a part of the river in which to dream of\r\nbygone days, and vanished forms and faces, and things that might have\r\nbeen, but are not, confound them.\r\n\r\nWe got out at Sonning, and went for a walk round the village. It is\r\nthe most fairy-like little nook on the whole river. It is more like\r\na stage village than one built of bricks and mortar. Every house is\r\nsmothered in roses, and now, in early June, they were bursting forth\r\nin clouds of dainty splendour. If you stop at Sonning, put up at the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBull,\xe2\x80\x9d behind the church. It is a veritable picture of an old country\r\ninn, with green, square courtyard in front, where, on seats beneath the\r\ntrees, the old men group of an evening to drink their ale and gossip\r\nover village politics; with low, quaint rooms and latticed windows, and\r\nawkward stairs and winding passages.\r\n\r\nWe roamed about sweet Sonning for an hour or so, and then, it being\r\ntoo late to push on past Reading, we decided to go back to one of the\r\nShiplake islands, and put up there for the night. It was still early\r\nwhen we got settled, and George said that, as we had plenty of time, it\r\nwould be a splendid opportunity to try a good, slap-up supper. He said\r\nhe would show us what could be done up the river in the way of cooking,\r\nand suggested that, with the vegetables and the remains of the cold\r\nbeef and general odds and ends, we should make an Irish stew.\r\n\r\nIt seemed a fascinating idea. George gathered wood and made a fire, and\r\nHarris and I started to peel the potatoes. I should never have thought\r\nthat peeling potatoes was such an undertaking. The job turned out to\r\nbe the biggest thing of its kind that I had ever been in. We began\r\ncheerfully, one might almost say skittishly, but our light-heartedness\r\nwas gone by the time the first potato was finished. The more we peeled,\r\nthe more peel there seemed to be left on; by the time we had got all\r\nthe peel off and all the eyes out, there was no potato left\xe2\x80\x94at least\r\nnone worth speaking of. George came and had a look at it\xe2\x80\x94it was about\r\nthe size of a pea-nut. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that won\xe2\x80\x99t do! You\xe2\x80\x99re wasting them. You must scrape them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo we scraped them, and that was harder work than peeling. They are\r\nsuch an extraordinary shape, potatoes\xe2\x80\x94all bumps and warts and hollows.\r\nWe worked steadily for five-and-twenty minutes, and did four potatoes.\r\nThen we struck. We said we should require the rest of the evening for\r\nscraping ourselves.\r\n\r\nI never saw such a thing as potato-scraping for making a fellow in a\r\nmess. It seemed difficult to believe that the potato-scrapings in which\r\nHarris and I stood, half smothered, could have come off four potatoes.\r\nIt shows you what can be done with economy and care.\r\n\r\nGeorge said it was absurd to have only four potatoes in an Irish stew,\r\nso we washed half-a-dozen or so more, and put them in without peeling.\r\nWe also put in a cabbage and about half a peck of peas. George stirred\r\nit all up, and then he said that there seemed to be a lot of room to\r\nspare, so we overhauled both the hampers, and picked out all the odds\r\nand ends and the remnants, and added them to the stew. There were half\r\na pork pie and a bit of cold boiled bacon left, and we put them in.\r\nThen George found half a tin of potted salmon, and he emptied that into\r\nthe pot.\r\n\r\nHe said that was the advantage of Irish stew: you got rid of such a lot\r\nof things. I fished out a couple of eggs that had got cracked, and put\r\nthose in. George said they would thicken the gravy.\r\n\r\nI forget the other ingredients, but I know nothing was wasted; and I\r\nremember that, towards the end, Montmorency, who had evinced great\r\ninterest in the proceedings throughout, strolled away with an earnest\r\nand thoughtful air, reappearing, a few minutes afterwards, with a dead\r\nwater-rat in his mouth, which he evidently wished to present as his\r\ncontribution to the dinner; whether in a sarcastic spirit, or with a\r\ngenuine desire to assist, I cannot say.\r\n\r\nWe had a discussion as to whether the rat should go in or not.\r\nHarris said that he thought it would be all right, mixed up with the\r\nother things, and that every little helped; but George stood up for\r\nprecedent. He said he had never heard of water-rats in Irish stew, and\r\nhe would rather be on the safe side, and not try experiments.\r\n\r\nHarris said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you never try a new thing, how can you tell what it\xe2\x80\x99s like? It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmen such as you that hamper the world\xe2\x80\x99s progress. Think of the man who\r\nfirst tried German sausage!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a great success, that Irish stew. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I ever enjoyed\r\na meal more. There was something so fresh and piquant about it. One\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npalate gets so tired of the old hackneyed things: here was a dish with\r\na new flavour, with a taste like nothing else on earth.\r\n\r\nAnd it was nourishing, too. As George said, there was good stuff in\r\nit. The peas and potatoes might have been a bit softer, but we all had\r\ngood teeth, so that did not matter much: and as for the gravy, it was a\r\npoem\xe2\x80\x94a little too rich, perhaps, for a weak stomach, but nutritious.\r\n\r\nWe finished up with tea and cherry tart. Montmorency had a fight with\r\nthe kettle during tea-time, and came off a poor second.\r\n\r\nThroughout the trip, he had manifested great curiosity concerning\r\nthe kettle. He would sit and watch it, as it boiled, with a puzzled\r\nexpression, and would try and rouse it every now and then by growling\r\nat it. When it began to splutter and steam, he regarded it as a\r\nchallenge, and would want to fight it, only, at that precise moment,\r\nsome one would always dash up and bear off his prey before he could get\r\nat it.\r\n\r\nTo-day he determined he would be beforehand. At the first sound\r\nthe kettle made, he rose, growling, and advanced towards it in a\r\nthreatening attitude. It was only a little kettle, but it was full of\r\npluck, and it up and spit at him.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Montmorency and the kettle] \xe2\x80\x9cAh! would ye!\xe2\x80\x9d growled\r\nMontmorency, showing his teeth; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll teach ye to cheek a hard-working,\r\nrespectable dog; ye miserable, long-nosed, dirty-looking scoundrel, ye.\r\nCome on!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he rushed at that poor little kettle, and seized it by the spout.\r\n\r\nThen, across the evening stillness, broke a blood-curdling yelp, and\r\nMontmorency left the boat, and did a constitutional three times round\r\nthe island at the rate of thirty-five miles an hour, stopping every now\r\nand then to bury his nose in a bit of cool mud.\r\n\r\nFrom that day Montmorency regarded the kettle with a mixture of awe,\r\nsuspicion, and hate. Whenever he saw it he would growl and back at a\r\nrapid rate, with his tail shut down, and the moment it was put upon the\r\nstove he would promptly climb out of the boat, and sit on the bank,\r\ntill the whole tea business was over.\r\n\r\nGeorge got out his banjo after supper, and wanted to play it, but\r\nHarris objected: he said he had got a headache, and did not feel strong\r\nenough to stand it. George thought the music might do him good\xe2\x80\x94said\r\nmusic often soothed the nerves and took away a headache; and he twanged\r\ntwo or three notes, just to show Harris what it was like.\r\n\r\nHarris said he would rather have the headache.\r\n\r\nGeorge has never learned to play the banjo to this day. He has had\r\ntoo much all-round discouragement to meet. He tried on two or three\r\nevenings, while we were up the river, to get a little practice, but it\r\nwas never a success. Harris\xe2\x80\x99s language used to be enough to unnerve any\r\nman; added to which, Montmorency would sit and howl steadily, right\r\nthrough the performance. It was not giving the man a fair chance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s he want to howl like that for when I\xe2\x80\x99m playing?\xe2\x80\x9d George would\r\nexclaim indignantly, while taking aim at him with a boot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want to play like that for when he is howling?\xe2\x80\x9d Harris\r\nwould retort, catching the boot. \xe2\x80\x9cYou let him alone. He can\xe2\x80\x99t help\r\nhowling. He\xe2\x80\x99s got a musical ear, and your playing _makes_ him howl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo George determined to postpone study of the banjo until he reached\r\nhome. But he did not get much opportunity even there. Mrs. P. used\r\nto come up and say she was very sorry\xe2\x80\x94for herself, she liked to hear\r\nhim\xe2\x80\x94but the lady upstairs was in a very delicate state, and the doctor\r\nwas afraid it might injure the child.\r\n\r\nThen George tried taking it out with him late at night, and practising\r\nround the square. But the inhabitants complained to the police about\r\nit, and a watch was set for him one night, and he was captured. The\r\nevidence against him was very clear, and he was bound over to keep the\r\npeace for six months.\r\n\r\nHe seemed to lose heart in the business after that. He did make one\r\nor two feeble efforts to take up the work again when the six months\r\nhad elapsed, but there was always the same coldness\xe2\x80\x94the same want of\r\nsympathy on the part of the world to fight against; and, after awhile,\r\nhe despaired altogether, and advertised the instrument for sale at a\r\ngreat sacrifice\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cowner having no further use for same\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and took to\r\nlearning card tricks instead.\r\n\r\nIt must be disheartening work learning a musical instrument. You would\r\nthink that Society, for its own sake, would do all it could to assist a\r\nman to acquire the art of playing a musical instrument. But it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t!\r\n\r\nI knew a young fellow once, who was studying to play the bagpipes, and\r\nyou would be surprised at the amount of opposition he had to contend\r\nwith. Why, not even from the members of his own family did he receive\r\nwhat you could call active encouragement. His father was dead against\r\nthe business from the beginning, and spoke quite unfeelingly on the\r\nsubject.\r\n\r\nMy friend used to get up early in the morning to practise, but he\r\nhad to give that plan up, because of his sister. She was somewhat\r\nreligiously inclined, and she said it seemed such an awful thing to\r\nbegin the day like that.\r\n\r\nSo he sat up at night instead, and played after the family had gone to\r\nbed, but that did not do, as it got the house such a bad name. People,\r\ngoing home late, would stop outside to listen, and then put it about\r\nall over the town, the next morning, that a fearful murder had been\r\ncommitted at Mr. Jefferson\xe2\x80\x99s the night before; and would describe how\r\nthey had heard the victim\xe2\x80\x99s shrieks and the brutal oaths and curses\r\nof the murderer, followed by the prayer for mercy, and the last dying\r\ngurgle of the corpse.\r\n\r\nSo they let him practise in the day-time, in the back-kitchen with all\r\nthe doors shut; but his more successful passages could generally be\r\nheard in the sitting-room, in spite of these precautions, and would\r\naffect his mother almost to tears.\r\n\r\nShe said it put her in mind of her poor father (he had been swallowed\r\nby a shark, poor man, while bathing off the coast of New Guinea\xe2\x80\x94where\r\nthe connection came in, she could not explain).\r\n\r\nThen they knocked up a little place for him at the bottom of the\r\ngarden, about quarter of a mile from the house, and made him take the\r\nmachine down there when he wanted to work it; and sometimes a visitor\r\nwould come to the house who knew nothing of the matter, and they would\r\nforget to tell him all about it, and caution him, and he would go out\r\nfor a stroll round the garden and suddenly get within earshot of those\r\nbagpipes, without being prepared for it, or knowing what it was. If he\r\nwere a man of strong mind, it only gave him fits; but a person of mere\r\naverage intellect it usually sent mad.\r\n\r\nThere is, it must be confessed, something very sad about the early\r\nefforts of an amateur in bagpipes. I have felt that myself when\r\nlistening to my young friend. They appear to be a trying instrument to\r\nperform upon. You have to get enough breath for the whole tune before\r\nyou start\xe2\x80\x94at least, so I gathered from watching Jefferson.\r\n\r\nHe would begin magnificently with a wild, full, come-to-the-battle sort\r\nof a note, that quite roused you. But he would get more and more piano\r\nas he went on, and the last verse generally collapsed in the middle\r\nwith a splutter and a hiss.\r\n\r\nYou want to be in good health to play the bagpipes.\r\n\r\nYoung Jefferson only learnt to play one tune on those bagpipes;\r\nbut I never heard any complaints about the insufficiency of his\r\nrepertoire\xe2\x80\x94none whatever. This tune was \xe2\x80\x9cThe Campbells are Coming,\r\nHooray\xe2\x80\x94Hooray!\xe2\x80\x9d so he said, though his father always held that it was\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Blue Bells of Scotland.\xe2\x80\x9d Nobody seemed quite sure what it was\r\nexactly, but they all agreed that it sounded Scotch.\r\n\r\nStrangers were allowed three guesses, and most of them guessed a\r\ndifferent tune each time.\r\n\r\nHarris was disagreeable after supper,\xe2\x80\x94I think it must have been the\r\nstew that had upset him: he is not used to high living,\xe2\x80\x94so George and\r\nI left him in the boat, and settled to go for a mouch round Henley. He\r\nsaid he should have a glass of whisky and a pipe, and fix things up for\r\nthe night. We were to shout when we returned, and he would row over\r\nfrom the island and fetch us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t go to sleep, old man,\xe2\x80\x9d we said as we started.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot much fear of that while this stew\xe2\x80\x99s on,\xe2\x80\x9d he grunted, as he pulled\r\nback to the island.\r\n\r\nHenley was getting ready for the regatta, and was full of bustle.\r\nWe met a goodish number of men we knew about the town, and in their\r\npleasant company the time slipped by somewhat quickly; so that it was\r\nnearly eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock before we set off on our four-mile walk home\xe2\x80\x94as\r\nwe had learned to call our little craft by this time.\r\n\r\nIt was a dismal night, coldish, with a thin rain falling; and as we\r\ntrudged through the dark, silent fields, talking low to each other, and\r\nwondering if we were going right or not, we thought of the cosy boat,\r\nwith the bright light streaming through the tight-drawn canvas; of\r\nHarris and Montmorency, and the whisky, and wished that we were there.\r\n\r\nWe conjured up the picture of ourselves inside, tired and a little\r\nhungry; of the gloomy river and the shapeless trees; and, like a giant\r\nglow-worm underneath them, our dear old boat, so snug and warm and\r\ncheerful. We could see ourselves at supper there, pecking away at cold\r\nmeat, and passing each other chunks of bread; we could hear the cheery\r\nclatter of our knives, the laughing voices, filling all the space, and\r\noverflowing through the opening out into the night. And we hurried on\r\nto realise the vision.\r\n\r\nWe struck the tow-path at length, and that made us happy; because\r\nprior to this we had not been sure whether we were walking towards the\r\nriver or away from it, and when you are tired and want to go to bed\r\nuncertainties like that worry you. We passed Skiplake as the clock was\r\nstriking the quarter to twelve; and then George said, thoughtfully:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t happen to remember which of the islands it was, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied, beginning to grow thoughtful too, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t. How many\r\nare there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly four,\xe2\x80\x9d answered George. \xe2\x80\x9cIt will be all right, if he\xe2\x80\x99s awake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if not?\xe2\x80\x9d I queried; but we dismissed that train of thought.\r\n\r\nWe shouted when we came opposite the first island, but there was no\r\nresponse; so we went to the second, and tried there, and obtained the\r\nsame result.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! I remember now,\xe2\x80\x9d said George; \xe2\x80\x9cit was the third one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd we ran on hopefully to the third one, and hallooed.\r\n\r\nNo answer!\r\n\r\nThe case was becoming serious. it was now past midnight. The hotels\r\nat Skiplake and Henley would be crammed; and we could not go round,\r\nknocking up cottagers and householders in the middle of the night, to\r\nknow if they let apartments! George suggested walking back to Henley\r\nand assaulting a policeman, and so getting a night\xe2\x80\x99s lodging in the\r\nstation-house. But then there was the thought, \xe2\x80\x9cSuppose he only hits us\r\nback and refuses to lock us up!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe could not pass the whole night fighting policemen. Besides, we did\r\nnot want to overdo the thing and get six months.\r\n\r\nWe despairingly tried what seemed in the darkness to be the fourth\r\nisland, but met with no better success. The rain was coming down fast\r\nnow, and evidently meant to last. We were wet to the skin, and cold and\r\nmiserable. We began to wonder whether there were only four islands or\r\nmore, or whether we were near the islands at all, or whether we were\r\nanywhere within a mile of where we ought to be, or in the wrong part\r\nof the river altogether; everything looked so strange and different in\r\nthe darkness. We began to understand the sufferings of the Babes in the\r\nWood.\r\n\r\nJust when we had given up all hope\xe2\x80\x94yes, I know that is always the\r\ntime that things do happen in novels and tales; but I can\xe2\x80\x99t help it.\r\nI resolved, when I began to write this book, that I would be strictly\r\ntruthful in all things; and so I will be, even if I have to employ\r\nhackneyed phrases for the purpose.\r\n\r\nIt _was_ just when we had given up all hope, and I must therefore say\r\nso. Just when we had given up all hope, then, I suddenly caught sight,\r\na little way below us, of a strange, weird sort of glimmer flickering\r\namong the trees on the opposite bank. For an instant I thought of\r\nghosts: it was such a shadowy, mysterious light. The next moment it\r\nflashed across me that it was our boat, and I sent up such a yell\r\nacross the water that made the night seem to shake in its bed.\r\n\r\nWe waited breathless for a minute, and then\xe2\x80\x94oh! divinest music of the\r\ndarkness!\xe2\x80\x94we heard the answering bark of Montmorency. We shouted back\r\nloud enough to wake the Seven Sleepers\xe2\x80\x94I never could understand myself\r\nwhy it should take more noise to wake seven sleepers than one\xe2\x80\x94and,\r\nafter what seemed an hour, but what was really, I suppose, about five\r\nminutes, we saw the lighted boat creeping slowly over the blackness,\r\nand heard Harris\xe2\x80\x99s sleepy voice asking where we were.\r\n\r\nThere was an unaccountable strangeness about Harris. It was something\r\nmore than mere ordinary tiredness. He pulled the boat against a part of\r\nthe bank from which it was quite impossible for us to get into it, and\r\nimmediately went to sleep. It took us an immense amount of screaming\r\nand roaring to wake him up again and put some sense into him; but we\r\nsucceeded at last, and got safely on board.\r\n\r\nHarris had a sad expression on him, so we noticed, when we got into the\r\nboat. He gave you the idea of a man who had been through trouble. We\r\nasked him if anything had happened, and he said\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n[Picture: Swans] \xe2\x80\x9cSwans!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt seemed we had moored close to a swan\xe2\x80\x99s nest, and, soon after George\r\nand I had gone, the female swan came back, and kicked up a row about\r\nit. Harris had chivied her off, and she had gone away, and fetched up\r\nher old man. Harris said he had had quite a fight with these two swans;\r\nbut courage and skill had prevailed in the end, and he had defeated\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nHalf-an-hour afterwards they returned with eighteen other swans! It\r\nmust have been a fearful battle, so far as we could understand Harris\xe2\x80\x99s\r\naccount of it. The swans had tried to drag him and Montmorency out of\r\nthe boat and drown them; and he had defended himself like a hero for\r\nfour hours, and had killed the lot, and they had all paddled away to\r\ndie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many swans did you say there were?\xe2\x80\x9d asked George.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThirty-two,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Harris, sleepily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said eighteen just now,\xe2\x80\x9d said George.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d grunted Harris; \xe2\x80\x9cI said twelve. Think I can\xe2\x80\x99t count?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat were the real facts about these swans we never found out. We\r\nquestioned Harris on the subject in the morning, and he said, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\nswans?\xe2\x80\x9d and seemed to think that George and I had been dreaming.\r\n\r\nOh, how delightful it was to be safe in the boat, after our trials and\r\nfears! We ate a hearty supper, George and I, and we should have had\r\nsome toddy after it, if we could have found the whisky, but we could\r\nnot. We examined Harris as to what he had done with it; but he did not\r\nseem to know what we meant by \xe2\x80\x9cwhisky,\xe2\x80\x9d or what we were talking about\r\nat all. Montmorency looked as if he knew something, but said nothing.\r\n\r\nI slept well that night, and should have slept better if it had not\r\nbeen for Harris. I have a vague recollection of having been woke up at\r\nleast a dozen times during the night by Harris wandering about the boat\r\nwith the lantern, looking for his clothes. He seemed to be worrying\r\nabout his clothes all night.\r\n\r\nTwice he routed up George and myself to see if we were lying on his\r\ntrousers. George got quite wild the second time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat the thunder do you want your trousers for, in the middle of the\r\nnight?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked indignantly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you lie down, and go to sleep?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI found him in trouble, the next time I awoke, because he could not\r\nfind his socks; and my last hazy remembrance is of being rolled over on\r\nmy side, and of hearing Harris muttering something about its being an\r\nextraordinary thing where his umbrella could have got to.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV.\r\n\r\n\r\nHousehold duties.\xe2\x80\x94Love of work.\xe2\x80\x94The old river hand, what he\r\ndoes and what he tells you he has done.\xe2\x80\x94Scepticism of the new\r\ngeneration.\xe2\x80\x94Early boating recollections.\xe2\x80\x94Rafting.\xe2\x80\x94George does the thing\r\nin style.\xe2\x80\x94The old boatman, his method.\xe2\x80\x94So calm, so full of peace.\xe2\x80\x94The\r\nbeginner.\xe2\x80\x94Punting.\xe2\x80\x94A sad accident.\xe2\x80\x94Pleasures of friendship.\xe2\x80\x94Sailing, my\r\nfirst experience.\xe2\x80\x94Possible reason why we were not drowned.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Woman at housework] We woke late the next morning, and,\r\nat Harris\xe2\x80\x99s earnest desire, partook of a plain breakfast, with \xe2\x80\x9cnon\r\ndainties.\xe2\x80\x9d Then we cleaned up, and put everything straight (a continual\r\nlabour, which was beginning to afford me a pretty clear insight into\r\na question that had often posed me\xe2\x80\x94namely, how a woman with the work\r\nof only one house on her hands manages to pass away her time), and, at\r\nabout ten, set out on what we had determined should be a good day\xe2\x80\x99s\r\njourney.\r\n\r\nWe agreed that we would pull this morning, as a change from towing; and\r\nHarris thought the best arrangement would be that George and I should\r\nscull, and he steer. I did not chime in with this idea at all; I said I\r\nthought Harris would have been showing a more proper spirit if he had\r\nsuggested that he and George should work, and let me rest a bit. It\r\nseemed to me that I was doing more than my fair share of the work on\r\nthis trip, and I was beginning to feel strongly on the subject.\r\n\r\nIt always does seem to me that I am doing more work than I should\r\ndo. It is not that I object to the work, mind you; I like work: it\r\nfascinates me. I can sit and look at it for hours. I love to keep it by\r\nme: the idea of getting rid of it nearly breaks my heart.\r\n\r\nYou cannot give me too much work; to accumulate work has almost become\r\na passion with me: my study is so full of it now, that there is hardly\r\nan inch of room for any more. I shall have to throw out a wing soon.\r\n\r\nAnd I am careful of my work, too.
