Why, some of the work that I have by\r\nme now has been in my possession for years and years, and there isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\na finger-mark on it. I take a great pride in my work; I take it down\r\nnow and then and dust it. No man keeps his work in a better state of\r\npreservation than I do.\r\n\r\nBut, though I crave for work, I still like to be fair. I do not ask for\r\nmore than my proper share.\r\n\r\nBut I get it without asking for it\xe2\x80\x94at least, so it appears to me\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nthis worries me.\r\n\r\nGeorge says he does not think I need trouble myself on the subject. He\r\nthinks it is only my over-scrupulous nature that makes me fear I am\r\nhaving more than my due; and that, as a matter of fact, I don\xe2\x80\x99t have\r\nhalf as much as I ought. But I expect he only says this to comfort me.\r\n\r\nIn a boat, I have always noticed that it is the fixed idea of each\r\nmember of the crew that he is doing everything. Harris\xe2\x80\x99s notion was,\r\nthat it was he alone who had been working, and that both George and I\r\nhad been imposing upon him. George, on the other hand, ridiculed the\r\nidea of Harris\xe2\x80\x99s having done anything more than eat and sleep, and had\r\na cast-iron opinion that it was he\xe2\x80\x94George himself\xe2\x80\x94who had done all the\r\nlabour worth speaking of.\r\n\r\nHe said he had never been out with such a couple of lazily skulks as\r\nHarris and I.\r\n\r\nThat amused Harris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFancy old George talking about work!\xe2\x80\x9d he laughed; \xe2\x80\x9cwhy, about\r\nhalf-an-hour of it would kill him. Have you ever seen George work?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nadded, turning to me.\r\n\r\nI agreed with Harris that I never had\xe2\x80\x94most certainly not since we had\r\nstarted on this trip.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t see how _you_ can know much about it, one way or the\r\nother,\xe2\x80\x9d George retorted on Harris; \xe2\x80\x9cfor I\xe2\x80\x99m blest if you haven\xe2\x80\x99t been\r\nasleep half the time. Have you ever seen Harris fully awake, except at\r\nmeal-time?\xe2\x80\x9d asked George, addressing me.\r\n\r\nTruth compelled me to support George. Harris had been very little good\r\nin the boat, so far as helping was concerned, from the beginning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, hang it all, I\xe2\x80\x99ve done more than old J., anyhow,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined\r\nHarris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t very well have done less,\xe2\x80\x9d added George.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose J. thinks he is the passenger,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Harris.\r\n\r\nAnd that was their gratitude to me for having brought them and their\r\nwretched old boat all the way up from Kingston, and for having\r\nsuperintended and managed everything for them, and taken care of them,\r\nand slaved for them. It is the way of the world.\r\n\r\nWe settled the present difficulty by arranging that Harris and George\r\nshould scull up past Reading, and that I should tow the boat on from\r\nthere. Pulling a heavy boat against a strong stream has few attractions\r\nfor me now. There was a time, long ago, when I used to clamour for the\r\nhard work: now I like to give the youngsters a chance.\r\n\r\nI notice that most of the old river hands are similarly retiring,\r\nwhenever there is any stiff pulling to be done. You can always tell\r\nthe old river hand by the way in which he stretches himself out upon\r\nthe cushions at the bottom of the boat, and encourages the rowers by\r\ntelling them anecdotes about the marvellous feats he performed last\r\nseason.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCall what you\xe2\x80\x99re doing hard work!\xe2\x80\x9d he drawls, between his contented\r\nwhiffs, addressing the two perspiring novices, who have been grinding\r\naway steadily up stream for the last hour and a half; \xe2\x80\x9cwhy, Jim Biffles\r\nand Jack and I, last season, pulled up from Marlow to Goring in one\r\nafternoon\xe2\x80\x94never stopped once. Do you remember that, Jack?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJack, who has made himself a bed up in the prow of all the rugs and\r\ncoats he can collect, and who has been lying there asleep for the last\r\ntwo hours, partially wakes up on being thus appealed to, and recollects\r\nall about the matter, and also remembers that there was an unusually\r\nstrong stream against them all the way\xe2\x80\x94likewise a stiff wind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout thirty-four miles, I suppose, it must have been,\xe2\x80\x9d adds the first\r\nspeaker, reaching down another cushion to put under his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94no; don\xe2\x80\x99t exaggerate, Tom,\xe2\x80\x9d murmurs Jack, reprovingly;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthirty-three at the outside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Jack and Tom, quite exhausted by this conversational effort, drop\r\noff to sleep once more. And the two simple-minded youngsters at the\r\nsculls feel quite proud of being allowed to row such wonderful oarsmen\r\nas Jack and Tom, and strain away harder than ever.\r\n\r\nWhen I was a young man, I used to listen to these tales from my elders,\r\nand take them in, and swallow them, and digest every word of them,\r\nand then come up for more; but the new generation do not seem to have\r\nthe simple faith of the old times. We\xe2\x80\x94George, Harris, and myself\xe2\x80\x94took\r\na \xe2\x80\x9craw \xe2\x80\x99un\xe2\x80\x9d up with us once last season, and we plied him with the\r\ncustomary stretchers about the wonderful things we had done all the way\r\nup.\r\n\r\nWe gave him all the regular ones\xe2\x80\x94the time-honoured lies that have done\r\nduty up the river with every boating-man for years past\xe2\x80\x94and added seven\r\nentirely original ones that we had invented for ourselves, including\r\na really quite likely story, founded, to a certain extent, on an all\r\nbut true episode, which had actually happened in a modified degree\r\nsome years ago to friends of ours\xe2\x80\x94a story that a mere child could have\r\nbelieved without injuring itself, much.\r\n\r\nAnd that young man mocked at them all, and wanted us to repeat the\r\nfeats then and there, and to bet us ten to one that we didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\r\n\r\nWe got to chatting about our rowing experiences this morning, and to\r\nrecounting stories of our first efforts in the art of oarsmanship.\r\nMy own earliest boating recollection is of five of us contributing\r\nthreepence each and taking out a curiously constructed craft on the\r\nRegent\xe2\x80\x99s Park lake, drying ourselves subsequently, in the park-keeper\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlodge.\r\n\r\nAfter that, having acquired a taste for the water, I did a good deal\r\nof rafting in various suburban brickfields\xe2\x80\x94an exercise providing more\r\ninterest and excitement than might be imagined, especially when you are\r\nin the middle of the pond and the proprietor of the materials of which\r\nthe raft is constructed suddenly appears on the bank, with a big stick\r\nin his hand.\r\n\r\nYour first sensation on seeing this gentleman is that, somehow or\r\nother, you don\xe2\x80\x99t feel equal to company and conversation, and that, if\r\nyou could do so without appearing rude, you would rather avoid meeting\r\nhim; and your object is, therefore, to get off on the opposite side of\r\nthe pond to which he is, and to go home quietly and quickly, pretending\r\nnot to see him. He, on the contrary is yearning to take you by the\r\nhand, and talk to you.\r\n\r\nIt appears that he knows your father, and is intimately acquainted\r\nwith yourself, but this does not draw you towards him. He says he\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nteach you to take his boards and make a raft of them; but, seeing\r\nthat you know how to do this pretty well already, the offer, though\r\ndoubtless kindly meant, seems a superfluous one on his part, and you\r\nare reluctant to put him to any trouble by accepting it.\r\n\r\nHis anxiety to meet you, however, is proof against all your coolness,\r\nand the energetic manner in which he dodges up and down the pond so as\r\nto be on the spot to greet you when you land is really quite flattering.\r\n\r\nIf he be of a stout and short-winded build, you can easily avoid his\r\nadvances; but, when he is of the youthful and long-legged type, a\r\nmeeting is inevitable. The interview is, however, extremely brief,\r\nmost of the conversation being on his part, your remarks being mostly\r\nof an exclamatory and mono-syllabic order, and as soon as you can tear\r\nyourself away you do so.\r\n\r\nI devoted some three months to rafting, and, being then as proficient\r\nas there was any need to be at that branch of the art, I determined to\r\ngo in for rowing proper, and joined one of the Lea boating clubs.\r\n\r\nBeing out in a boat on the river Lea, especially on Saturday\r\nafternoons, soon makes you smart at handling a craft, and spry at\r\nescaping being run down by roughs or swamped by barges; and it also\r\naffords plenty of opportunity for acquiring the most prompt and\r\ngraceful method of lying down flat at the bottom of the boat so as to\r\navoid being chucked out into the river by passing tow-lines.\r\n\r\nBut it does not give you style. It was not till I came to the Thames\r\nthat I got style. My style of rowing is very much admired now. People\r\nsay it is so quaint.\r\n\r\nGeorge never went near the water until he was sixteen. Then he and\r\neight other gentlemen of about the same age went down in a body to Kew\r\none Saturday, with the idea of hiring a boat there, and pulling to\r\nRichmond and back; one of their number, a shock-headed youth, named\r\nJoskins, who had once or twice taken out a boat on the Serpentine, told\r\nthem it was jolly fun, boating!\r\n\r\nThe tide was running out pretty rapidly when they reached the\r\nlanding-stage, and there was a stiff breeze blowing across the river,\r\nbut this did not trouble them at all, and they proceeded to select\r\ntheir boat.\r\n\r\nThere was an eight-oared racing outrigger drawn up on the stage; that\r\nwas the one that took their fancy. They said they\xe2\x80\x99d have that one,\r\nplease. The boatman was away, and only his boy was in charge. The boy\r\ntried to damp their ardour for the outrigger, and showed them two or\r\nthree very comfortable-looking boats of the family-party build, but\r\nthose would not do at all; the outrigger was the boat they thought they\r\nwould look best in.\r\n\r\nSo the boy launched it, and they took off their coats and prepared to\r\ntake their seats. The boy suggested that George, who, even in those\r\ndays, was always the heavy man of any party, should be number four.\r\nGeorge said he should be happy to be number four, and promptly stepped\r\ninto bow\xe2\x80\x99s place, and sat down with his back to the stern. They got him\r\ninto his proper position at last, and then the others followed.\r\n\r\nA particularly nervous boy was appointed cox, and the steering\r\nprinciple explained to him by Joskins. Joskins himself took stroke. He\r\ntold the others that it was simple enough; all they had to do was to\r\nfollow him.\r\n\r\nThey said they were ready, and the boy on the landing stage took a\r\nboat-hook and shoved him off.\r\n\r\nWhat then followed George is unable to describe in detail. He has a\r\nconfused recollection of having, immediately on starting, received\r\na violent blow in the small of the back from the butt-end of number\r\nfive\xe2\x80\x99s scull, at the same time that his own seat seemed to disappear\r\nfrom under him by magic, and leave him sitting on the boards. He also\r\nnoticed, as a curious circumstance, that number two was at the same\r\ninstant lying on his back at the bottom of the boat, with his legs in\r\nthe air, apparently in a fit.\r\n\r\nThey passed under Kew Bridge, broadside, at the rate of eight miles an\r\nhour. Joskins being the only one who was rowing. George, on recovering\r\nhis seat, tried to help him, but, on dipping his oar into the water, it\r\nimmediately, to his intense surprise, disappeared under the boat, and\r\nnearly took him with it.\r\n\r\nAnd then \xe2\x80\x9ccox\xe2\x80\x9d threw both rudder lines over-board, and burst into tears.\r\n\r\nHow they got back George never knew, but it took them just forty\r\nminutes. A dense crowd watched the entertainment from Kew Bridge with\r\nmuch interest, and everybody shouted out to them different directions.\r\nThree times they managed to get the boat back through the arch, and\r\nthree times they were carried under it again, and every time \xe2\x80\x9ccox\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nlooked up and saw the bridge above him he broke out into renewed sobs.\r\n\r\nGeorge said he little thought that afternoon that he should ever come\r\nto really like boating.\r\n\r\nHarris is more accustomed to sea rowing than to river work, and says\r\nthat, as an exercise, he prefers it. I don\xe2\x80\x99t. I remember taking a small\r\nboat out at Eastbourne last summer: I used to do a good deal of sea\r\nrowing years ago, and I thought I should be all right; but I found I\r\nhad forgotten the art entirely. When one scull was deep down underneath\r\nthe water, the other would be flourishing wildly about in the air. To\r\nget a grip of the water with both at the same time I had to stand up.\r\nThe parade was crowded with nobility and gentry, and I had to pull past\r\nthem in this ridiculous fashion. I landed half-way down the beach, and\r\nsecured the services of an old boatman to take me back.\r\n\r\nI like to watch an old boatman rowing, especially one who has been\r\nhired by the hour. There is something so beautifully calm and restful\r\nabout his method. It is so free from that fretful haste, that\r\nvehement striving, that is every day becoming more and more the bane\r\nof nineteenth-century life. He is not for ever straining himself to\r\npass all the other boats. If another boat overtakes him and passes\r\nhim it does not annoy him; as a matter of fact, they all do overtake\r\nhim and pass him\xe2\x80\x94all those that are going his way. This would trouble\r\nand irritate some people; the sublime equanimity of the hired boatman\r\nunder the ordeal affords us a beautiful lesson against ambition and\r\nuppishness.\r\n\r\nPlain practical rowing of the get-the-boat-along order is not a very\r\ndifficult art to acquire, but it takes a good deal of practice before\r\na man feels comfortable, when rowing past girls. It is the \xe2\x80\x9ctime\xe2\x80\x9d that\r\nworries a youngster. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s jolly funny,\xe2\x80\x9d he says, as for the twentieth\r\ntime within five minutes he disentangles his sculls from yours; \xe2\x80\x9cI can\r\nget on all right when I\xe2\x80\x99m by myself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo see two novices try to keep time with one another is very amusing.\r\nBow finds it impossible to keep pace with stroke, because stroke rows\r\nin such an extraordinary fashion. Stroke is intensely indignant at\r\nthis, and explains that what he has been endeavouring to do for the\r\nlast ten minutes is to adapt his method to bow\xe2\x80\x99s limited capacity. Bow,\r\nin turn, then becomes insulted, and requests stroke not to trouble his\r\nhead about him (bow), but to devote his mind to setting a sensible\r\nstroke.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Two novices in a boat]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr, shall _I_ take stroke?\xe2\x80\x9d he adds, with the evident idea that that\r\nwould at once put the whole matter right.\r\n\r\nThey splash along for another hundred yards with still moderate\r\nsuccess, and then the whole secret of their trouble bursts upon stroke\r\nlike a flash of inspiration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tell you what it is: you\xe2\x80\x99ve got my sculls,\xe2\x80\x9d he cries, turning to\r\nbow; \xe2\x80\x9cpass yours over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, do you know, I\xe2\x80\x99ve been wondering how it was I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get on\r\nwith these,\xe2\x80\x9d answers bow, quite brightening up, and most willingly\r\nassisting in the exchange. \xe2\x80\x9c_Now_ we shall be all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut they are not\xe2\x80\x94not even then. Stroke has to stretch his arms nearly\r\nout of their sockets to reach his sculls now; while bow\xe2\x80\x99s pair, at each\r\nrecovery, hit him a violent blow in the chest. So they change back\r\nagain, and come to the conclusion that the man has given them the wrong\r\nset altogether; and over their mutual abuse of this man they become\r\nquite friendly and sympathetic.\r\n\r\nGeorge said he had often longed to take to punting for a change.\r\nPunting is not as easy as it looks. As in rowing, you soon learn how\r\nto get along and handle the craft, but it takes long practice before\r\nyou can do this with dignity and without getting the water all up your\r\nsleeve.\r\n\r\nOne young man I knew had a very sad accident happen to him the first\r\ntime he went punting. He had been getting on so well that he had grown\r\nquite cheeky over the business, and was walking up and down the punt,\r\nworking his pole with a careless grace that was quite fascinating to\r\nwatch. Up he would march to the head of the punt, plant his pole, and\r\nthen run along right to the other end, just like an old punter. Oh! it\r\nwas grand.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Man and pole] And it would all have gone on being grand if he\r\nhad not unfortunately, while looking round to enjoy the scenery, taken\r\njust one step more than there was any necessity for, and walked off\r\nthe punt altogether. The pole was firmly fixed in the mud, and he was\r\nleft clinging to it while the punt drifted away. It was an undignified\r\nposition for him. A rude boy on the bank immediately yelled out to a\r\nlagging chum to \xe2\x80\x9churry up and see a real monkey on a stick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI could not go to his assistance, because, as ill-luck would have it,\r\nwe had not taken the proper precaution to bring out a spare pole with\r\nus. I could only sit and look at him. His expression as the pole slowly\r\nsank with him I shall never forget; there was so much thought in it.\r\n\r\nI watched him gently let down into the water, and saw him scramble out,\r\nsad and wet. I could not help laughing, he looked such a ridiculous\r\nfigure. I continued to chuckle to myself about it for some time, and\r\nthen it was suddenly forced in upon me that really I had got very\r\nlittle to laugh at when I came to think of it. Here was I, alone in\r\na punt, without a pole, drifting helplessly down mid-stream\xe2\x80\x94possibly\r\ntowards a weir.\r\n\r\nI began to feel very indignant with my friend for having stepped\r\noverboard and gone off in that way. He might, at all events, have left\r\nme the pole.\r\n\r\nI drifted on for about a quarter of a mile, and then I came in sight of\r\na fishing-punt moored in mid-stream, in which sat two old fishermen.\r\nThey saw me bearing down upon them, and they called out to me to keep\r\nout of their way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d I shouted back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you don\xe2\x80\x99t try,\xe2\x80\x9d they answered.\r\n\r\nI explained the matter to them when I got nearer, and they caught me\r\nand lent me a pole. The weir was just fifty yards below. I am glad they\r\nhappened to be there.\r\n\r\nThe first time I went punting was in company with three other fellows;\r\nthey were going to show me how to do it. We could not all start\r\ntogether, so I said I would go down first and get out the punt, and\r\nthen I could potter about and practice a bit until they came.\r\n\r\nI could not get a punt out that afternoon, they were all engaged; so I\r\nhad nothing else to do but to sit down on the bank, watching the river,\r\nand waiting for my friends.\r\n\r\nI had not been sitting there long before my attention became attracted\r\nto a man in a punt who, I noticed with some surprise, wore a jacket\r\nand cap exactly like mine. He was evidently a novice at punting, and\r\nhis performance was most interesting. You never knew what was going\r\nto happen when he put the pole in; he evidently did not know himself.\r\nSometimes he shot up stream and sometimes he shot down stream, and at\r\nother times he simply spun round and came up the other side of the\r\npole. And with every result he seemed equally surprised and annoyed.\r\n\r\nThe people about the river began to get quite absorbed in him after\r\na while, and to make bets with one another as to what would be the\r\noutcome of his next push.\r\n\r\nIn the course of time my friends arrived on the opposite bank, and\r\nthey stopped and watched him too. His back was towards them, and they\r\nonly saw his jacket and cap. From this they immediately jumped to the\r\nconclusion that it was I, their beloved companion, who was making an\r\nexhibition of himself, and their delight knew no bounds. They commenced\r\nto chaff him unmercifully.\r\n\r\nI did not grasp their mistake at first, and I thought, \xe2\x80\x9cHow rude of\r\nthem to go on like that, with a perfect stranger, too!\xe2\x80\x9d But before I\r\ncould call out and reprove them, the explanation of the matter occurred\r\nto me, and I withdrew behind a tree.\r\n\r\nOh, how they enjoyed themselves, ridiculing that young man! For five\r\ngood minutes they stood there, shouting ribaldry at him, deriding him,\r\nmocking him, jeering at him. They peppered him with stale jokes, they\r\neven made a few new ones and threw at him. They hurled at him all the\r\nprivate family jokes belonging to our set, and which must have been\r\nperfectly unintelligible to him. And then, unable to stand their brutal\r\njibes any longer, he turned round on them, and they saw his face!\r\n\r\nI was glad to notice that they had sufficient decency left in them to\r\nlook very foolish. They explained to him that they had thought he was\r\nsome one they knew. They said they hoped he would not deem them capable\r\nof so insulting any one except a personal friend of their own.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Bathing] Of course their having mistaken him for a friend\r\nexcused it. I remember Harris telling me once of a bathing experience\r\nhe had at Boulogne. He was swimming about there near the beach, when\r\nhe felt himself suddenly seized by the neck from behind, and forcibly\r\nplunged under water. He struggled violently, but whoever had got hold\r\nof him seemed to be a perfect Hercules in strength, and all his efforts\r\nto escape were unavailing. He had given up kicking, and was trying to\r\nturn his thoughts upon solemn things, when his captor released him.\r\n\r\nHe regained his feet, and looked round for his would-be murderer. The\r\nassassin was standing close by him, laughing heartily, but the moment\r\nhe caught sight of Harris\xe2\x80\x99s face, as it emerged from the water, he\r\nstarted back and seemed quite concerned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI really beg your pardon,\xe2\x80\x9d he stammered confusedly, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I took you\r\nfor a friend of mine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHarris thought it was lucky for him the man had not mistaken him for a\r\nrelation, or he would probably have been drowned outright.\r\n\r\nSailing is a thing that wants knowledge and practice too\xe2\x80\x94though, as a\r\nboy, I did not think so. I had an idea it came natural to a body, like\r\nrounders and touch. I knew another boy who held this view likewise, and\r\nso, one windy day, we thought we would try the sport. We were stopping\r\ndown at Yarmouth, and we decided we would go for a trip up the Yare. We\r\nhired a sailing boat at the yard by the bridge, and started off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s rather a rough day,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man to us, as we put off: \xe2\x80\x9cbetter\r\ntake in a reef and luff sharp when you get round the bend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe said we would make a point of it, and left him with a cheery\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-morning,\xe2\x80\x9d wondering to ourselves how you \xe2\x80\x9cluffed,\xe2\x80\x9d and where we\r\nwere to get a \xe2\x80\x9creef\xe2\x80\x9d from, and what we were to do with it when we had\r\ngot it.\r\n\r\nWe rowed until we were out of sight of the town, and then, with a\r\nwide stretch of water in front of us, and the wind blowing a perfect\r\nhurricane across it, we felt that the time had come to commence\r\noperations.\r\n\r\nHector\xe2\x80\x94I think that was his name\xe2\x80\x94went on pulling while I unrolled the\r\nsail. It seemed a complicated job, but I accomplished it at length, and\r\nthen came the question, which was the top end?\r\n\r\nBy a sort of natural instinct, we, of course, eventually decided that\r\nthe bottom was the top, and set to work to fix it upside-down. But\r\nit was a long time before we could get it up, either that way or any\r\nother way. The impression on the mind of the sail seemed to be that we\r\nwere playing at funerals, and that I was the corpse and itself was the\r\nwinding-sheet.\r\n\r\nWhen it found that this was not the idea, it hit me over the head with\r\nthe boom, and refused to do anything.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWet it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hector; \xe2\x80\x9cdrop it over and get it wet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe said people in ships always wetted the sails before they put them\r\nup. So I wetted it; but that only made matters worse than they were\r\nbefore. A dry sail clinging to your legs and wrapping itself round your\r\nhead is not pleasant, but, when the sail is sopping wet, it becomes\r\nquite vexing.\r\n\r\nWe did get the thing up at last, the two of us together. We fixed it,\r\nnot exactly upside down\xe2\x80\x94more sideways like\xe2\x80\x94and we tied it up to the\r\nmast with the painter, which we cut off for the purpose.\r\n\r\nThat the boat did not upset I simply state as a fact. Why it did not\r\nupset I am unable to offer any reason. I have often thought about\r\nthe matter since, but I have never succeeded in arriving at any\r\nsatisfactory explanation of the phenomenon.\r\n\r\nPossibly the result may have been brought about by the natural\r\nobstinacy of all things in this world. The boat may possibly have come\r\nto the conclusion, judging from a cursory view of our behaviour, that\r\nwe had come out for a morning\xe2\x80\x99s suicide, and had thereupon determined\r\nto disappoint us. That is the only suggestion I can offer.\r\n\r\nBy clinging like grim death to the gunwale, we just managed to keep\r\ninside the boat, but it was exhausting work. Hector said that pirates\r\nand other seafaring people generally lashed the rudder to something\r\nor other, and hauled in the main top-jib, during severe squalls, and\r\nthought we ought to try to do something of the kind; but I was for\r\nletting her have her head to the wind.\r\n\r\nAs my advice was by far the easiest to follow, we ended by adopting it,\r\nand contrived to embrace the gunwale and give her her head.\r\n\r\nThe boat travelled up stream for about a mile at a pace I have never\r\nsailed at since, and don\xe2\x80\x99t want to again. Then, at a bend, she heeled\r\nover till half her sail was under water. Then she righted herself by a\r\nmiracle and flew for a long low bank of soft mud.\r\n\r\nThat mud-bank saved us. The boat ploughed its way into the middle of it\r\nand then stuck. Finding that we were once more able to move according\r\nto our ideas, instead of being pitched and thrown about like peas in a\r\nbladder, we crept forward, and cut down the sail.\r\n\r\nWe had had enough sailing. We did not want to overdo the thing and\r\nget a surfeit of it. We had had a sail\xe2\x80\x94a good all-round exciting,\r\ninteresting sail\xe2\x80\x94and now we thought we would have a row, just for a\r\nchange like.\r\n\r\nWe took the sculls and tried to push the boat off the mud, and, in\r\ndoing so, we broke one of the sculls. After that we proceeded with\r\ngreat caution, but they were a wretched old pair, and the second one\r\ncracked almost easier than the first, and left us helpless.\r\n\r\nThe mud stretched out for about a hundred yards in front of us, and\r\nbehind us was the water. The only thing to be done was to sit and wait\r\nuntil someone came by.\r\n\r\nIt was not the sort of day to attract people out on the river, and it\r\nwas three hours before a soul came in sight. It was an old fisherman\r\nwho, with immense difficulty, at last rescued us, and we were towed\r\nback in an ignominious fashion to the boat-yard.\r\n\r\nWhat between tipping the man who had brought us home, and paying for\r\nthe broken sculls, and for having been out four hours and a half, it\r\ncost us a pretty considerable number of weeks\xe2\x80\x99 pocket-money, that sail.\r\nBut we learned experience, and they say that is always cheap at any\r\nprice.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI.\r\n\r\n\r\nReading.\xe2\x80\x94We are towed by steam launch.\xe2\x80\x94Irritating behaviour of small\r\nboats.\xe2\x80\x94How they get in the way of steam launches.\xe2\x80\x94George and Harris\r\nagain shirk their work.\xe2\x80\x94Rather a hackneyed story.\xe2\x80\x94Streatley and Goring.\r\n\r\nWe came in sight of Reading about eleven. The river is dirty and\r\ndismal here. One does not linger in the neighbourhood of Reading.\r\nThe town itself is a famous old place, dating from the dim days of\r\nKing Ethelred, when the Danes anchored their warships in the Kennet,\r\nand started from Reading to ravage all the land of Wessex; and here\r\nEthelred and his brother Alfred fought and defeated them, Ethelred\r\ndoing the praying and Alfred the fighting.\r\n\r\nIn later years, Reading seems to have been regarded as a handy place\r\nto run down to, when matters were becoming unpleasant in London.\r\nParliament generally rushed off to Reading whenever there was a plague\r\non at Westminster; and, in 1625, the Law followed suit, and all the\r\ncourts were held at Reading. It must have been worth while having a\r\nmere ordinary plague now and then in London to get rid of both the\r\nlawyers and the Parliament.\r\n\r\nDuring the Parliamentary struggle, Reading was besieged by the Earl of\r\nEssex, and, a quarter of a century later, the Prince of Orange routed\r\nKing James\xe2\x80\x99s troops there.\r\n\r\nHenry I. lies buried at Reading, in the Benedictine abbey founded by\r\nhim there, the ruins of which may still be seen; and, in this same\r\nabbey, great John of Gaunt was married to the Lady Blanche.\r\n\r\nAt Reading lock we came up with a steam launch, belonging to some\r\nfriends of mine, and they towed us up to within about a mile of\r\nStreatley. It is very delightful being towed up by a launch. I prefer\r\nit myself to rowing. The run would have been more delightful still, if\r\nit had not been for a lot of wretched small boats that were continually\r\ngetting in the way of our launch, and, to avoid running down which, we\r\nhad to be continually easing and stopping. It is really most annoying,\r\nthe manner in which these rowing boats get in the way of one\xe2\x80\x99s launch\r\nup the river; something ought to done to stop it.\r\n\r\nAnd they are so confoundedly impertinent, too, over it. You can whistle\r\ntill you nearly burst your boiler before they will trouble themselves\r\nto hurry. I would have one or two of them run down now and then, if I\r\nhad my way, just to teach them all a lesson.\r\n\r\nThe river becomes very lovely from a little above Reading. The railway\r\nrather spoils it near Tilehurst, but from Mapledurham up to Streatley\r\nit is glorious. A little above Mapledurham lock you pass Hardwick\r\nHouse, where Charles I. played bowls. The neighbourhood of Pangbourne,\r\nwhere the quaint little Swan Inn stands, must be as familiar to the\r\n_habitues_ of the Art Exhibitions as it is to its own inhabitants.\r\n\r\nMy friends\xe2\x80\x99 launch cast us loose just below the grotto, and then Harris\r\nwanted to make out that it was my turn to pull. This seemed to me most\r\nunreasonable. It had been arranged in the morning that I should bring\r\nthe boat up to three miles above Reading. Well, here we were, ten miles\r\nabove Reading! Surely it was now their turn again.\r\n\r\nI could not get either George or Harris to see the matter in its proper\r\nlight, however; so, to save argument, I took the sculls. I had not been\r\npulling for more than a minute or so, when George noticed something\r\nblack floating on the water, and we drew up to it. George leant over,\r\nas we neared it, and laid hold of it. And then he drew back with a cry,\r\nand a blanched face.\r\n\r\nIt was the dead body of a woman. It lay very lightly on the water, and\r\nthe face was sweet and calm. It was not a beautiful face; it was too\r\nprematurely aged-looking, too thin and drawn, to be that; but it was a\r\ngentle, lovable face, in spite of its stamp of pinch and poverty, and\r\nupon it was that look of restful peace that comes to the faces of the\r\nsick sometimes when at last the pain has left them.\r\n\r\nFortunately for us\xe2\x80\x94we having no desire to be kept hanging about\r\ncoroners\xe2\x80\x99 courts\xe2\x80\x94some men on the bank had seen the body too, and now\r\ntook charge of it from us.\r\n\r\nWe found out the woman\xe2\x80\x99s story afterwards. Of course it was the old,\r\nold vulgar tragedy. She had loved and been deceived\xe2\x80\x94or had deceived\r\nherself. Anyhow, she had sinned\xe2\x80\x94some of us do now and then\xe2\x80\x94and her\r\nfamily and friends, naturally shocked and indignant, had closed their\r\ndoors against her.\r\n\r\nLeft to fight the world alone, with the millstone of her shame around\r\nher neck, she had sunk ever lower and lower. For a while she had kept\r\nboth herself and the child on the twelve shillings a week that twelve\r\nhours\xe2\x80\x99 drudgery a day procured her, paying six shillings out of it for\r\nthe child, and keeping her own body and soul together on the remainder.\r\n\r\nSix shillings a week does not keep body and soul together very\r\nunitedly. They want to get away from each other when there is only\r\nsuch a very slight bond as that between them; and one day, I suppose,\r\nthe pain and the dull monotony of it all had stood before her eyes\r\nplainer than usual, and the mocking spectre had frightened her. She had\r\nmade one last appeal to friends, but, against the chill wall of their\r\nrespectability, the voice of the erring outcast fell unheeded; and then\r\nshe had gone to see her child\xe2\x80\x94had held it in her arms and kissed it, in\r\na weary, dull sort of way, and without betraying any particular emotion\r\nof any kind, and had left it, after putting into its hand a penny box\r\nof chocolate she had bought it, and afterwards, with her last few\r\nshillings, had taken a ticket and come down to Goring.\r\n\r\n                      [Picture: Woman in the water]\r\n\r\nIt seemed that the bitterest thoughts of her life must have centred\r\nabout the wooded reaches and the bright green meadows around Goring;\r\nbut women strangely hug the knife that stabs them, and, perhaps, amidst\r\nthe gall, there may have mingled also sunny memories of sweetest hours,\r\nspent upon those shadowed deeps over which the great trees bend their\r\nbranches down so low.\r\n\r\nShe had wandered about the woods by the river\xe2\x80\x99s brink all day, and\r\nthen, when evening fell and the grey twilight spread its dusky robe\r\nupon the waters, she stretched her arms out to the silent river that\r\nhad known her sorrow and her joy. And the old river had taken her into\r\nits gentle arms, and had laid her weary head upon its bosom, and had\r\nhushed away the pain.\r\n\r\nThus had she sinned in all things\xe2\x80\x94sinned in living and in dying. God\r\nhelp her! and all other sinners, if any more there be.\r\n\r\nGoring on the left bank and Streatley on the right are both or\r\neither charming places to stay at for a few days. The reaches down\r\nto Pangbourne woo one for a sunny sail or for a moonlight row, and\r\nthe country round about is full of beauty. We had intended to push\r\non to Wallingford that day, but the sweet smiling face of the river\r\nhere lured us to linger for a while; and so we left our boat at the\r\nbridge, and went up into Streatley, and lunched at the \xe2\x80\x9cBull,\xe2\x80\x9d much to\r\nMontmorency\xe2\x80\x99s satisfaction.\r\n\r\nThey say that the hills on each ride of the stream here once joined\r\nand formed a barrier across what is now the Thames, and that then the\r\nriver ended there above Goring in one vast lake. I am not in a position\r\neither to contradict or affirm this statement. I simply offer it.\r\n\r\nIt is an ancient place, Streatley, dating back, like most river-side\r\ntowns and villages, to British and Saxon times. Goring is not nearly so\r\npretty a little spot to stop at as Streatley, if you have your choice;\r\nbut it is passing fair enough in its way, and is nearer the railway in\r\ncase you want to slip off without paying your hotel bill.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVII.\r\n\r\n\r\nWashing day.\xe2\x80\x94Fish and fishers.\xe2\x80\x94On the art of angling.\xe2\x80\x94A conscientious\r\nfly-fisher.\xe2\x80\x94A fishy story.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Washing line] We stayed two days at Streatley, and got our\r\nclothes washed. We had tried washing them ourselves, in the river,\r\nunder George\xe2\x80\x99s superintendence, and it had been a failure. Indeed, it\r\nhad been more than a failure, because we were worse off after we had\r\nwashed our clothes than we were before. Before we had washed them, they\r\nhad been very, very dirty, it is true; but they were just wearable.\r\n_After_ we had washed them\xe2\x80\x94well, the river between Reading and Henley\r\nwas much cleaner, after we had washed our clothes in it, than it was\r\nbefore. All the dirt contained in the river between Reading and Henley,\r\nwe collected, during that wash, and worked it into our clothes.\r\n\r\nThe washerwoman at Streatley said she felt she owed it to herself to\r\ncharge us just three times the usual prices for that wash. She said it\r\nhad not been like washing, it had been more in the nature of excavating.\r\n\r\nWe paid the bill without a murmur.\r\n\r\nThe neighbourhood of Streatley and Goring is a great fishing centre.\r\nThere is some excellent fishing to be had here. The river abounds in\r\npike, roach, dace, gudgeon, and eels, just here; and you can sit and\r\nfish for them all day.\r\n\r\nSome people do. They never catch them. I never knew anybody catch\r\nanything, up the Thames, except minnows and dead cats, but that has\r\nnothing to do, of course, with fishing! The local fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s guide\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t say a word about catching anything. All it says is the place\r\nis \xe2\x80\x9ca good station for fishing;\xe2\x80\x9d and, from what I have seen of the\r\ndistrict, I am quite prepared to bear out this statement.\r\n\r\nThere is no spot in the world where you can get more fishing, or where\r\nyou can fish for a longer period. Some fishermen come here and fish for\r\na day, and others stop and fish for a month. You can hang on and fish\r\nfor a year, if you want to: it will be all the same.\r\n\r\nThe _Angler\xe2\x80\x99s Guide to the Thames_ says that \xe2\x80\x9cjack and perch are also\r\nto be had about here,\xe2\x80\x9d but there the _Angler\xe2\x80\x99s Guide_ is wrong. Jack\r\nand perch may _be_ about there. Indeed, I know for a fact that they\r\nare. You can _see_ them there in shoals, when you are out for a walk\r\nalong the banks: they come and stand half out of the water with their\r\nmouths open for biscuits. And, if you go for a bathe, they crowd round,\r\nand get in your way, and irritate you. But they are not to be \xe2\x80\x9chad\xe2\x80\x9d by\r\na bit of worm on the end of a hook, nor anything like it\xe2\x80\x94not they!\r\n\r\nI am not a good fisherman myself. I devoted a considerable amount of\r\nattention to the subject at one time, and was getting on, as I thought,\r\nfairly well; but the old hands told me that I should never be any real\r\ngood at it, and advised me to give it up. They said that I was an\r\nextremely neat thrower, and that I seemed to have plenty of gumption\r\nfor the thing, and quite enough constitutional laziness. But they\r\nwere sure I should never make anything of a fisherman. I had not got\r\nsufficient imagination.\r\n\r\nThey said that as a poet, or a shilling shocker, or a reporter, or\r\nanything of that kind, I might be satisfactory, but that, to gain any\r\nposition as a Thames angler, would require more play of fancy, more\r\npower of invention than I appeared to possess.\r\n\r\nSome people are under the impression that all that is required to\r\nmake a good fisherman is the ability to tell lies easily and without\r\nblushing; but this is a mistake. Mere bald fabrication is useless;\r\nthe veriest tyro can manage that. It is in the circumstantial\r\ndetail, the embellishing touches of probability, the general air of\r\nscrupulous\xe2\x80\x94almost of pedantic\xe2\x80\x94veracity, that the experienced angler is\r\nseen.\r\n\r\nAnybody can come in and say, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I caught fifteen dozen perch\r\nyesterday evening;\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cLast Monday I landed a gudgeon, weighing\r\neighteen pounds, and measuring three feet from the tip to the tail.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere is no art, no skill, required for that sort of thing. It shows\r\npluck, but that is all.\r\n\r\nNo; your accomplished angler would scorn to tell a lie, that way. His\r\nmethod is a study in itself.\r\n\r\nHe comes in quietly with his hat on, appropriates the most comfortable\r\nchair, lights his pipe, and commences to puff in silence. He lets the\r\nyoungsters brag away for a while, and then, during a momentary lull, he\r\nremoves the pipe from his mouth, and remarks, as he knocks the ashes\r\nout against the bars:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I had a haul on Tuesday evening that it\xe2\x80\x99s not much good my\r\ntelling anybody about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! why\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d they ask.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I don\xe2\x80\x99t expect anybody would believe me if I did,\xe2\x80\x9d replies the\r\nold fellow calmly, and without even a tinge of bitterness in his tone,\r\nas he refills his pipe, and requests the landlord to bring him three of\r\nScotch, cold.\r\n\r\nThere is a pause after this, nobody feeling sufficiently sure of\r\nhimself to contradict the old gentleman. So he has to go on by himself\r\nwithout any encouragement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he continues thoughtfully; \xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t believe it myself if\r\nanybody told it to me, but it\xe2\x80\x99s a fact, for all that. I had been\r\nsitting there all the afternoon and had caught literally nothing\xe2\x80\x94except\r\na few dozen dace and a score of jack; and I was just about giving it up\r\nas a bad job when I suddenly felt a rather smart pull at the line. I\r\nthought it was another little one, and I went to jerk it up. Hang me,\r\nif I could move the rod! It took me half-an-hour\xe2\x80\x94half-an-hour, sir!\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nland that fish; and every moment I thought the line was going to snap!\r\nI reached him at last, and what do you think it was? A sturgeon! a\r\nforty pound sturgeon! taken on a line, sir! Yes, you may well look\r\nsurprised\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll have another three of Scotch, landlord, please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then he goes on to tell of the astonishment of everybody who saw\r\nit; and what his wife said, when he got home, and of what Joe Buggles\r\nthought about it.\r\n\r\nI asked the landlord of an inn up the river once, if it did not injure\r\nhim, sometimes, listening to the tales that the fishermen about there\r\ntold him; and he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no; not now, sir. It did used to knock me over a bit at first,\r\nbut, lor love you! me and the missus we listens to \xe2\x80\x99em all day now.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s what you\xe2\x80\x99re used to, you know. It\xe2\x80\x99s what you\xe2\x80\x99re used to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI knew a young man once, he was a most conscientious fellow, and, when\r\nhe took to fly-fishing, he determined never to exaggerate his hauls by\r\nmore than twenty-five per cent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I have caught forty fish,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cthen I will tell people that\r\nI have caught fifty, and so on. But I will not lie any more than that,\r\nbecause it is sinful to lie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the twenty-five per cent. plan did not work well at all. He never\r\nwas able to use it. The greatest number of fish he ever caught in one\r\nday was three, and you can\xe2\x80\x99t add twenty-five per cent. to three\xe2\x80\x94at\r\nleast, not in fish.\r\n\r\nSo he increased his percentage to thirty-three-and-a-third; but that,\r\nagain, was awkward, when he had only caught one or two; so, to simplify\r\nmatters, he made up his mind to just double the quantity.\r\n\r\nHe stuck to this arrangement for a couple of months, and then he grew\r\ndissatisfied with it. Nobody believed him when he told them that he\r\nonly doubled, and he, therefore, gained no credit that way whatever,\r\nwhile his moderation put him at a disadvantage among the other anglers.\r\nWhen he had really caught three small fish, and said he had caught six,\r\nit used to make him quite jealous to hear a man, whom he knew for a\r\nfact had only caught one, going about telling people he had landed two\r\ndozen.\r\n\r\nSo, eventually, he made one final arrangement with himself, which he\r\nhas religiously held to ever since, and that was to count each fish\r\nthat he caught as ten, and to assume ten to begin with. For example,\r\nif he did not catch any fish at all, then he said he had caught ten\r\nfish\xe2\x80\x94you could never catch less than ten fish by his system; that was\r\nthe foundation of it. Then, if by any chance he really did catch one\r\nfish, he called it twenty, while two fish would count thirty, three\r\nforty, and so on.\r\n\r\nIt is a simple and easily worked plan, and there has been some talk\r\nlately of its being made use of by the angling fraternity in general.\r\nIndeed, the Committee of the Thames Angler\xe2\x80\x99s Association did recommend\r\nits adoption about two years ago, but some of the older members opposed\r\nit. They said they would consider the idea if the number were doubled,\r\nand each fish counted as twenty.\r\n\r\nIf ever you have an evening to spare, up the river, I should advise\r\nyou to drop into one of the little village inns, and take a seat in\r\nthe tap-room. You will be nearly sure to meet one or two old rod-men,\r\nsipping their toddy there, and they will tell you enough fishy stories,\r\nin half an hour, to give you indigestion for a month.\r\n\r\nGeorge and I\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what had become of Harris; he had gone out\r\nand had a shave, early in the afternoon, and had then come back and\r\nspent full forty minutes in pipeclaying his shoes, we had not seen him\r\nsince\xe2\x80\x94George and I, therefore, and the dog, left to ourselves, went\r\nfor a walk to Wallingford on the second evening, and, coming home, we\r\ncalled in at a little river-side inn, for a rest, and other things.\r\n\r\nWe went into the parlour and sat down. There was an old fellow there,\r\nsmoking a long clay pipe, and we naturally began chatting.\r\n\r\nHe told us that it had been a fine day to-day, and we told him that it\r\nhad been a fine day yesterday, and then we all told each other that we\r\nthought it would be a fine day to-morrow; and George said the crops\r\nseemed to be coming up nicely.\r\n\r\nAfter that it came out, somehow or other, that we were strangers in the\r\nneighbourhood, and that we were going away the next morning.\r\n\r\n[Picture: The trout] Then a pause ensued in the conversation, during\r\nwhich our eyes wandered round the room. They finally rested upon a\r\ndusty old glass-case, fixed very high up above the chimney-piece, and\r\ncontaining a trout. It rather fascinated me, that trout; it was such a\r\nmonstrous fish. In fact, at first glance, I thought it was a cod.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said the old gentleman, following the direction of my gaze, \xe2\x80\x9cfine\r\nfellow that, ain\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite uncommon,\xe2\x80\x9d I murmured; and George asked the old man how much he\r\nthought it weighed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEighteen pounds six ounces,\xe2\x80\x9d said our friend, rising and taking down\r\nhis coat. \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, \xe2\x80\x9cit wur sixteen year ago, come the third\r\no\xe2\x80\x99 next month, that I landed him. I caught him just below the bridge\r\nwith a minnow. They told me he wur in the river, and I said I\xe2\x80\x99d have\r\nhim, and so I did. You don\xe2\x80\x99t see many fish that size about here now,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m thinking. Good-night, gentlemen, good-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd out he went, and left us alone.\r\n\r\nWe could not take our eyes off the fish after that. It really was a\r\nremarkably fine fish. We were still looking at it, when the local\r\ncarrier, who had just stopped at the inn, came to the door of the room\r\nwith a pot of beer in his hand, and he also looked at the fish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-sized trout, that,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, turning round to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! you may well say that, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the man; and then, after a\r\npull at his beer, he added, \xe2\x80\x9cMaybe you wasn\xe2\x80\x99t here, sir, when that fish\r\nwas caught?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d we told him. We were strangers in the neighbourhood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said the carrier, \xe2\x80\x9cthen, of course, how should you? It was nearly\r\nfive years ago that I caught that trout.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! was it you who caught it, then?\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the genial old fellow. \xe2\x80\x9cI caught him just below the\r\nlock\xe2\x80\x94leastways, what was the lock then\xe2\x80\x94one Friday afternoon; and the\r\nremarkable thing about it is that I caught him with a fly. I\xe2\x80\x99d gone out\r\npike fishing, bless you, never thinking of a trout, and when I saw that\r\nwhopper on the end of my line, blest if it didn\xe2\x80\x99t quite take me aback.\r\nWell, you see, he weighed twenty-six pound. Good-night, gentlemen,\r\ngood-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFive minutes afterwards, a third man came in, and described how _he_\r\nhad caught it early one morning, with bleak; and then he left, and a\r\nstolid, solemn-looking, middle-aged individual came in, and sat down\r\nover by the window.\r\n\r\nNone of us spoke for a while; but, at length, George turned to the new\r\ncomer, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon, I hope you will forgive the liberty that we\xe2\x80\x94perfect\r\nstrangers in the neighbourhood\xe2\x80\x94are taking, but my friend here and\r\nmyself would be so much obliged if you would tell us how you caught\r\nthat trout up there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, who told you I caught that trout!\xe2\x80\x9d was the surprised query.\r\n\r\nWe said that nobody had told us so, but somehow or other we felt\r\ninstinctively that it was he who had done it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s a most remarkable thing\xe2\x80\x94most remarkable,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the\r\nstolid stranger, laughing; \xe2\x80\x9cbecause, as a matter of fact, you are quite\r\nright. I did catch it. But fancy your guessing it like that. Dear me,\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s really a most remarkable thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then he went on, and told us how it had taken him half an hour\r\nto land it, and how it had broken his rod. He said he had weighed\r\nit carefully when he reached home, and it had turned the scale at\r\nthirty-four pounds.\r\n\r\nHe went in his turn, and when he was gone, the landlord came in to us.\r\nWe told him the various histories we had heard about his trout, and he\r\nwas immensely amused, and we all laughed very heartily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFancy Jim Bates and Joe Muggles and Mr. Jones and old Billy Maunders\r\nall telling you that they had caught it. Ha! ha! ha! Well, that is\r\ngood,\xe2\x80\x9d said the honest old fellow, laughing heartily. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, they are\r\nthe sort to give it _me_, to put up in _my_ parlour, if _they_ had\r\ncaught it, they are! Ha! ha! ha!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then he told us the real history of the fish. It seemed that he had\r\ncaught it himself, years ago, when he was quite a lad; not by any art\r\nor skill, but by that unaccountable luck that appears to always wait\r\nupon a boy when he plays the wag from school, and goes out fishing on a\r\nsunny afternoon, with a bit of string tied on to the end of a tree.\r\n\r\nHe said that bringing home that trout had saved him from a whacking,\r\nand that even his school-master had said it was worth the rule-of-three\r\nand practice put together.\r\n\r\nHe was called out of the room at this point, and George and I again\r\nturned our gaze upon the fish.\r\n\r\nIt really was a most astonishing trout. The more we looked at it, the\r\nmore we marvelled at it.\r\n\r\nIt excited George so much that he climbed up on the back of a chair to\r\nget a better view of it.\r\n\r\nAnd then the chair slipped, and George clutched wildly at the\r\ntrout-case to save himself, and down it came with a crash, George and\r\nthe chair on top of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou haven\xe2\x80\x99t injured the fish, have you?\xe2\x80\x9d I cried in alarm, rushing up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope not,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, rising cautiously and looking about.\r\n\r\nBut he had. That trout lay shattered into a thousand fragments\xe2\x80\x94I say\r\na thousand, but they may have only been nine hundred. I did not count\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nWe thought it strange and unaccountable that a stuffed trout should\r\nbreak up into little pieces like that.\r\n\r\nAnd so it would have been strange and unaccountable, if it had been a\r\nstuffed trout, but it was not.\r\n\r\nThat trout was plaster-of-Paris.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVIII.\r\n\r\n\r\nLocks.\xe2\x80\x94George and I are\r\nphotographed.\xe2\x80\x94Wallingford.\xe2\x80\x94Dorchester.\xe2\x80\x94Abingdon.\xe2\x80\x94A family man.\xe2\x80\x94A good\r\nspot for drowning.\xe2\x80\x94A difficult bit of water.\xe2\x80\x94Demoralizing effect of\r\nriver air.\r\n\r\nWe left Streatley early the next morning, and pulled up to Culham, and\r\nslept under the canvas, in the backwater there.\r\n\r\nThe river is not extraordinarily interesting between Streatley and\r\nWallingford. From Cleve you get a stretch of six and a half miles\r\nwithout a lock. I believe this is the longest uninterrupted stretch\r\nanywhere above Teddington, and the Oxford Club make use of it for their\r\ntrial eights.\r\n\r\nBut however satisfactory this absence of locks may be to rowing-men, it\r\nis to be regretted by the mere pleasure-seeker.\r\n\r\nFor myself, I am fond of locks. They pleasantly break the monotony of\r\nthe pull. I like sitting in the boat and slowly rising out of the cool\r\ndepths up into new reaches and fresh views; or sinking down, as it\r\nwere, out of the world, and then waiting, while the gloomy gates creak,\r\nand the narrow strip of day-light between them widens till the fair\r\nsmiling river lies full before you, and you push your little boat out\r\nfrom its brief prison on to the welcoming waters once again.\r\n\r\nThey are picturesque little spots, these locks. The stout old\r\nlock-keeper, or his cheerful-looking wife, or bright-eyed daughter,\r\nare pleasant folk to have a passing chat with. {287} You meet other\r\nboats there, and river gossip is exchanged. The Thames would not be the\r\nfairyland it is without its flower-decked locks.\r\n\r\nTalking of locks reminds me of an accident George and I very nearly had\r\none summer\xe2\x80\x99s morning at Hampton Court.\r\n\r\nIt was a glorious day, and the lock was crowded; and, as is a common\r\npractice up the river, a speculative photographer was taking a picture\r\nof us all as we lay upon the rising waters.\r\n\r\nI did not catch what was going on at first, and was, therefore,\r\nextremely surprised at noticing George hurriedly smooth out his\r\ntrousers, ruffle up his hair, and stick his cap on in a rakish manner\r\nat the back of his head, and then, assuming an expression of mingled\r\naffability and sadness, sit down in a graceful attitude, and try to\r\nhide his feet.\r\n\r\nMy first idea was that he had suddenly caught sight of some girl he\r\nknew, and I looked about to see who it was. Everybody in the lock\r\nseemed to have been suddenly struck wooden. They were all standing or\r\nsitting about in the most quaint and curious attitudes I have ever seen\r\noff a Japanese fan. All the girls were smiling. Oh, they did look so\r\nsweet! And all the fellows were frowning, and looking stern and noble.\r\n\r\nAnd then, at last, the truth flashed across me, and I wondered if I\r\nshould be in time. Ours was the first boat, and it would be unkind of\r\nme to spoil the man\xe2\x80\x99s picture, I thought.\r\n\r\nSo I faced round quickly, and took up a position in the prow, where I\r\nleant with careless grace upon the hitcher, in an attitude suggestive\r\nof agility and strength. I arranged my hair with a curl over the\r\nforehead, and threw an air of tender wistfulness into my expression,\r\nmingled with a touch of cynicism, which I am told suits me.\r\n\r\nAs we stood, waiting for the eventful moment, I heard someone behind\r\ncall out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHi! look at your nose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI could not turn round to see what was the matter, and whose nose it\r\nwas that was to be looked at. I stole a side-glance at George\xe2\x80\x99s nose!\r\nIt was all right\xe2\x80\x94at all events, there was nothing wrong with it that\r\ncould be altered. I squinted down at my own, and that seemed all that\r\ncould be expected also.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at your nose, you stupid ass!\xe2\x80\x9d came the same voice again, louder.\r\n\r\nAnd then another voice cried:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPush your nose out, can\xe2\x80\x99t you, you\xe2\x80\x94you two with the dog!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeither George nor I dared to turn round. The man\xe2\x80\x99s hand was on the\r\ncap, and the picture might be taken any moment. Was it us they were\r\ncalling to? What was the matter with our noses? Why were they to be\r\npushed out!\r\n\r\nBut now the whole lock started yelling, and a stentorian voice from the\r\nback shouted:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at your boat, sir; you in the red and black caps. It\xe2\x80\x99s your two\r\ncorpses that will get taken in that photo, if you ain\xe2\x80\x99t quick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe looked then, and saw that the nose of our boat had got fixed under\r\nthe woodwork of the lock, while the in-coming water was rising all\r\naround it, and tilting it up. In another moment we should be over.\r\nQuick as thought, we each seized an oar, and a vigorous blow against\r\nthe side of the lock with the butt-ends released the boat, and sent us\r\nsprawling on our backs.\r\n\r\n[Picture: The photograph] We did not come out well in that photograph,\r\nGeorge and I. Of course, as was to be expected, our luck ordained it,\r\nthat the man should set his wretched machine in motion at the precise\r\nmoment that we were both lying on our backs with a wild expression of\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere am I? and what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d on our faces, and our four feet waving\r\nmadly in the air.\r\n\r\nOur feet were undoubtedly the leading article in that photograph.\r\nIndeed, very little else was to be seen. They filled up the foreground\r\nentirely. Behind them, you caught glimpses of the other boats, and bits\r\nof the surrounding scenery; but everything and everybody else in the\r\nlock looked so utterly insignificant and paltry compared with our feet,\r\nthat all the other people felt quite ashamed of themselves, and refused\r\nto subscribe to the picture.\r\n\r\nThe owner of one steam launch, who had bespoke six copies, rescinded\r\nthe order on seeing the negative. He said he would take them if anybody\r\ncould show him his launch, but nobody could. It was somewhere behind\r\nGeorge\xe2\x80\x99s right foot.\r\n\r\nThere was a good deal of unpleasantness over the business. The\r\nphotographer thought we ought to take a dozen copies each, seeing that\r\nthe photo was about nine-tenths us, but we declined. We said we had no\r\nobjection to being photo\xe2\x80\x99d full-length, but we preferred being taken\r\nthe right way up.\r\n\r\nWallingford, six miles above Streatley, is a very ancient town, and has\r\nbeen an active centre for the making of English history. It was a rude,\r\nmud-built town in the time of the Britons, who squatted there, until\r\nthe Roman legions evicted them; and replaced their clay-baked walls by\r\nmighty fortifications, the trace of which Time has not yet succeeded in\r\nsweeping away, so well those old-world masons knew how to build.\r\n\r\nBut Time, though he halted at Roman walls, soon crumbled Romans to\r\ndust; and on the ground, in later years, fought savage Saxons and huge\r\nDanes, until the Normans came.\r\n\r\nIt was a walled and fortified town up to the time of the Parliamentary\r\nWar, when it suffered a long and bitter siege from Fairfax. It fell at\r\nlast, and then the walls were razed.\r\n\r\nFrom Wallingford up to Dorchester the neighbourhood of the river grows\r\nmore hilly, varied, and picturesque. Dorchester stands half a mile from\r\nthe river. It can be reached by paddling up the Thame, if you have a\r\nsmall boat; but the best way is to leave the river at Day\xe2\x80\x99s Lock, and\r\ntake a walk across the fields. Dorchester is a delightfully peaceful\r\nold place, nestling in stillness and silence and drowsiness.\r\n\r\nDorchester, like Wallingford, was a city in ancient British times;\r\nit was then called Caer Doren, \xe2\x80\x9cthe city on the water.\xe2\x80\x9d In more\r\nrecent times the Romans formed a great camp here, the fortifications\r\nsurrounding which now seem like low, even hills. In Saxon days it was\r\nthe capital of Wessex. It is very old, and it was very strong and great\r\nonce. Now it sits aside from the stirring world, and nods and dreams.\r\n\r\nRound Clifton Hampden, itself a wonderfully pretty village,\r\nold-fashioned, peaceful, and dainty with flowers, the river scenery\r\nis rich and beautiful. If you stay the night on land at Clifton, you\r\ncannot do better than put up at the \xe2\x80\x9cBarley Mow.\xe2\x80\x9d It is, without\r\nexception, I should say, the quaintest, most old-world inn up the\r\nriver. It stands on the right of the bridge, quite away from the\r\nvillage. Its low-pitched gables and thatched roof and latticed windows\r\ngive it quite a story-book appearance, while inside it is even still\r\nmore once-upon-a-timeyfied.\r\n\r\nIt would not be a good place for the heroine of a modern novel to stay\r\nat. The heroine of a modern novel is always \xe2\x80\x9cdivinely tall,\xe2\x80\x9d and she is\r\never \xe2\x80\x9cdrawing herself up to her full height.\xe2\x80\x9d At the \xe2\x80\x9cBarley Mow\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nwould bump her head against the ceiling each time she did this.\r\n\r\nIt would also be a bad house for a drunken man to put up at. There are\r\ntoo many surprises in the way of unexpected steps down into this room\r\nand up into that; and as for getting upstairs to his bedroom, or ever\r\nfinding his bed when he got up, either operation would be an utter\r\nimpossibility to him.\r\n\r\nWe were up early the next morning, as we wanted to be in Oxford by the\r\nafternoon. It is surprising how early one _can_ get up, when camping\r\nout. One does not yearn for \xe2\x80\x9cjust another five minutes\xe2\x80\x9d nearly so much,\r\nlying wrapped up in a rug on the boards of a boat, with a Gladstone bag\r\nfor a pillow, as one does in a featherbed. We had finished breakfast,\r\nand were through Clifton Lock by half-past eight.\r\n\r\nFrom Clifton to Culham the river banks are flat, monotonous, and\r\nuninteresting, but, after you get through Culhalm Lock\xe2\x80\x94the coldest and\r\ndeepest lock on the river\xe2\x80\x94the landscape improves.\r\n\r\nAt Abingdon, the river passes by the streets. Abingdon is a typical\r\ncountry town of the smaller order\xe2\x80\x94quiet, eminently respectable, clean,\r\nand desperately dull. It prides itself on being old, but whether it\r\ncan compare in this respect with Wallingford and Dorchester seems\r\ndoubtful. A famous abbey stood here once, and within what is left of\r\nits sanctified walls they brew bitter ale nowadays.\r\n\r\nIn St. Nicholas Church, at Abingdon, there is a monument to John\r\nBlackwall and his wife Jane, who both, after leading a happy married\r\nlife, died on the very same day, August 21, 1625; and in St. Helen\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nChurch, it is recorded that W. Lee, who died in 1637, \xe2\x80\x9chad in his\r\nlifetime issue from his loins two hundred lacking but three.\xe2\x80\x9d If you\r\nwork this out you will find that Mr. W. Lee\xe2\x80\x99s family numbered one\r\nhundred and ninety-seven. Mr. W. Lee\xe2\x80\x94five times Mayor of Abingdon\xe2\x80\x94was,\r\nno doubt, a benefactor to his generation, but I hope there are not many\r\nof his kind about in this overcrowded nineteenth century.\r\n\r\nFrom Abingdon to Nuneham Courteney is a lovely stretch. Nuneham Park\r\nis well worth a visit. It can be viewed on Tuesdays and Thursdays. The\r\nhouse contains a fine collection of pictures and curiosities, and the\r\ngrounds are very beautiful.\r\n\r\nThe pool under Sandford lasher, just behind the lock, is a very good\r\nplace to drown yourself in. The undercurrent is terribly strong, and if\r\nyou once get down into it you are all right. An obelisk marks the spot\r\nwhere two men have already been drowned, while bathing there; and the\r\nsteps of the obelisk are generally used as a diving-board by young men\r\nnow who wish to see if the place really _is_ dangerous.\r\n\r\n                          [Picture: River scene]\r\n\r\nIffley Lock and Mill, a mile before you reach Oxford, is a favourite\r\nsubject with the river-loving brethren of the brush. The real article,\r\nhowever, is rather disappointing, after the pictures. Few things, I\r\nhave noticed, come quite up to the pictures of them, in this world.\r\n\r\nWe passed through Iffley Lock at about half-past twelve, and then,\r\nhaving tidied up the boat and made all ready for landing, we set to\r\nwork on our last mile.\r\n\r\nBetween Iffley and Oxford is the most difficult bit of the river I\r\nknow. You want to be born on that bit of water, to understand it. I\r\nhave been over it a fairish number of times, but I have never been able\r\nto get the hang of it. The man who could row a straight course from\r\nOxford to Iffley ought to be able to live comfortably, under one roof,\r\nwith his wife, his mother-in-law, his elder sister, and the old servant\r\nwho was in the family when he was a baby.\r\n\r\nFirst the current drives you on to the right bank, and then on to the\r\nleft, then it takes you out into the middle, turns you round three\r\ntimes, and carries you up stream again, and always ends by trying to\r\nsmash you up against a college barge.\r\n\r\nOf course, as a consequence of this, we got in the way of a good many\r\nother boats, during the mile, and they in ours, and, of course, as a\r\nconsequence of that, a good deal of bad language occurred.\r\n\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know why it should be, but everybody is always so exceptionally\r\nirritable on the river. Little mishaps, that you would hardly notice on\r\ndry land, drive you nearly frantic with rage, when they occur on the\r\nwater. When Harris or George makes an ass of himself on dry land, I\r\nsmile indulgently; when they behave in a chuckle-head way on the river,\r\nI use the most blood-curdling language to them. When another boat gets\r\nin my way, I feel I want to take an oar and kill all the people in it.\r\n\r\nThe mildest tempered people, when on land, become violent and\r\nblood-thirsty when in a boat. I did a little boating once with a young\r\nlady. She was naturally of the sweetest and gentlest disposition\r\nimaginable, but on the river it was quite awful to hear her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, drat the man!\xe2\x80\x9d she would exclaim, when some unfortunate sculler\r\nwould get in her way; \xe2\x80\x9cwhy don\xe2\x80\x99t he look where he\xe2\x80\x99s going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, bother the silly old thing!\xe2\x80\x9d she would say indignantly, when\r\nthe sail would not go up properly. And she would catch hold of it, and\r\nshake it quite brutally.\r\n\r\nYet, as I have said, when on shore she was kind-hearted and amiable\r\nenough.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Man at the lock] [Picture: Man at the lock] The air of the\r\nriver has a demoralising effect upon one\xe2\x80\x99s temper, and this it is,\r\nI suppose, which causes even barge men to be sometimes rude to one\r\nanother, and to use language which, no doubt, in their calmer moments\r\nthey regret.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIX.\r\n\r\n\r\nOxford.\xe2\x80\x94Montmorency\xe2\x80\x99s idea of Heaven.\xe2\x80\x94The hired up-river boat,\r\nits beauties and advantages.\xe2\x80\x94The \xe2\x80\x9cPride of the Thames.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94The\r\nweather changes.\xe2\x80\x94The river under different aspects.\xe2\x80\x94Not a cheerful\r\nevening.\xe2\x80\x94Yearnings for the unattainable.\xe2\x80\x94The cheery chat goes\r\nround.\xe2\x80\x94George performs upon the banjo.\xe2\x80\x94A mournful melody.\xe2\x80\x94Another wet\r\nday.\xe2\x80\x94Flight.\xe2\x80\x94A little supper and a toast.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Dog running] We spent two very pleasant days at Oxford. There\r\nare plenty of dogs in the town of Oxford. Montmorency had eleven fights\r\non the first day, and fourteen on the second, and evidently thought he\r\nhad got to heaven.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Dogs fighting] Among folk too constitutionally weak, or too\r\nconstitutionally lazy, whichever it may be, to relish up-stream work,\r\nit is a common practice to get a boat at Oxford, and row down. For the\r\nenergetic, however, the up-stream journey is certainly to be preferred.\r\nIt does not seem good to be always going with the current. There is\r\nmore satisfaction in squaring one\xe2\x80\x99s back, and fighting against it, and\r\nwinning one\xe2\x80\x99s way forward in spite of it\xe2\x80\x94at least, so I feel, when\r\nHarris and George are sculling and I am steering.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Dog running] To those who do contemplate making Oxford their\r\nstarting-place, I would say, take your own boat\xe2\x80\x94unless, of course, you\r\ncan take someone else\xe2\x80\x99s without any possible danger of being found\r\nout. The boats that, as a rule, are let for hire on the Thames above\r\nMarlow, are very good boats. They are fairly water-tight; and so long\r\nas they are handled with care, they rarely come to pieces, or sink.\r\nThere are places in them to sit down on, and they are complete with all\r\nthe necessary arrangements\xe2\x80\x94or nearly all\xe2\x80\x94to enable you to row them and\r\nsteer them.\r\n\r\nBut they are not ornamental. The boat you hire up the river above\r\nMarlow is not the sort of boat in which you can flash about and give\r\nyourself airs. The hired up-river boat very soon puts a stop to any\r\nnonsense of that sort on the part of its occupants. That is its\r\nchief\xe2\x80\x94one may say, its only recommendation.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Dog] The man in the hired up-river boat is modest and\r\nretiring. He likes to keep on the shady side, underneath the trees, and\r\nto do most of his travelling early in the morning or late at night,\r\nwhen there are not many people about on the river to look at him.\r\n\r\nWhen the man in the hired up-river boat sees anyone he knows, he gets\r\nout on to the bank, and hides behind a tree.\r\n\r\nI was one of a party who hired an up-river boat one summer, for a few\r\ndays\xe2\x80\x99 trip. We had none of us ever seen the hired up-river boat before;\r\nand we did not know what it was when we did see it.\r\n\r\nWe had written for a boat\xe2\x80\x94a double sculling skiff; and when we went\r\ndown with our bags to the yard, and gave our names, the man said:\r\n\r\n[Picture: The Pride of the Thames] \xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes; you\xe2\x80\x99re the party that\r\nwrote for a double sculling skiff. It\xe2\x80\x99s all right. Jim, fetch round\r\n_The Pride of the Thames_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boy went, and re-appeared five minutes afterwards, struggling\r\nwith an antediluvian chunk of wood, that looked as though it had been\r\nrecently dug out of somewhere, and dug out carelessly, so as to have\r\nbeen unnecessarily damaged in the process.\r\n\r\nMy own idea, on first catching sight of the object, was that it was a\r\nRoman relic of some sort,\xe2\x80\x94relic of _what_ I do not know, possibly of a\r\ncoffin.\r\n\r\nThe neighbourhood of the upper Thames is rich in Roman relics, and my\r\nsurmise seemed to me a very probable one; but our serious young man,\r\nwho is a bit of a geologist, pooh-poohed my Roman relic theory, and\r\nsaid it was clear to the meanest intellect (in which category he seemed\r\nto be grieved that he could not conscientiously include mine) that\r\nthe thing the boy had found was the fossil of a whale; and he pointed\r\nout to us various evidences proving that it must have belonged to the\r\npreglacial period.\r\n\r\nTo settle the dispute, we appealed to the boy. We told him not to\r\nbe afraid, but to speak the plain truth: Was it the fossil of a\r\npre-Adamite whale, or was it an early Roman coffin?\r\n\r\nThe boy said it was _The Pride of the Thames_.\r\n\r\nWe thought this a very humorous answer on the part of the boy at first,\r\nand somebody gave him twopence as a reward for his ready wit; but when\r\nhe persisted in keeping up the joke, as we thought, too long, we got\r\nvexed with him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, come, my lad!\xe2\x80\x9d said our captain sharply, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t let us have any\r\nnonsense. You take your mother\xe2\x80\x99s washing-tub home again, and bring us a\r\nboat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boat-builder himself came up then, and assured us, on his word, as\r\na practical man, that the thing really was a boat\xe2\x80\x94was, in fact, _the_\r\nboat, the \xe2\x80\x9cdouble sculling skiff\xe2\x80\x9d selected to take us on our trip down\r\nthe river.\r\n\r\nWe grumbled a good deal. We thought he might, at least, have had it\r\nwhitewashed or tarred\xe2\x80\x94had _something_ done to it to distinguish it from\r\na bit of a wreck; but he could not see any fault in it.\r\n\r\nHe even seemed offended at our remarks. He said he had picked us out\r\nthe best boat in all his stock, and he thought we might have been more\r\ngrateful.\r\n\r\nHe said it, _The Pride of the Thames_, had been in use, just as it now\r\nstood (or rather as it now hung together), for the last forty years, to\r\n_his_ knowledge, and nobody had complained of it before, and he did not\r\nsee why we should be the first to begin.\r\n\r\nWe argued no more.\r\n\r\nWe fastened the so-called boat together with some pieces of string,\r\ngot a bit of wall-paper and pasted over the shabbier places, said our\r\nprayers, and stepped on board.\r\n\r\nThey charged us thirty-five shillings for the loan of the remnant\r\nfor six days; and we could have bought the thing out-and-out for\r\nfour-and-sixpence at any sale of drift-wood round the coast.\r\n\r\nThe weather changed on the third day,\xe2\x80\x94Oh! I am talking about our\r\npresent trip now,\xe2\x80\x94and we started from Oxford upon our homeward journey\r\nin the midst of a steady drizzle.\r\n\r\nThe river\xe2\x80\x94with the sunlight flashing from its dancing wavelets, gilding\r\ngold the grey-green beech-trunks, glinting through the dark, cool wood\r\npaths, chasing shadows o\xe2\x80\x99er the shallows, flinging diamonds from the\r\nmill-wheels, throwing kisses to the lilies, wantoning with the weirs\xe2\x80\x99\r\nwhite waters, silvering moss-grown walls and bridges, brightening every\r\ntiny townlet, making sweet each lane and meadow, lying tangled in the\r\nrushes, peeping, laughing, from each inlet, gleaming gay on many a far\r\nsail, making soft the air with glory\xe2\x80\x94is a golden fairy stream.\r\n\r\nBut the river\xe2\x80\x94chill and weary, with the ceaseless rain-drops falling on\r\nits brown and sluggish waters, with a sound as of a woman, weeping low\r\nin some dark chamber; while the woods, all dark and silent, shrouded in\r\ntheir mists of vapour, stand like ghosts upon the margin; silent ghosts\r\nwith eyes reproachful, like the ghosts of evil actions, like the ghosts\r\nof friends neglected\xe2\x80\x94is a spirit-haunted water through the land of vain\r\nregrets.\r\n\r\nSunlight is the life-blood of Nature. Mother Earth looks at us with\r\nsuch dull, soulless eyes, when the sunlight has died away from out of\r\nher. It makes us sad to be with her then; she does not seem to know\r\nus or to care for us. She is as a widow who has lost the husband she\r\nloved, and her children touch her hand, and look up into her eyes, but\r\ngain no smile from her.\r\n\r\nWe rowed on all that day through the rain, and very melancholy work\r\nit was. We pretended, at first, that we enjoyed it. We said it was a\r\nchange, and that we liked to see the river under all its different\r\naspects. We said we could not expect to have it all sunshine, nor\r\nshould we wish it. We told each other that Nature was beautiful, even\r\nin her tears.\r\n\r\n                     [Picture: The boat in the rain]\r\n\r\nIndeed, Harris and I were quite enthusiastic about the business, for\r\nthe first few hours. And we sang a song about a gipsy\xe2\x80\x99s life, and how\r\ndelightful a gipsy\xe2\x80\x99s existence was!\xe2\x80\x94free to storm and sunshine, and to\r\nevery wind that blew!\xe2\x80\x94and how he enjoyed the rain, and what a lot of\r\ngood it did him; and how he laughed at people who didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it.\r\n\r\nGeorge took the fun more soberly, and stuck to the umbrella.\r\n\r\nWe hoisted the cover before we had lunch, and kept it up all the\r\nafternoon, just leaving a little space in the bow, from which one of us\r\ncould paddle and keep a look-out. In this way we made nine miles, and\r\npulled up for the night a little below Day\xe2\x80\x99s Lock.\r\n\r\nI cannot honestly say that we had a merry evening. The rain poured\r\ndown with quiet persistency. Everything in the boat was damp and\r\nclammy. Supper was not a success. Cold veal pie, when you don\xe2\x80\x99t feel\r\nhungry, is apt to cloy. I felt I wanted whitebait and a cutlet; Harris\r\nbabbled of soles and white-sauce, and passed the remains of his pie to\r\nMontmorency, who declined it, and, apparently insulted by the offer,\r\nwent and sat over at the other end of the boat by himself.\r\n\r\nGeorge requested that we would not talk about these things, at all\r\nevents until he had finished his cold boiled beef without mustard.\r\n\r\nWe played penny nap after supper. We played for about an hour and a\r\nhalf, by the end of which time George had won fourpence\xe2\x80\x94George always\r\nis lucky at cards\xe2\x80\x94and Harris and I had lost exactly twopence each.\r\n\r\nWe thought we would give up gambling then. As Harris said, it breeds an\r\nunhealthy excitement when carried too far.
