George offered to go on and\r\ngive us our revenge; but Harris and I decided not to battle any further\r\nagainst Fate.\r\n\r\nAfter that, we mixed ourselves some toddy, and sat round and talked.\r\nGeorge told us about a man he had known, who had come up the river\r\ntwo years ago and who had slept out in a damp boat on just such\r\nanother night as that was, and it had given him rheumatic fever, and\r\nnothing was able to save him, and he had died in great agony ten days\r\nafterwards. George said he was quite a young man, and was engaged to be\r\nmarried. He said it was one of the saddest things he had ever known.\r\n\r\nAnd that put Harris in mind of a friend of his, who had been in the\r\nVolunteers, and who had slept out under canvas one wet night down at\r\nAldershot, \xe2\x80\x9con just such another night as this,\xe2\x80\x9d said Harris; and he\r\nhad woke up in the morning a cripple for life. Harris said he would\r\nintroduce us both to the man when we got back to town; it would make\r\nour hearts bleed to see him.\r\n\r\nThis naturally led to some pleasant chat about sciatica, fevers,\r\nchills, lung diseases, and bronchitis; and Harris said how very awkward\r\nit would be if one of us were taken seriously ill in the night, seeing\r\nhow far away we were from a doctor.\r\n\r\nThere seemed to be a desire for something frolicksome to follow upon\r\nthis conversation, and in a weak moment I suggested that George should\r\nget out his banjo, and see if he could not give us a comic song.\r\n\r\nI will say for George that he did not want any pressing. There was no\r\nnonsense about having left his music at home, or anything of that sort.\r\nHe at once fished out his instrument, and commenced to play \xe2\x80\x9cTwo Lovely\r\nBlack Eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had always regarded \xe2\x80\x9cTwo Lovely Black Eyes\xe2\x80\x9d as rather a commonplace\r\ntune until that evening. The rich vein of sadness that George extracted\r\nfrom it quite surprised me.\r\n\r\nThe desire that grew upon Harris and myself, as the mournful strains\r\nprogressed, was to fall upon each other\xe2\x80\x99s necks and weep; but by great\r\neffort we kept back the rising tears, and listened to the wild yearnful\r\nmelody in silence.\r\n\r\nWhen the chorus came we even made a desperate effort to be merry. We\r\nre-filled our glasses and joined in; Harris, in a voice trembling with\r\nemotion, leading, and George and I following a few words behind:\r\n\r\n          \xe2\x80\x9cTwo lovely black eyes;\r\n          Oh! what a surprise!\r\n    Only for telling a man he was wrong,\r\n          Two\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere we broke down. The unutterable pathos of George\xe2\x80\x99s accompaniment\r\nto that \xe2\x80\x9ctwo\xe2\x80\x9d we were, in our then state of depression, unable to bear.\r\nHarris sobbed like a little child, and the dog howled till I thought\r\nhis heart or his jaw must surely break.\r\n\r\nGeorge wanted to go on with another verse. He thought that when he had\r\ngot a little more into the tune, and could throw more \xe2\x80\x9cabandon,\xe2\x80\x9d as it\r\nwere, into the rendering, it might not seem so sad. The feeling of the\r\nmajority, however, was opposed to the experiment.\r\n\r\nThere being nothing else to do, we went to bed\xe2\x80\x94that is, we undressed\r\nourselves, and tossed about at the bottom of the boat for some three or\r\nfour hours. After which, we managed to get some fitful slumber until\r\nfive a.m., when we all got up and had breakfast.\r\n\r\nThe second day was exactly like the first. The rain continued to pour\r\ndown, and we sat, wrapped up in our mackintoshes, underneath the canvas,\r\nand drifted slowly down.\r\n\r\nOne of us\xe2\x80\x94I forget which one now, but I rather think it was\r\nmyself\xe2\x80\x94made a few feeble attempts during the course of the morning to\r\nwork up the old gipsy foolishness about being children of Nature and\r\nenjoying the wet; but it did not go down well at all. That\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cI care not for the rain, not I!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nwas so painfully evident, as expressing the sentiments of each of us,\r\nthat to sing it seemed unnecessary.\r\n\r\nOn one point we were all agreed, and that was that, come what might,\r\nwe would go through with this job to the bitter end. We had come out\r\nfor a fortnight\xe2\x80\x99s enjoyment on the river, and a fortnight\xe2\x80\x99s enjoyment\r\non the river we meant to have. If it killed us! well, that would be a\r\nsad thing for our friends and relations, but it could not be helped. We\r\nfelt that to give in to the weather in a climate such as ours would be\r\na most disastrous precedent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s only two days more,\xe2\x80\x9d said Harris, \xe2\x80\x9cand we are young and strong.\r\nWe may get over it all right, after all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt about four o\xe2\x80\x99clock we began to discuss our arrangements for the\r\nevening. We were a little past Goring then, and we decided to paddle on\r\nto Pangbourne, and put up there for the night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnother jolly evening!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured George.\r\n\r\nWe sat and mused on the prospect. We should be in at Pangbourne by\r\nfive. We should finish dinner at, say, half-past six. After that we\r\ncould walk about the village in the pouring rain until bed-time; or we\r\ncould sit in a dimly-lit bar-parlour and read the almanac.\r\n\r\n[Picture: Lady in skirt] \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, the Alhambra would be almost more\r\nlively,\xe2\x80\x9d said Harris, venturing his head outside the cover for a moment\r\nand taking a survey of the sky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith a little supper at the --- {311} to follow,\xe2\x80\x9d I added, half\r\nunconsciously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes it\xe2\x80\x99s almost a pity we\xe2\x80\x99ve made up our minds to stick to this boat,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nanswered Harris; and then there was silence for a while.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf we _hadn\xe2\x80\x99t_ made up our minds to contract our certain deaths in\r\nthis bally old coffin,\xe2\x80\x9d observed George, casting a glance of intense\r\nmalevolence over the boat, \xe2\x80\x9cit might be worth while to mention that\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s a train leaves Pangbourne, I know, soon after five, which would\r\njust land us in town in comfortable time to get a chop, and then go on\r\nto the place you mentioned afterwards.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNobody spoke. We looked at one another, and each one seemed to see his\r\nown mean and guilty thoughts reflected in the faces of the others. In\r\nsilence, we dragged out and overhauled the Gladstone. We looked up the\r\nriver and down the river; not a soul was in sight!\r\n\r\nTwenty minutes later, three figures, followed by a shamed-looking dog,\r\nmight have been seen creeping stealthily from the boat-house at the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSwan\xe2\x80\x9d towards the railway station, dressed in the following neither\r\nneat nor gaudy costume:\r\n\r\nBlack leather shoes, dirty; suit of boating flannels, very dirty; brown\r\nfelt hat, much battered; mackintosh, very wet; umbrella.\r\n\r\nWe had deceived the boatman at Pangbourne. We had not had the face to\r\ntell him that we were running away from the rain. We had left the boat,\r\nand all it contained, in his charge, with instructions that it was to\r\nbe ready for us at nine the next morning. If, we said\xe2\x80\x94_if_ anything\r\nunforeseen should happen, preventing our return, we would write to him.\r\n\r\nWe reached Paddington at seven, and drove direct to the restaurant\r\nI have before described, where we partook of a light meal, left\r\nMontmorency, together with suggestions for a supper to be ready at\r\nhalf-past ten, and then continued our way to Leicester Square.\r\n\r\nWe attracted a good deal of attention at the Alhambra. On our\r\npresenting ourselves at the paybox we were gruffly directed to go round\r\nto Castle Street, and were informed that we were half-an-hour behind\r\nour time.\r\n\r\nWe convinced the man, with some difficulty, that we were _not_ \xe2\x80\x9cthe\r\nworld-renowned contortionists from the Himalaya Mountains,\xe2\x80\x9d and he took\r\nour money and let us pass.\r\n\r\nInside we were a still greater success. Our fine bronzed countenances\r\nand picturesque clothes were followed round the place with admiring\r\ngaze. We were the cynosure of every eye.\r\n\r\nIt was a proud moment for us all.\r\n\r\nWe adjourned soon after the first ballet, and wended our way back to\r\nthe restaurant, where supper was already awaiting us.\r\n\r\nI must confess to enjoying that supper. For about ten days we seemed\r\nto have been living, more or less, on nothing but cold meat, cake, and\r\nbread and jam. It had been a simple, a nutritious diet; but there had\r\nbeen nothing exciting about it, and the odour of Burgundy, and the\r\nsmell of French sauces, and the sight of clean napkins and long loaves,\r\nknocked as a very welcome visitor at the door of our inner man.\r\n\r\nWe pegged and quaffed away in silence for a while, until the time\r\ncame when, instead of sitting bolt upright, and grasping the knife\r\nand fork firmly, we leant back in our chairs and worked slowly and\r\ncarelessly\xe2\x80\x94when we stretched out our legs beneath the table, let our\r\nnapkins fall, unheeded, to the floor, and found time to more critically\r\nexamine the smoky ceiling than we had hitherto been able to do\xe2\x80\x94when we\r\nrested our glasses at arm\xe2\x80\x99s-length upon the table, and felt good, and\r\nthoughtful, and forgiving.\r\n\r\nThen Harris, who was sitting next the window, drew aside the curtain\r\nand looked out upon the street.\r\n\r\nIt glistened darkly in the wet, the dim lamps flickered with each gust,\r\nthe rain splashed steadily into the puddles and trickled down the\r\nwater-spouts into the running gutters. A few soaked wayfarers hurried\r\npast, crouching beneath their dripping umbrellas, the women holding up\r\ntheir skirts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Harris, reaching his hand out for his glass, \xe2\x80\x9cwe have had\r\na pleasant trip, and my hearty thanks for it to old Father Thames\xe2\x80\x94but I\r\nthink we did well to chuck it when we did. Here\xe2\x80\x99s to Three Men well out\r\nof a Boat!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Montmorency, standing on his hind legs, before the window, peering\r\nout into the night, gave a short bark of decided concurrence with the\r\ntoast.\r\n\r\n                   [Picture: Neptune drinking a toast]\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nFootnotes.\r\n\r\n\r\n{287} Or rather _were_. The Conservancy of late seems to have\r\nconstituted itself into a society for the employment of idiots. A good\r\nmany of the new lock-keepers, especially in the more crowded portions\r\nof the river, are excitable, nervous old men, quite unfitted for their\r\npost.\r\n\r\n{311} A capital little out-of-the-way restaurant, in the neighbourhood\r\nof ---, where you can get one of the best-cooked and cheapest little\r\nFrench dinners or suppers that I know of, with an excellent bottle of\r\nBeaune, for three-and-six; and which I am not going to be idiot enough\r\nto advertise.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n***END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THREE MEN IN A BOAT***\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\r\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
